“the WONDERFUL VISIT.”
We talk glibly about “entertaining angels un-
awares ; ” but how would we entertain them ?
Suppose you were buttonholed at your suburban
gate by a Thing which called itself an angel,
which at any rate so far agreed with conventional
angelic improprieties that it had real wings and
very little drapery, what exactly would you do ?
Would you ask It in to tea and offer it chops?
What would your wife say to It ? What would
the neighbors think? How would It comport
Itself ?
That is what H. G. Wells (the clever author of
“ The Time Machine ”) sets himself to answer in
his new book, “The Wonderful Visit ” (6s. 6d.).
Mr. Wells is a young man of 28, and a very young
writer of 3. He was a tutor till lung-collapse
drove him into literature, to literature’s great
gain. For Mr. Wells has, of all the writers of the
day, the happiest, freshest imagination, and his
scientific training is enabling him to plough quite
untilled acres in the field of fiction. Two other of
his works —“ The Island of Dr. Moreau,” a vivi-
sectionist romance, and “The Stolen Bacillus,” a
volume of short stories —are nearly ready for pub-
lication.
The Angel drops inexplicably into a little
English village. There have been rumors of a
strange bird, and the ornithological vicar seizes
his gun, sallies out, and—puts a charge of swan-
shot into the Angel’s wing!
There was a scream of superhuman agony, the wings
beat the air twice, and the victim came slanting swiftly
downward and struck the ground—a struggling heap of
writhing body, broken wing, and flying blood-stained
plumes—upon the turfy slope behind.
The Vicar stood aghast, with his smoking gun in his
hand. It was no bird at all, but a youth with an ex-
tremely beautiful face, clad in a robe of saffron and with
iridescent wings, across whose pinions great waves of
colour, flushes of purple and crimson, golden green and
intense blue, pursued one another as he writhed in his
agony. Never had the Vicar seen such gorgeous floods of
colour. Not stained-glass windows, not the wings of
butterflies, not even the glories of crystals seen between
prisms, no colours on earth could compare with them.
Twice the Angel raised himself, only to fall over sideways
again. Then the beating of the wings diminished, the
terrified face grew pale, the floods of colour abated, and
suddenly with a sob he lay prone, and the changing hues
of the broken wings faded swiftly into one uniform dull
grey hue.
“Oh ! what has happened to me?” cried the Angel
(for such it was), shuddering violently, hands outstretched
and clutching the ground, and then lying still.
“ Dear me ! ” said the Vicar. “ I had no idea.” He
came forward cautiously. “ Excuse me,” he said, “I am
afraid I have shot you.”
It was the obvious remark.
The wound is bound up, and the Vicar takes the
Angel home. The curate’s wife and daughters are
playing tennis with a friend on the vicarage lawn.
You see, it came on the Curate’s wife suddenly. The
Angel’s face being towards her she saw nothing of the
wings. Only a face of unearthly beauty in a halo of
chestnut hair, and a graceful figure clothed in a saffron
garment that barely reached the knees. The thought of
those knees flashed upon the Vicar at once. He, too, was
horror-struck. So were the two girls and Mrs. Jehoram.
All horror-struck. The Angel stared in astonishment at
the horror-struck group. You see, he had never seen
anyone horror-struck before.
“ Mis-ter Hilly er! ” said the Curate’s wife. “This is
too much!” She stood speechless for a moment. "Oh!"
■ She swept round upon the rigid girls. “ Come ! ” The
Vicar opened and shut his voiceless mouth. The world
hummed and spun about him. There was a whirling of
zephyr skirts, four impassioned faces sweeping towards
the open door of the passage that ran through the
vicarage. He felt that his position went with them.
“Mrs. Mendham,” said the Vicar, stepping- forward,
“Mrs. Mendham, you don’t understand ”
“ Oh /” they all said again.
One, two, three, four skirts vanished in the doorway.
The Vicar staggered half-way across the lawn and
stopped, aghast. “ This comes,” he heard the Curate’s
wife say, out of the depths of the passage, “ of having an
unmanned Vicar -—.” The front door of the vicarage
slammed like a minute-gun. There was silence for a space.
“I might have thought,” he said. “She is always so
hasty.”
The slight misconception as to the Angel’s sex
is set right, and the Vicar’s character cleared to
some extent, but the curate, and the doctor, and
the village positively refuse to accept the Angel,
in spite of his wings. He is for them a lunatic or
an impostor. They dress him in the Vicar’s old
clothes, and day by day the mean and sordid life
of earth depraves his angelic nature. Only the
quaint old Vicar realises how beautiful is the soul-
life of which his awkward visitor gives him
glimpses. From the angelic point of view the
author launches shafts of vigorous satire. The
Angel meets a philosophical Tramp.
“See that chap ploughin’? ” said the Tramp. “Ain’t
it ridiculous ?—centuries and centuries of civilisation, and
look at that poor swine there ; sweatin’ ’isself empty and
trudgin’ up that ’ill-side. ’E’s English, ’e ’is. ’E belongs
to the top race in creation, ’e does. ’E’s one of the rulers
of Indjer. It’s enough io make a nigger laugh. The flag
that’s brayed a thousand years the battle and the breeze
—that’s ’is flag. There never was a country was as great
and glorious as this. Never. And that’s wot it makes of
us.
Day by day for a week the Angel grows sadder,
and wearier, and more out of tune with the ugly
life of earth. And then—and then the end comes.
Let the author tell it. ■
Mr. Wells has made in “ The Wonderful Visit ”
a singularly beautiful and original book. The
thought is strong; the play of wit and fancy
brilliant; the style not elaborate, but graceful and
natural. Nothing better in its way has ever been
accomplished.
Recent Publications.
J. A. Andrews, the mild-mannered anarchist
who was honored in Sydney by Govt, persecution,
is issuing some unsold copies of his “Temple
Mystic and Other Poems ” at the reduced price of
Is. 3d. posted. The verses (which were dedicated
to Victor Daley so far back as 1888) have not
much poetical value, but a good deal of personal
interest—dating, as they do, from a period before
anarchy possessed the. author’s soul.
Scribner’s Magazine for Nov. (Is. 2d.) is a
particularly good number. There is a splendid
poem by H. C. Bunner, editor of Puck, which has
the lilt of Whitman and Whitman’s warm soul.
Some exceptionally fine engravings illustrate the
American festiyal of Thanksgiving Day.
“The Science and Art of Training”(ls. 3d.), by
Henry Hoole, M.D., is one of the very best books
on the subject, and can be warmly recommended
to all classes of athletes. Tlie author (a first-class
authority) recommends in many cases the inges-
tion of a moderate amount of alcohol, which (he
says) “gives tone to the muscles and nerves, and
stimulates both digestion and assimilation, fre-
quently to a wonderful degree.”
Melville, Mullen, and Slade issue “The Mel-
bourne Guide-Book” (Is. 3d.). No better could
be wished for.
Lewis Findlay East is author of “ The Quest of
Love ” and other verses of little strength or
originality (with the exception of “ The Charge of
the Scots Greys ”). Melville, Mullen, and Slade
publish the volume, the printing and binding of
which deserve a special word of praise. It is, by
the way, extremely difficult to get books well
bound in Sydney; but Melb. craftsmen leave no-
thing to be desired.
“ The Boating Man’s Vade-Mecum ” (Is. fid.) is
exceedingly good value, telling everything that is
to be told about the pleasant pastime of yachting - .
NOTES.
The publishers of “The Banjo’s” verses are
sending the stereotype-plates to England for use
in the preparation of a special English edition.
For this and future reprints the following typo-
graphical errors in the first and second Australian
editions are worth noting Pages xv. and 136,
“ aringing ” should be “ a-ringing.” “ Were ”is
printed for “where,” p. xv.; “wont” for “ won’t,”
p. 33; “breach ” for “breech,” p. 131; “ Gentle-
men” for “Gentleman,” p. 180. There is also a
turned “s,” p. 62, 1. 18; and the “Prelude” is
described as “Introductory Verse” on the first
contents page. “The Banjo,” in "his way, is an
Australian classic, and one likes- to have his typo-
graphy brought to classical perfection.
Angus and Robertson’s forthcoming volumes
include Henry Lawson’s poems ; the same writer’s
prose sketches; “The Banjo’s” stories ; a novel by
Lawson, “ The Hero of Redd ay ; ” and poems by
Victor Daley and John Farrell. Lawson’s poems
(ss. 6d.) and “ The, Banjo’s ” stories (price not yet
fixed) are in the press, and subscribers will’be
supplied from this office immediately on issue.
Dent’s handsome series of translations of
Balzac’s novels (edited by Saintsbury) is coming
to hand in monthly volumes (4s. 6d. each). The
volumes already issued are “The Wild Ass’s
Skin ” (one wonders how Mr. Saintsbury came to
prefer this title to “The Piece of Shagreen”),
The Chouans,” “At the Sign of the Cat and
Racket,” and “The Country Doctor.” The
edition is one for which English readers may well
be grateful.
Good English critics are not uncomplimentary
to Francis Kenna. Says the Saturday Review :
“ There is a note of sincerity in those among the
‘Songs of the. Season” which are personal; the
poet has felt something at any rate, the melan-
choly is real; but he does not succeed when he
leaves himself and becomes universal, as in the
villanelle which sets forth what the world needs.”
And The Bookman thinks that “many of the
verses are pleasing in a melancholy fashion, and
all are above the average of minor verse in evi-
dence of culture.”
“Rolf Boldrewood ” has finished a new story,
“The Sealskin Cloak,” which is to be published
serially in one of the English magazines.
Mary Gaunt (Mrs. Miller), daughter of Vic.
County Court Judge Gaunt ; gets a flattering
notice and unflattering portrait in Oct. Bookman.
Her books, “Dave’s Sweetheart,” and “The
Moving Finger,’’are readable.
Gordon and Gotch have opened a branch at
Perth, Westralia, where The Bulletin may be
obtained immediately on arrival of the mail.
The Bulletin Christmas Number will contain
sketches by “Hop,” Percy Spence, Frank
Mahony, B. E. Minns, and other well-known
Bulletin artists. The literary contributors in-
clude, in prose, Louise Mack, Ethel Turner, Alex.
Montgomery, Henry Lawson, Louis Becke, J.
Evison, Ernest Fayenc, and others well-known in
literature; and specially good verse comes from
Arthur Adams, Victor Daley, “Glenrowan,” and
others. Altogether, the number promises to be
an exceedingly good one. By post, 7d.
Traveller: “Charles L. Webster and Co., the
N.Y. publishing house which played ducks and
drakes with Mark Twain’s money, drew probably
the largest single cheque ever paid for literary-
work. This cheque was for $200,000, approxi-
mately <£40,000, drawn to the order of the widow
of Gen. Grant, and was a first instalment only on
account of payment for Grant’s Memoirs, heroi-
cally written by him during his last illness
(cancer), and finished only a few days before
his agonising death. Grant, always taciturn and
reserved, disliked autobiography as much as he
disliked public speaking. The Republic had not
been ungrateful to him, but he had lost his for-
tune through the extraordinary stupidity of his
son and the rascality of that son’s partner, to
w'hom the elder Grant had furnished the capital
to engage in stock-broking.”
The serial rights alone of Hall Caine’s next book
will be worth nearly £3000 to him.
One of Anthony Hope’s new tales, “ Phrozo,”
will first appear in the Idler. It deals with the
lively adventures of an Englishman and a Greek
princess on a Mediterranean island. The other,
“ The Chronicles of Count Antonio,” is a romance
of mediaeval Italy.
“Q.’s ” new volume of short stories, “ Wander-
ing Heath, ” will soon be available. “ Q. ’s ” recent
work has lacked interest and value; and, indeed,
nothing that he has done comes up to the early
“Noughts and Crosses.”
Stanley Weyman’s “ Red Cockade ” will reach
Australia in volume-form about the middle of
January. It has materially brightened To-Day
during its serial run, and is well up to Mr. Wey-
man’s standard.
The first edition (5000) of Louis Becke’s “His
Native Wife ” is nearly exhausted, and a second
edition may be looked fox’. With the consent of
the publisher, Alex. Lindsay, Mr. Becke has sent
the yarn for English sexual publication—px-obably
in the Pall Mall Gazette.
Nov. Cosmos sold out in a few days—partly,
they say, owing to naughty Louise Mack’s roman
(i clef reproducing the chatter of some well-known
Sydney society ladies overheard on the Neutral
Bay ferry-boat.

To Correspondents.— “ Strange Dream ” :
Don’t think Twain’s remark about Harte was a
bad joke : it was merely bad criticism. In July
North American Review Mark similarly slanged
Fenimore Cooper, and he is charged by D. F. Han-
nigan in Oct. Free Review with “ bad temper and
want of the faculty of appreciativeness. ” The com;,
merit is just enough. Anybody, like Mark, can
see the holes in a ladder, but a critic ought to see
the ladder. Mark seems to fear lest other American
reputations may detract from his own V. :
Apropos of what? A.W. : We’ll post you “His
Natural Life ” for 3s., and you can look it up for
yourself...... Scissors : Don’t agree ; S.M. Herald
literary criticisms are generally capable and just.
Anybody can slobber praise ; very few can write
judicious appreciation Jap : Anarchist An-
drews was the writer of ‘ ‘ Invicta Spes a very
decent bit of philosophical verse. (See note re his
published poems On this page).

Q L I N D S.
The word is suggestive. Many dealers keep their
customers very much in the dark as to the true
state of things. So to speak, they get behind their
own blinds because the circumstances won’t allow
them to emerge into the full light.
OUR REPRESENTATIVE IN CHINA
writes as follows :
I should like to mention certain matters in connection
with the blind trade. With every order I place I INSIST
ON INSPECTION before packing, with the result that
not less than 25 to 30 per cent, are rejected. But I find
this s-ason a firm has placed large oiders, but ABSO-
LUTELY NO INSPECTION takes place, consequently
makers are competing for the orders at lower rates on
these terms.
We take our friends behind the scenes so that
they may see WHAT A TRAGEDY the blind
trade is, and so that they may, when buying blinds,
discriminate between honest dealers and the other
sort. No doubt trie blinds WHICH ARE
SHIPPED WITHOUT INSPECTION are
offered at lower prices than SELECTED BLINDS,
but wise people will prefer giving a trifle more,
should it be necessary, with the certainty of get-
ting BLINDS which offer some show of protec-
tion, and that do not tumble in pieces after a
fortnight’s wear.
BAMBOO BLINDS
made of the outside of skin or the bamboo in
its natural color. SPECIALLY SELECTED.
Fitted with CORDS and pulleys ready to put up.
It may be added that the COKDS are strong and
suitable, and not liable to rot and break on the
least provocation. Sizes from 3ft. wide by 6ft.
deep to 12ft. wide by Bft. deep ; prices, Is. to
ss. 6d. each blind.
CHINESE BLINDS
made of fine split bamboo, painted green on both
sides, and fitted with CORDS and pulleys of
best quality. These blinds are SPECIALLY
SELECTED. Sizes from 3ft. wide by 6ft. deep
to 12ft. wide by 10ft. deep ; prices from Is. Bd. to
7s, 6d. each blind.
BEAD AND REED BLINDS
Charming assortment of colors and styles. These
blinds are available for balls, doorways and win-
dows. They say they frighten the flies away. If
so, they are worth a pound an inch. Prices from

| A Gippsland farmer up Mirboo way has a
common white duck which lays perfectly black
eggs. A disease of the oviducts causes this
phenomenon.
The Maori disease ngere-ngere (also known
as puhipuhi and tuwhenna) is now accepted as true
leprosy, and traced to the marooning of a leper-
whaler near Hauraki.
“The way to reform woman’s dress is to
cut 10 inches off the skirt. Think how many
hundreds of tons of mud-bedraggled dry goods
would then drop from the overweighted hips of
womanhood.”— North American Review.
Prof. Baldwin Spencer, of Melb. Univer-
sity, doubts the truth of the common assertion
; that a snake swallows its young when danger
threatens. He wants a sworn affidavit by a
veracious eye-witness, and the body of the mother
with the young inside her. At present, he says,
there is no proof of this trick.
Dear Bulletin, —The platypus certainly
lives a long way north of the Tropic of Capricorn.
Years ago they were plentiful in the Barron, just
above the falls, and I believe they can be found
right along the N.Q. coast range. I have seen
them both in the Herbert and Burdekin and their
tributaries, but mostly above the range. On one
occasion I saw one killed in Gowrie Creek, Lower
Herbert district, where alligators are quite plenti-
ful.—O.K., Ravenswood, Oct. 29, ’95.
The recent heartless decision of Melb. City
Court in the case of the Antiope seamen again
exhibits the Bench as a mere formula-driven
machine which only requires two knives and a fly-
wheel to be a chaff-cutter. It is interesting to
learn, by the way, that the Antiope seaman’s
wages were £2 15s. per month—or about Is.
per day, and the incomparable tucker served out
by British “limejuioers.” For Is. Id. per day, |
or about IJd. an hour, these men risk their lives. I
Can shore-sweating and the martyrdom of woman
much outdo this ? ’ j
H. ; “ This Melb. Evening News is
the acme of downright leaden stupidity. All
the Herald’s rivals have fallen into exactly the
same trap, thinking that a paper can be ‘ written
up,’ while it is nowhere in point of news—which
is what newspaper readers want, particularly at

done.”
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
| Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
| of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
! block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 6Goft.,
and contains the followinar 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-

main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long.*
Miss Balfour, the sister of the Tory-leader
lately made a speech, which had the misfortune to
get into print. Here’s a morsel from the reckless
spinster’s appeal to electioneering-agents: “It
had often been said that the Conservatives were
much less active, both in voting and in canvassing,
than the Radicals. She believed there was a good
deal of truth in that. A person who was already
in a comfortable armchair did not want to get out
of it, but a person who had no armchair was will-
ing to work, especially if he thought he could get
somebody else’s armchair by so doing.” Balfour’s
sister gives a simple definition of a Tory—a
person unwilling to work, because he can take his
ease in the cosy chair which other persons’ labor
provides for him.
Writes a Bulletin correspondent : “ Had
I been told this, instead of having seen it, I
should probably have called the teller a liar.
About half-past one on Thursday, 14th inst., a
carrier’s covered van, with a man and a boy on
the front seat, was being driven rapidly across
the intersection of Bourke and Swanston-streets,
Melbourne. A chestnut horse had bolted with
its rider along Bourke-street, and made straight
for the van. A collision seemed inevitable ; but
to the surprise of about 100 spectators the chest-
nut leaped clear over the hind-quarters of the
carrier’s horse, and arrived on the other side
riderless. Even more surprised were we to see
three people on the seat of the van. The rider
had been knocked off the horse by the van’s pro-
jecting roof on to the van-seat. That might not
occur again in a million tries.”
“ ’TIS ONLY NOBLE TO BE GOOD.”
— Tennyson.
But can we all “ be good ”?—that is the rub.
We look around, and noble him we dub
Who does no sin ; while he who oft-times errs
The “righteous wrath ” of sinless ones incurs.
But is this justice? Do we guilty hold
The penny-piece that is not made of gold ?
Do we chastise with stripes the sheep
That cannot like a racehorse leap?
Then why reproach the man
Who cannot do what others can ?
The strong man must be strong, the weak man
weak,
The haughty haughty, and the meek man meek.
Just as the potter moulds the plastic clay,
So we in shape our architect obey.
Hence, while it may be “ noble to be good,” ’tis
mad
To say ’tis wicked to be bad.
Narandera (N.S. W.)
J.A.
Of all sad news conceived by Jews
The saddest is Melbourne’s Evening News.
But, when the johnnies who form the staff
Have vanished like wind pursued by chaff,
Their weeping friends, of News bereft.
Will find some joy in the rag that’s left;
And say, as they take up the ’Ebald again,
“Ah ! this isn’t so bad as it might have been.”

HighestGrade.Mild Plug Tobacco
YANKEE DOODLE .
“YANKEE DOODLE” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has evei
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it lias been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker twin test the statement, foi
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its’sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this?
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
been accustomed to. DTXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century to accomplish this; and, as
their leading business has been built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford tc
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIXSON & SONS, who use more American leaf
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brands:—“CON-
QUEROR,” “SIR ROGER,” “CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;” but “YANKEE DOODLE'
ranks supreme.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 30, 1895.
A Stupendous Grab.
A small, unostentatious bill turned up the
other day in New South Wales Assembly,
which purports to give to one John Sulman
(a Sydney architect), or his assigns, jpower
to construct and work a submarine railway
from Sydney to North Shore. It comes in
unobtrusively towards the end of the session,
when the Legislature is very tired and is
wont to rush things through with little con-
sideration, and it appeals strongly to the
Labor element of the Assembly on the ground
that it will furnish a certain amount of em-
ployment—not much, certainly, but a little.
The House has had much experience of
such things in the past, the general rule
being that some speculator got a concession
to build some urgent public work, and then
endeavored to sell it to some other specu-
lator, and, failing in that, simply hung on to
it, doing nothing himself and refusing to let
anyone else do anything unless he was paid
handsomely to stand out. To guard against
this and prove that he is absolutely genuine,
Mr. John Sulman agrees to deposit £3OOO
with the Treasury within six months after
the passing of the Act, and to expend at
least T 30,000 on the work within 18 months.
The goods and parcels rates are to be the
same as those on the New South Wales State
railways, which is sufficiently vague, and the
fares are not to exceed Id. for each section,
the length' of a section being undefined.
John Sulman is also to have the option of
constructing a second tunnel if he sees fit.
Altogether, the scheme is a gentlemanly and
polished grab with all the objectionable
features so carefully kept out of sight that
they may pass comparatively unnoticed.
* * *
Yet, despite the polish, the whole business
is a sinful snatch at one of the most valuable
concessions in New South Wales. The syn-
dicate is to get sole possession of by far the
best site —almost the only possible site, in
fact—for a submarine line across Sydney
Harbor. It will occupy Dawes Point on one
side and Milson’s Point on the other, and
have the shortest crossing and the one
nearest to the business part of the city. On
the North Shore side it will occupy whatever
space is available at Milson’s Point station ;
therefore it shuts down on any possibility of
extending the present Stale line under the
city and the harbor to connect with the
North Shore rail way at that con-
venient point. Some day or other, it
is admitted. by everyone, the State line
must be extended through the city. When
that is done the shortest, cheapest and best
route would be to connect from Redfern via
Dawes Point with North Shore, building
intermediate stations where required. The
new line, instead of the present awkward
and lengthy deviation by Strathfield, Byde
and Carlingford, would then be the great
national railway that connects S.A. with
Queensland. Even now this interprovincial
railroad forms a connection from Oodnadatta
to Charleville, a distance of nearly 3000 miles,
and before very many years have elapsed it
will probably form a direct track from Perth
to the Gulf of Carpentaria. All the syndi-
cate asks, therefore, is that it may own
about miles in the centre and busiest part
of this great railroad, and wait there to be
bought out when it becomes an absolutely
unendurable nuisance.
* * #
The syndicate also wants a monopoly of
the local traffic between Sydney and the now
city which is growing up on the northern side
of the harbor. The State is creating that
new city and making the traffic by the con-
struction of the costly and, so far, un-
profitable line to Hornsby. That State-
created traffic has made the pro-
posed tunnel possible and profitable, and
the syndicate asks that the State line shall
act as a feeder to its little private spec. If
the tunnel were a State undertaking the
trains would go right through to Sydney,
but the intervention of a private line will
perpetuate the present awkward and need-
less break in the journey. When the old
railroad is extended from Redfern into
Sydney proper, then the Government will be
confronted with the following alternatives.
(1) It may let the syndicate's tunnel form
the sole connection between the two cities, in
which case there will be a permanent and
cumbrous break in the direct transcon-
tinental line, and the two State arteries of
traffic will both exist largely to serve as
feeders for the private enterprise. (2) The
State may build its own connection with
North Shore, but in that case it will have to
take a longer and more expensive route, and
the fact that the syndicate has a lien on
the Milson s Point landing-place will pro-
bably make it necessary to shift that
station to some other site in order to
get room to make a connection ; and, in
any case, the private line will take much
of the local traffic to the serious detri-
ment of the public revenue. Or (3) the
State may buy up the private enterprise, to
the great profit of the syndicate ; in other
words, it may buy ba’ck for solid cash the
concession which it now gives for absolutely
nothing. And the State must face one of
these three alternatives in the near future.
* * #
New South Wales has absolutely nothing
to gain by han ing over the North Shore
submarine railway or tramway—whichever
it may be called - to Messrs. John Sulman
and Co. instead of making it a part of the
State railway and tramway system. The
money for the work will no doubt be raised in
England—as the money always is in such
cases—and it will form an addition to the
foreign debt of the province, just as much as
if it was borrowed by the Government. The
receipts will go to England as dividends,
whereas the other way they would go to
England and be called interest ; the name is
different, but the drain on the public
resources is just the same. As regards
cost, the difference is wholly in favor of
State ownership. The State has its twm
termini, at Redfern and MUson’s Point, and
has only to construct the line and the in-
termediate platforms. It has also its work-
ing staff and board of management. The
private co. has to build its termini at both
ends, to supply working staff, directorate,
and rolling-stock, and the interest on the
money thus needlessly expended and the
extra working charges have to be made out
of the country in some shape or other ; also,
the unnecessary capital outlay will have to
be repaid by the country when the line is
taken over, which must happen sooner or
later. The break in the journey at Milson’s
Point will still continue, the only difference
being that the railway passenger will trans-
fer himself to a private car instead of a
private steamer, so that there is very little
gain in convenience as a set-off' against the
loss incurred by handing over to a syndicate
the only good crossing-place on the harbor.
Moreover, even the stipulations about the
deposit and che expenditure of £30,000 in 18
months are no guarantees that Sydney will
get its Uv.m"’Hv after all. The value of the
concession is such that the syndicate may, if it
chooses, simply pay its £3OOO, and expend the
£30,000 on preliminary work, and then lie
low while it endeavors, by the aid of Parlia-
mentary influence, to force the State to buy
it out at a high figure in order to clear the
way for railway extension.
* * #
In such a matter State and private enter-
prise stand on an entirely different footing.
The State builds its railways and tramways
to work them straightforwardly for the
public convenience and the profit of the
Treasury. The private speculator gets his
concession generally that he may sell it at a
good price to a syndicate, and the syndicate
endeavors to transfer it, at a big advance, to
a limited liability co. after grabbing at the
promoters’ shares and judiciously letting a
number of “substantial” people have an
interest so that it may have a solid hold on
political influence for future emergencies.
The next business is generally to borrow
money on debentures for construction pur-
poses. The main object of everybody con-
cerned is to -boom the shares and sell at a
big advance, and to work Parliament for
new concessions, and to manipulate the
share-market, and to pass the thing on to
someone else at an increased price, and to
grab at directorships ; and the public con-
venience doesn’t come in at all except in so
far as it must be studied in order to help
along the interests of the promoters. In
short, the chief end and aim of almost every
big concession is to sell ouc, to the public if
need be ; to the State—which is regarded as
a foolish and gullible institution—-if possible.
* * •
To sum the matter up, Sydney wants to
carry its railway through the city and unite
it with the North Shore branch so as to
make one continuous connection. It wants
to run its passenger service right through
instead of having to transfer from the rail-
way to the bus or tramcar at one end and
to the steamboat at the other, so that the
journey from St. Leonards or Gordon to
stations on the Redfern line can be done in
one continuous act instead of by three in-
stalments. Also, it wants to save the expen-
sive cartage of goods into the city, or their
transference, so far as concerns the Northern
suburbs, by the slow and primitive horse-
punt. And if it gives the proposed con-
cession the passenger journey will still have
to be made by three awkward instalments as at
present, the only difference being that the
submarine tram will come in instead of the
steam ferry. Also, the goods traffic will not
be assisted in any material degree, as the trans-
fer from the private to the public vehicle will
have to be made just as at present. Further,
the business of the North Shore Ferry Co.,
which is at least a local enterprise, will be
ruined to help along a mainly foreign syndi-
cate that will offer very little more conveni-
ence than the Ferry Co. now does. The
extension of the railway system will be
blocked, or else it will be necessary to buy
out the new monopoly at great cost, or
to make an extensive and costly deviation
to avoid its ground, in which case the
private line will compete against it and
eat considerably into the profits, or the
State must let the syndicate own the
busiest section of the biggest State rail-
way in Australia. And further, a great
vested interest will be created which will in-
fluence and crooken the Legislature, as all
such big monopolies do. Moreover, it is
noteworthy that the syndicate scheme was
never heard of until the Railway Depart-
ment seriously recommended the execution
of a similar work by Government, which
lends still more color to the suspicion that it
exists, quite as much for speculative as for
construction purposes. Under all these
circumstances every politician should be
called upon by his constituents to vote
straight and consistently against this stu-
pendous grab.
A Severed Knot!
“ Oxx, the agony ! ” ejaculated unhappy Arthur
Dacre, as he stood bleeding to death by the
corpse of the wife he had sent before him. In
which terribly pregnant laconicism no record of
any anguish but physical appears hitherto to
have been discovered by the general public—-
that smugly insensate public which, having
practically done to death these “ star-crossed
lovers,” has since been solacing itself with the
gory incidentals to its own deed, and has
hustled away—in its vulgar hunger after cheap
sensation—the very mourners from the grave-
side. And to the abject squalidity of soul of
which such things are symbols are unquestion-
ably due the violent and untimely deaths of
the Dacres.
* # #
With the genuine artist, his work—graphic,
literary, musical, or histrionic—constitutes,
for him, a second and a major individuality,
to which, in his own eyes, his mere
personality is but a more or less in-
significant adjunct. And the Dacres were
genuine artists to the tips of their fingers. The
virtual professional status of either husband or
wife is beside the question, although in the
former case it was more than respectable, and,
in the latter, little, if at all, below the top of
the tree in the lady’s special line. Amy
Roselle—despite the inevitable physical deca-
dence to which she would never confess, and of
which she was, no doubt, entirely unconscious—
was still a vivid, convincing, and accomplished
actress, to whom, with marvellous effect, would
come at times electrifying flashes of her former
power, and Arthur Dacre—all untouched, per-
haps, of the celestial fire of genius—was never-
theless far enough removed from mediocrity to
be capable of sincere, original, and highly-
finished dramatic work. And the fact that his
histrionic possibilities were not of a higher
order was in no way incompatible with such an
enthusiastic devotion to, and respect for, his
art as would not let him long survive the cir-
cumstance that his wife and himself—a charm-
ing, refined, and intellectual woman, and a
high-bred, chivalrous, and keenly-sensitive man
—had been compelled, as a pis alter, to assist
in providing for the play-going swine the
lamentable hog-wash without which it refuseth
to be comforted.
* * #
It may be that Dacre gave circumstance too
easy a victory, and—if it were not the most
miserable fatuity for anybody, under one set of
psychological conditions, to presume to say
what another person’s conduct should be under
an entirely different set—it might be asserted
that a man with any grit in him would have
“ seen it out ” for the sake, at any rate, of the
“poor little woman” whom he so tenderly
loved, and who, according to his own inex-
pressibly pathetic statement to a friend, had
fought on his behalf such a desperate battle
with disappointment and misfortune. All such
speculation, however, is as hopelessly futile as
is the discussion of the degree to which Dacre
was responsible for his actions at the time of
the double immolation. There is much in the
piercing simplicity of his last epistles to point to
that pseudo-hysterical condition which—simu-
lating in the one sex an affection specially
characteristic of the other—means practically
temporary insanity j and the fact, also, that
Dacre—a man of normally generous and con-
fiding disposition—had latterly become liable,
under pressure of anxiety, to a condition com-
bining suspiciousness with irritability, goes far
to make out a primd facie case for the incipience
in his self-tortured mind of that variety of
mania characterised by what is called persecu-
tory or conspiratory delusion. But the point
as to whether the unfortunate fellow was or
was not specifically insane loses all practical
significance in view of the simple fact that, since
he did what he did, his mental balance must
have been at the time sufficiently disturbed to
allow of his doing it. Agendum est ! is the
most unanswerable of all arguments.
*# • *
Sheer waste of time, also, is it to endeavor to
determine whether or not the devoted wife was
a consenting party to her swift and painless
taking-off. It is hardly less certain that she
was than it is that she would not, in any case,
have long survived the man to whom she was
attached by the very strongest link affection
can forge—the recollection of a long co-partner-
ship in the struggle with evil fortune. “ Love,”
as a generic term, is made to comprehend a
large variety of emotional conditions which
differ, not only in degree, but in kind. Be-
tween such a perfect and beautiful mutual
attachment as that of the Dacres and the
“ love ” which could endure, after the lapse of
a miserable month or two, the substitution of
another partner for the ostentatiously-lamented
dead, there is absolutely nothing in common
but the label.
* *
If some men like Shakespeare’s Third
Richard—are “ born to bite the world,” there
are others who are born to be bitten by it—and
of these was Arthur Dacre. “He killed him-
self,” said a friend of his, “ with imaginary
troubles,” and—setting aside the fact that,
although an apprehension may be ill-founded,
it cannot possibly be in itself “ imaginary ”
the utterance furnishes the key-note to the
hopeless inability of a mutton-headed public to
comprehend that what doesn’t wring its withers,
or disturb its liver, or cost it a night’s sleep—-
to say nothing of driving it to apply a razor to
its fat, complacent throat—may inflict any or
all of these undesirable exercitations upon the
more highly-strung organism which serves, here
and there, to lighten up somewhat the leaden
density of the prevailing human dough. “An
additional source of annoyance” to the Dacres,
serenely observes a well-known entrepreneur,
“ would be the necessity of appearing in new
pieces which they could not care about.” Not
care about ! The heart-sick loathing with
which a delicately-apprehensive pair like the
Dacres would find themselves mouthing the
ignominious fustian of the “ piece ” which prac-
tically finished them, can, perhaps, best be
estimated by those whose evil hap it is to be
professionally constrained to sit out the long
farrago of turgid balderdash—artistically con-
temptible, and intellectually not even worth con-
tempt —which, too often, nowadays, superscribes
itself a “drama,” and which, miserabile dicta,
is tolerably certain to replenish that managerial
cash-box which has returned but empty echoes
to the lofty voices of the immortals.
• • *
But, what is, is ! —and a state of things for
which there is at present no perceivable remedy
is only worth referring to because, but for its
existence, the gifted and kindly Dacres would
probably not be to-day “twin-tombed beside
an alien sea.” Disgust with a degraded public
taste—a weariness of the artistic spirit, more or
less chronic with every discriminative intel-
ligence—appears to have reached, in this
instance, a violently acute development, and to
have focussed itself with overpowering and
disastrous effect upon the mind of a man who—
though, perhaps, personally and artistically
super-sensitive—lacked, nevertheless, that use-
ful husk of mechanical conceit which has pre-
served, under much more desperate conditions,
the hopefulness—and, by consequence, the life—
of many an inferior man to Arthur Dacre.
* * *
The segregatory element which lies at the
root of the domestic sentiment has many a time,
ere now, found its extreme development in the
compact by which husband and wife have
“doomed their own hearts to undivided dust,”
but never before, perhaps, has the soul of any
community been stirred by such a singular
and harrowing combination of the idyllic with
the tragic as appears in the beautiful and terri-
ble story of the Dacres. The callow theological
prig may glibly relegate them to the “zealots’
ready hell,” and the helots of a hide-bound
ecclesiasticism may parade, once more, the
grotesque imbecility about “consecrated
ground,” but only a sordid soul or a purblind
intelligence will have room for any feeling but
a profound and enduring pity for the pair who
have elected thus, hand in hand, to cross
——the mystic line
That parts a life whose joys and wosa
Themselves are Heavens and Hells
From what, beyond—no mortal knows—
And no immortal tells!
A.M.
PLAIN ENGLTSH.
Fat Man in Racing Clubs.
Now that Arthur Anderson, the V.A.T.C.
steward, has remembered the truth about an
episode in his turf career, and apologised to
“J. B. Clark,” it is interesting to .look back
and observe the good old Tory method of deal-
ing with truth-tellers at public meetings, when
they happen to reflect upon any influential
person s character. This is what happened at


the V.A.T.C. meeting two months ago. In the
course of a warm discussion, “J. B. Clark”
accused the chairman of having sat, as a judge,
upon a racing appeal case in which he was
financially interested. Arthur Anderson was
that chairman. In the natural course of such
things he arose, with indignation in his eye, and
called “ J. B. Clark ” a liar. Also, the more
highly-respectable section of the meeting cried
“Shame!” upon the wicked imputator, in
exactly the same tone of voice as was adopted
by the friends of Smellbourne’s busted bank
directors in the early days of “ revelations,”
when facts were only beginning to assert them-
selves, and the bluffers had not yet thrown up
the sponge. “ J. B. Clark’s ” allegation was
scandalous, outrageous, disgraceful, infamous —
anything that these highly-respectable gentle-
men liked to call it. Frank Madden moved a
characteristic resolution to the effect that the
club didn’t believe “ J. B. Clark’s ” monstrous
statement, and James Grice (part-owner of
Hova) seconded it, and the brazen bit of
stupidity was declared carried by a respectable
law’norder majority. “ J. B. Clark ” had said
something nasty concerning a director of the
company, so to speak, therefore he was howled
to scorn, and recorded as a libellous liar upon
the minutes of the meeting. Had it been
possible for the great Spirit of Respectability
to strike the offender dead before he could
prove his charge, he would have given the
Spirit no further trouble. The “Lardy-Dardy
scandal ” would have been buried with his dis-
credited corpse. But “ Clark ’’-Bowden lived
to gather corroborative evidence, and enjoy the
graceful backing-down of Mr. Anderson. Here
is Mr. Anderson’s recantation: “I sat at
Moonee Valley, on the 23rd Nov., ’9l, as chair-
man of stewards when the Lord John and
Lardy-Dardy case was dealt with. Four other
stewards were present, and, as all were
unanimous in allowing the protest, no necessity
for my vote arose, and I did not vote. I appeared
shortly afterwards in my representative capacity
with another Moonee Valley steward, by in-
vitation, before the V.R.C. committee on appeal,
and stated that I had backed Lord John and
had not voted on the protest. The appeal was
dismissed. I had forsotten the circumstances
of this case when Mr. Bowden suddenly brought
the matter up in a manner that seemed to im-
pute dishonest motives to me. I am exceedingly
sorry that in the heat of the moment I made an
accusation of falsehood against Mr. Bowden,
and will do all that lies in my power to have
the motion that the meeting disbelieved his
statement expunged.” Fortunately Mr. Ander-
son can’t expunge the moral lesson of this
Lardy-Dardy rumpus, which was not, as now
appears, so much an ‘sporting scandal as
a modern official outrage. Anderson’s share
in the business is less worth bearing in mind
than the precious resolution fathered by Frank
Madden and seconded by James Grice. Some-
body “ seems to impute dishonest motives ” to
the chairman of a public meeting, who gives
his accuser the lie direct. Whereupon Fat
Man expresses his solemn, dunder-headed dis-
approval of all aspersions on the powers that
be by deciding, off-hand, without enquiry, that
the other fellow’s statement is necessarily
false and malicious.
The Gunuedah Romance.
The recent developments in the Gunnedah
(N.S.W.) alleged poisoning-case are a whole
essay on the unreliability of the female witness.
The heroine of the romance, Ethel Wright, a
guileless child of nature, aged 17, swore that
one morning at 4 o’clock she saw' a neighbor
named Roberts putting strychnine in the
family flour-bin. Finding himself discovered,
he pointed a gun at her, threatened to blind
her with vitriol, and poured a quantity of it over
her, thereby burning large holes in her night-
dress and slightly injuring her shoulder, and
otherwise conducted himself like the villain of
the six-act melodrama. The story came to
grief chiefly through want of attention to
detail. The strychnine was identified as some
that had been sold, not to Roberts, but to the
girl’s aged and pious uncle. There were no
tracks to show where Roberts had invaded the
premises. The night-dress had apparently been
just taken out of a drawer and not worn,
whereas the gentle Ethel swore she had it on
at the time of the trouble. Also, the small
burn on her shoulder was not at all com-
mensurate with the large amount of • vitriol
which had been poured over the garment.
Owing to this want of finish Roberts wasn’t
hanged amid the execrations of an infuriated
populace, and when the story showed definite
signs of breaking down Miss Wright confessed
that it was all pure invention, and had been
made up by the pious uncle aforesaid, and re-
peated by her under that relative’s compulsion.
Thereupon she and the uncle were both
arrested. A little later, she further confessed
that the uncle hadn’t invented the story, nor
incited her to tell it, nor been in any way con-
nected with it; the whole affair was her
own romance from first to last. Then the
dazed old man was turned loose, and the girl
remains in gaol awaiting further developments
and revelations. So far as appears, up to date,
she had no personal grudge against the man
whom she accused, nor against her uncle ; the
narrative seems to have been got up in a per-
fectly casual fashion, possibly through
hysteria, or a cracked brain, or much read-
ing about the duke in the London Journal,
or a desire to brighten up her dreary
life on a backblocks selection with a little
romance, or something of the sort. Even
her recantations don’t count for any more
than her original statements, except where
they are supported by independent evidence.
A wildly irresponsible female may swear to
anything, or confess anything. The hysterical
girl is an imitative person, and if one of the
tribe takes it into her head some day to give
herself into custody for an imaginary and pic-
turesque crime, others will almost certainly
follow suit, and then a new trouble will be
added to those that already harass policeman
O’Hara, of the backblocks. If some one would
tour the country districts—especially the more
lonesome agricultural regions—with a drama in
which the beautiful heroine, for some roman tic-
reason or other, gives herself in custody on a
false charge of the most gory character, he
might produce highly interesting results. Bub
this motive hasn’t been used yet —at least, not
much used. In the average drama and tale the
heroine is always falsely accused, or otherwise
harassed by the machinations of some pic-
turesque ruffian or other, and the local girl,
who wants some more romance in her life than
is afforded by a heavy Wesleyan uncle and a
cow, goes and does likewise. If the picturesque
ruffian isn’t at hand she constructs him, in her
own crude fashion, out of some dull, common-
place selector or swagman, who is generally
utterly unfitted for the part, and might as well
be asked to play Macbeth at a minute’s notice.
Then the romance naturally goes wrong, but it
is a relief from the monotony of the pious uncle
and the cow while it lasts. The grievous, dull-
brown, arid sameness of the old man and the
cow have probably more to do with such out-
breaks as "that of Miss Wright than is com-
monly imagined.
Melb. University.—A Justification.
Dear Bulletin, —In your issue of Nov. 16,
your correspondent “ Fluffy ” makes an attack
on Melbourne University, with small founda-
tion in fact, and with many absolute mis-state-
ments. (1) “ What does the University do for
its Government-grant of £12,250 ? ” It sup-
plies teaching, examinations, and degrees to
over 600 students per annum, whose fees and
living expenses would otherwise go out of the
province ; it examines annually at the matricu-
lation examinations 1400 boys and girls; it
provides Victoria with its solicitors, barristers,
doctors, civil engineers, and is beginning to
supply it with first-class musicians, assayers,
metallurgists, geologists, and commercial
chemists ; by its teaching, examinations, and
guidance it “ promotes sound learning” through-
out the province, and sets and maintains the
standard of general education. (2) “It imports
a large and expensive staff.” (a) Of the teach-
ing staff of 37, only 10 were specially imported;
applications are always advertised for through-
out the provinces as well as in London, and the
best-qualified candidates are appointed. None
of the skilled assistants or clerical staff were
imported, (b) The staff is large, because 123
subjects are taught and 150 subjects examined
in. (cj The staff' is expensive because, to get
the best men and enable them to do their best
work, the salaries must be such as to make
them practically independent. The salaries!
are lower than those of the best full-time
professors in the United Kingdom; lower
than those of Supreme Court Judges,
heads of civil departments, cabinet ministers,
heads of big public schools, government
experts, and the like ; much less than the in-
comes of solicitors, barristers, bankers, brokers,
doctors, &c., in the first flight. The salaries of
the part-time teachers are lower than in Great
Britain. (3) “ The University is a mere train-
ing-ground for several professions—law, theo-
logy, medicine, school-teaching, and so forth.”
The University is absolutely debarred by its
charter from teaching, examining or giving
degrees in theology ; pedagogics it does not
teach. Theology is taught by denominational
halls outside of and wholly unconnected with
the University ; pedagogics are taught to
would-be school-teachers by special officers of
the Education Department unconnected with
the University. In addition to being a train-
ing-ground for the professions of medicine, law,
engineering and music, the University provides
all-comers with a liberal education and sound
learning in its arts and science courses. (4)
“ The professors only lecture when they please,
and habitually stay away without notice.” Not
one per cent, of lectures are omitted ; in-
tended omission is always notified in the
morning papers, on notice - boards and
lecture-room doors, and, in the medical
course, also at Melbourne Hospital. (5) “ At
lecture-time, four anajmic students appear and
wait.” Extremely few classes are below 20;
most are from 30 to 60 strong ; some number as
high as 120 members. Under-graduate anaemia
is a matter between students and their phy-
sicians, no concern of the University as such.
(6) “One professor recently repudiated his lec-
tures for a week, without notice—a typical
case.” In that week his wife died and was
buried, therefore the case was not typical; due
notice was given. (7) As to day lectures. It
takes nine hours a day to deliver the present
instruction, therefore shifting it into the night
is impossible, unless night is to be turned to
day and lectures given, say, from 7 p.m.
to 4 a.m. Further, practical scientific work,
dissections, and, above all, hospital work,
can be done only inconveniently and either
badly or not at all at night. At all universities
teaching is done by day. Evening lectures,
where given, are but few, and then given by a
special staff. Again, when, five years ago, an
agitation arose for evening lectures,-the Univer-
sity offered to supply them, if the demand were
sufficient. Only some three dozen applied, and
they wanted over two dozen subjects lectured
on. The University naturally, and with good
warrant, declined. (8) “The door should be
open to the clever youth of the whole com-
munity, not reserved for gilded youth.” The
door is so open. The State awards exhibitions
annually to the best State school pupils. The
University colleges offer 12 £SO and eight £25
scholarships, and 12 £lO exhibitions. The
University chest gives prizes, exhibitions and
scholarships in cash worth £1526. Special
endowments provide cash scholarships worth
£4BOO. That is, over £7246 is given annually
in cash prizes, which are open alike to the

The State as an Importer.
For 20 years past Victoria has regularly had a
large surplus of grain for export, varying from
about £50,000 worth to the value of about
£1,750,000, and during the last 15 years the
smallest balance of exports over imports in
this line has been £250,000. In the drought-
season of 1895-96, however, the province is
confronted by the possibility of a new experi-
ence. The failure of the crops has been very
extensive; there may be a small balance for
export, or the supply may not even quite equal
local requirements—at present no one can say
for certain. Under its protective system Vic-
toria, despite its very small area, has come to
be the largest grower of wheat, oats, barley,
potatoes, wine, and many other products in
Australia; still even this cannot guarantee
that, in the worst drought year, it will grow
enough for its own population. The four or
five Foreign-traders in Vic. Assembly already
propose to use this exceptional state of things
as an argument in favor of their importing policy.
If Victoria has a shortage in this one year it must
import; if it is compelled to import the protective
duties will make the prices of the imported
grain needlessly high, there being insufficient
local competition. The only remedy for this,
according to the Freetrade argument, is to
abolish the duties permanently and to wreck
the great Vic. agricultural industry for good,
because of one season’s casual scarcity. And if
the worst should happen, and the local supplies
be actually insufficient, a good many people,
with the usual illogical tendency to run to
extremes, will probably accept this view. Yet
there is a very simple and effective middle
course which would fully meet the emergency,
if the said emergency should come to pass.
Let the protective duties, which have served
Victoria so well in the past, be maintained.
But, as a provision against that possible year
of scarcity which may arrive once in a genera-
tion or two until an efficient irrigation system
is established, it should be enacted that, when
the local supply is unequal to the demand —the
latter being estimated at the average of the
previous two or three years —the Agricultural
Department should be authorised to import,
duty free, enough grain to fully supply the defi-
ciency, and should sell it in small lots at current
prices. Unless the scarcity was world-wide it
would realise a handsome profit on the deal, and
it is only in accord with democratic principles
that this profit, made out of the necessities of
the people, should go into the people’s Treasury,
instead of going into the hands of private specu-
lators, as it would do if the duties were sus-
pended to meet the difficulty. The case may
be summed up in four propositions : (1) Victoria
has come so near to actual scarcity, if it has not
actually arrived there, through the present
drought, that it is quite evident it may get
there very badly some other year when the
population is larger and the drought equally
bad or worse ; (2) Protection alone doesn’t
meet the difficulty, for Customs duties don’t
provide for droughts; (3) Freetrade doesn’t
meet it any better—all it can do is to destroy
the local agricultural industry in good years,
and then a time would come, sooner or later,
when there was a sudden scarcity in the outer
world, and the province, with no large local
supplies to rely upon, would suffer from famine
prices ; (4) therefore, what is wanted is Protec-
tion at all ordinary times and in famine years,
if they come, just as much Freetrade as the
temporary necessity demands. But when,
through some unavoidable disaster, it is neces-
sary to supplement local supplies the State
should be the only importer, so that any money
that is made out of the necessities of the
people shall be the people’s property.
Thrown Away.
The troubles of the Homeward Bound Mining
Co. of Yalwal (N.S.W.) serve to illustrate, for
the benefit of Australasia in general, one of the
remarkable features of a very much mixed legal
system. This co. bought its lease from the
previous holder for .£19,000 and expended
£46,000 in machinery and working expenses.
It paid its rent to the Treasury regularly for a
time, and then by a luckless oversight on
the part of its Sydney agent the payments
were allowed to fall into arrears. The conse-
quence was that the lease was cancelled. So
far there was nothing in the legal system that
could reasonably be objected to. The holder
of a mining lease who fails to pay his rent or
fulfil the other conditions of his tenure does so
entirely at inis own risk ; if he doesn’t do his
duty, or see that his deputy does it, he must
take the consequences. But at this point the
wholly ridiculous feature of the case comes in.
The Homeward Bound mine reverted to the
State. It was a valuable property, and with
the shafts, drives and other works that
were necessarily forfeited along with it
would probably have realised £12,000 or
so cash at auction. There was a very
large battery on the ground, and as this heavy
article would hardly pay the cost of taking
down, removal and re-erection, the new lease-
holder would almost certainly be able to get it
at a mere fraction of its cost, which further
enhanced the value of the claim. Yet there is
no provision in the N.S.W. mining laws
whereby the State could sell this important asset
so as to realise anything like its worth. When
theforfeiture was gazetted the-ground wassimply
thrown open to be scrambled for by the first
comers, just as if it had been a piece of bare
ground in the wilderness whose value was
wholly problematical and might be nil. The
scramble, of course, came off, and, as- is usual
in such cases, was followed by long and expen-
sive litigation, and the State, in addition to
giving away .£12,000 worth of property, paid a
considerable amount in judicial salaries over
the matter. This sort of thing seems eminently
ridiculous. When the State forfeits a lot of
smuggled jewellery it sells the goods for the
best price obtainable, but if the Customs were
run on the same principle as the Mines
Department it would dump the whole lot down
in the middle of the street to be scrambled for
by all comers. As it doesn’t follow this course,
it would seem that the Customs knows how to
come in out of the rain, and the Mines doesn’t.
A Triumph, of Noodledom.
Pitt Town Co-operative Settlement is dead :
who would not weep for Pitt Town ? Pitt
Town Settlement was born a little more than
two years ago, with the prayers of every
benevolent noodle in New South Wales. Dur-
ing those two years, about 100 men, 100 women,
and 200 children have fed on the bounty of the
charitable New South Wales taxpayer. They
have received a subsidy of £7705 in solid cash,
and have earned and spent another £IOOO in
addition —total £8705. They have been sup-
plied with a large tract of land of excellent
agricultural quality in some parts, in others
covered with saleable timber; with a .£220
sawmill, horses, cattle, carts, drays, and imple-
ments ; with a school, a library, and, at any rate,
half a church ; and with oceans of good advice.
There was every requisite for “a beautiful Arca-
dian existence.” Thewolf was keptfromthedoor
by Govt, rations : there was nothing to do but
go ahead and prosper. Philanthropic gentle-
men blessed the settlers; M’sP. wagged formid-
able jaws at them ; in their honor was held a
magic-lantern shivoo at Sydney Town Hall,
when a reverend orator waxed ecstatic over a
display of the settlement’s agricultural pro-
duce ; pointed triumphantly to mammoth twin
pumpkins (himself the rarest pumpkin of the
three); and explained that the New Era was
arriving, Universal Brotherhood was coming
real slick, and Pitt Town Settlement was the
first instalment. And now, of all these glories,
what remains? The sawmill (more or less
busted by Arcadian arts), the horses
and cattle and carts, the fencing, and
eight dozen Arcadian cottages fast going to
decay. Total value on the ground, perhaps
,£IOOO. Total sale-value for removal, perhaps
£SOO. Dead loss to the charitable tax-payer,
say, £7OOO. A batch of young lives shattered
and Pitt Town’s resignation handed in.
There still remain a few families gaining a
miserable living by clearing off the firewood,
thus taking the best remnant of value out of
the property ; but it is only a matter of days
before they, too, give up their ghost of a
struggle, and the New Era of Universal
Brotherhood goes once more to limbo. But
the noodles are nob disheartened. Prodigal
of plans, singularly economical of their own
cash, they only want the charitable
taxpayer to advance a few more thousands, and
they are confident they can galvanise the
corpse. Your true noodle never learns from ex-
perience. He never sees that direct Govt, aid
demoralises most men, and that the greater the
aid the greater the demoralisation. He never
sees that, in every community of co-operators,
everybody wants the other fellow to take the
heavy end of the log, and that reliance on the
other fellow weakens the muscles, slackens the
joints, saps the energy, and ruins the character
even of the originally deserving. The Bulletin,
after long years of observation, rises to affirm
that the noodle is incorrigible.
The Vic. Factories Bill provides for the stamp-
ing of all Chinese-made furniture, any person
removing the stamp to be liable to a £5
penalty. Murray Smith and his little Free-
trade following fought desperately against this
last clause, on the ground that when a person
bought an article he had a right to do as he
liked with it, and any enactment to the con-
trary would be “an interference with the
liberty of the subject.” As the person who
bought the article would be, in most cases, the
dealer who intended selling it over again,
this plea for liberty involved, of course,
liberty to sell it as the product of white
labor, so the Assembly sat heavily on
Chinaman Smith and his amendment'. Another
interference with the liberty of the subject was
added in the shape of a clause providing that
no person employed in a factory or workroom
is to receive less than 2s. 6d. a week. This is
meant to knock out the “improver” swindle.
Some large millinery and other workrooms are
now run almost wholly by “ improvers ” who
receive no wages for the first year, when they
are supposed bo be learning the business, but
are promised good pay in their second year.
When that time arrives, however, they are run
out, and other “improvers” take their places
on the same conditions. The Council, how-
ever, is strong on the “ liberty of the subject,”
and will probably make trouble over some of
these reforms when the Factories Bill comes
within its reach.
Barrister Knox, M.L.A., argues that legal
amalgamation will simply lead to partner :
ships between lawyers who now practise in
separate branches. This arrangement would
be infinitely preferable to the present one.
Every term, the Supreme Court has to deal
with a few of its officers (mostly practising by
themselves) for downright thievery, which
would ensure an unprivileged rogue anything
up to 14 years’gaol. Partnerships (there being
safety in numbers) would obviously avert this
to some extent —unless Mr. Knox makes the
unlawyerlike admission that every lawyer is a
scoundrel addicted to conspiracies of the ugliest
kind. Willis, M.P., touches the real key-
note of reform in his clause to prevent legal
gentlemen from absorbing fees without even
pretending to earn them. Some time ago, a
popular “big” favorite appeared before the
Supreme Court, in a certain suit. Junior
counsel had nob arrived when the case was
called. “ Would y’honah allow this matter to
stand over for a few minutes ? ” asked the
Q.C.; “Mr.- isn’t here yet.” “Can’t
you go on without him?” “I didn’t get the
brief until this morning, y’honah, and I only
partially know the facts.” “’m. Is the
ejectment by a mortgagor?” “Well—er—
y’honah, I can’t remember. Er—you see, I’ve
so much to attend to.” Mr. Willis’s amend-
ment, if it becomes law, will do much to limit
this privileged pilfering, which has almost
vanished in neighboring provinces as an imme-
diate outcome of legal amalgamation.
The Melb. General Assembly, a pathetic set of
local parsons, is to consider whether raffling at
bazaars is a wicked thing, but in its sanctified
stupidity it leaves alone the question whether
bazaars may not be a more holy fraud than
raffles. In a raffle, no matter how much behind
is the parson’s salary, it is generally possible to
get something more valuable than the amount
risked, but an ordinary bazaar-sale is ordinary
swindling—except when it is sweating. The
sallow virgin of 47, who offers a smoking-
cap and a pair of braces for ten shillings
each, knows perfectly well that the lot is
not worth ninepence, but the more she gets
the larger and more monkey-like will be her
pastor’s smile. These priests make their temple
a den of thieves as in the olden time, they con-
secrate trade immorality for the sake of their
fetish, and it is small additional wrong if they
add a raffle and a race-game to the other detrac-

Visitors to the Spouting Spring at Heli-
don, 70 miles from Brisbane, are always astounded
at the quantity of water they can consume at the
Spring. Ten or 12 tumblers in an hour or so are
taken with comfort. The reason is that the
tissues of the body are absorbing the water all the
time. Something beneficial is always added to
the system by every glass of Helidon Spa water
consumed. Messrs. Tooth and Co. Kent
Brewery, are the Sydney agents.

The Vic. Sinking'-Fund.
The Victorian Government, at the last moment,
inserted a clause in the Malice Bill providing
that, after Ist July, 1896, all revenue from
mallee lands shall be set aside to form a fund
for the reduction of the public debt. If the
House of 48 Rich Landlords does not interfere
with this wise arrangement, it will form an
interesting experiment. In the first place, it
is a recognition by Victoria that being hope-
lessly in pawn to the British money-lender
should not be the permanent and recognised
condition of the country. Further, it implies
that under the Turner regime, at all events,
Victoria is done with the foreign-borrowing
curse, for (except in Maoriland) it is recognised
as a futile and absurd expedient to pay off old
debts with one hand while contracting new
ones with the other. The mallee country
yields a present revenue of .£16,000 a year,
which will probably increase to somewhere
between £50,000 and £lOO,OOO in a few years
under the new Act-the House of 48 Rich
Landlords permitting. The Turner Govt, also
hopes to be able, later on, to set aside the whole
land revenue of the province for debt-redemp-
tion purposes, but that is an affair of the
future. Assuming that the amount set apart
reaches £lOO,OOO a year, it will be sufficient at
simple interest to extinguish the present Vic.
public debt in about 450 years, so, though the
present small beginning is much better than
nothing, it is only a small beginning at the
best. If allowed to accumulate at compound
interest the process would be more rapid, but
Premier Turner evidently recognises the fact
that sooner or later an impecunious Govern-
ment would annex the fund for current revenue,
so his scheme provides that as each loan falls
due as much of it shall be paid off as the cash
in hand will admit, and the balance renewed as
usual. The impossibility of tying up a Govern-
ment so that it can’t get at the sinking-fund
has been fully evidenced in Maoriland, there-
fore the wisdom of Turner’s proposal is suffi-
ciently evident. If he succeeds in making a
steady reduction, however small, in the public
debt of Victoria, it will be the first definite
step in the right direction achieved by any Aus-
tralasian province since the borrowing disease
first began, and the fact that he has taken this
step so disastrous a period of the
province s finances says a lot for his courage and
his resolve to get back to the paths of national
honesty. The resolutions which every Govern-
ment which desires to see Australia free from a
degrading servitude to the British pawnbroker
needs to paste in its joint and several hat are
these four : (1) There should be no new loans
floated except for defence purnoses in case of
actual war ; (2) the revenue should be charged
every year with a certain sum, say a-quarter or
half a million, to allow of the construction of
new public works, in addition to keeping the
old ones in repair, whether the money has to
be raised by retrenchment, by new taxation,
by drink and tobacco monopolies, by State lot-
teries, or any other means. The first duty
of the State is to be solvent, even if in
being so it can t afford to be moral accord-
ing to the W.C.T.U. standard; (3) The
proceeds of some specified source of revenue,
even if it is a small one, should be set aside
to reduce the debts already existing; and
(4) as each loan falls due it should be renewed,
if possible, in the local market, so as to make
the remaining debt a local instead of a foreign
liability. The Government that puts these four
axioms in the forefront of its policy will be the
first honest one which this country has seen for
very many years. Meanwhile, the Turner
Government’s adherence to axiom 3, and the
fact that, so far, it has acted up to No. 1 and
has shown some signs of a leaning towards
No. 4, are points much in its favor.
Supplementing the S.M. Herald and other
newspaper “ suicide ” articles, prompted by
recent tragedies, the fact may be recalled that
a suicide State once really existed. Massilia,
which corresponds loosely with what is now Mar-
seilles, when under Greek government, conferred
on its subjects the nght to destroy themselves
for approved reasons. When, for instance, a
man like Lacre considered himself insufficiently
appreciated he was instructed to state his case
to what might be called the Governor in Council,
and, if that body judged his impeachment of
society to be well-founded, a decree was written
out and signed, empowering him to make away
with himself and forbidding the community to
attach any odious consequence to the act. In
many cases the governing body refused its
sanction, and then, if the desperate individual
took his life, public odium was free to follow
him and his. Sir Thomas More, in his
“ Ut°pi a ,” similarly provides for a certain class
of unfortunates. The philosophical theory is
that the individual’s life is the property of the
society of which he is a member, and that if he
and the society agree to extinguish it the act
is as valid as any other that can be sanctioned.
M° re confined the right to self-destruction,
under State sanction, to persons afflicted with
unendurable physical diseases, but did he see
with the eyes of to-day he would certainly have
included the ills of mind.
The Wild-Cat Column.
In the continued absence of any satisfactory
explanation from M.L. Govt, it now seems almost certain
that Treas. Ward has seized the sinking-funds of local
bodies which are really trust-moneys, as well as the
national sinking-fund to which he has a claim by law to
square his budget this year. In that case, Maoriland is
now using borrowed money for revenue purposes, which
is even worse than the old Vogel policy of using it for
P' l . I '. works. Maoriland has an enormous war-debt, in
addition to its public works debt, and with this extra
handicap it seems that the province can’t keep straight
or else it doesn’t try hard enough. Moreover, it is loaded
down with snuffle as well as with war-debts. In a reason-
«ii u B 'c u comm,, nity the hard-driven Treasurer mhdit
nil the financial gap by running a Tattersall’s consulta-
tion business as a State monopoly, or by establishing
btate drink, tobacco and opium monopolies, or by levying
taxes on other forms of vice, but the great national
snume forbids. In a truly pious country the Government
may square its accounts by sneaking the trust funds and
afterwards denying the fact, and the public conscience
doesn t object much; but if it proposed to raise money
out of some public vice which wouldn’t thereby be made
one fraction worse than it was before, the great national
hypocrisy would fall on it like an avalanche. The really
tngh-toned public conscience doesn’t much mind a little
mild dishonesty, or even lying, but it is an awful whale
on morality.’

It is now about two years since the private
note-issues were abolished in Queensland, and according
to the latest returns four banka have still £7443 of note*
in circulation in that province. It is two years since the
Bank of North Queensland ceased to issue notes in
N.S.W.: it had only one branch, and a very small and not
long-established local circulation, yet £62 of its paper-
money is reported still to be afloat. Of the temporary
N.S. W. Treasury notes, long since withdrawn,
£260 worth have not yet been presented for payment. A
good part of these amounts probably represent absolute
losses. No one is ever likely to know exactly wbat the
banks make through the disappearance of their notes by
means of bush-fires, burning houses, drunken bushmen
who use them for pipe lights, men who vanish in the
bush and are eaten by wild dogs, sunken ships, casual
crownings, floods, rats, mice, and miscellam ous forms of
destruction, but paper-money can be destroyed in so
many ways that the amount must be considerable. One
of the small side-issues of the State currency ques-
tion is that, when all notes are issued by the State, the
public Treasury will get whatever benefit is to be ob-
tained from this source of revenue.

„ According to the report of the National Wool
Co. it is estimated that Australia pays £700,000 a year for
tile warehousing of its wool in London and commission
on wool-sales. A good part of the clip—rather more than
one-sixth-was auctioned last year in Australia, and
fetched, on the whole, rather better than London prices.
If Australia can contrive by-and-bye to become indepen-
dent of the London market, these two almost unnoticed
items of warehousing and commission will mean a lot of
money retained in this suffering country.
Some S. A. authorities have just dropped to the
fact that the mimosa Aralica , from which gum-arabic
is procured, will grow as well in Central and Northern
Austiaha as in Africa, and an attempt is being made to
cultivate it. Gum-arabic is largely used for confectionery
and other purposes, and these provinces send away about
£14,000 annually to pay for imports of this sticky sub-
stance, the best quality of which fetches as much as 2s.
6d. per lb.
* • » •
F. ; “B. Liste ” points a very proper moral
about a badly-managed State Bank. Vic. and S.A. seem
likely to try the banking experiment soon, and even the
alleged failure of the railways doesn’t seem to frighten
the two provinces much. Is it not true that a badly-
managed State concern may be better than the best-
managed private concern ? If the Vic. railways had been
privately managed, the country railways in poor districts
would be either non est or gone bung at the present time
V 1 c that pay would be run on the plan
of the big combines in the States, where, in some cities,
extortionate fares and freights are wrung out of the
public. News of a trust, comprising 10 big companies,
has just been cabled. Moreover, the employes would be
as the tramway employes are now—sweated to the last
degree.
I® fch® case of banks, private management re-
sulted in the biggest crash and dislocation of business and
credit that any country has ever seen in recent years.
It involved £150,000,000 of capital and deposits in
one year—it involved every man, woman and child in Aus-
tralia. Furthermore, the astute financiers so muddled
things that the crisis is still with us, doddering along
from reconstruction to reconstruction. The deplorable
V[ic. Savings Banks, run by unpaid commissioners, did
-tts bit of harm, but not proportionately to the private
failures, and the State guarantee staved off the woes of
repudiation. When the State awakes to the bad manage-
ment of one of its departments it can take time for altera-
tions and repairs—a private concern must ‘simply’
suspend operations. But there is no need for the State
Bank to be badly-managed. There are, perhaps, five
capable financiers in the world who would be open to
accept office at £lO,OOO a year. As the required manager
would be worth £lOO,OOO a year, Vic. would make a clear
saving of £90,000 by paying him £lO,OOO. If the affair
were made inter-provincial, with interprovincial notes and
interprovmcial investments, there would be no limit to
the possibilities of its success.”

A Victorian provincial reconstructed bank-
manager suicided, the other day, because he never could
get over the shame of having told customers the bank
was sound. And the rest of the bankers say temporary
insanity, or, indeed, permanent. Write his epitaph :
Here rests a man with a heart.
* * • •
One R. Jones, in Melb., lectured to a novel
tune, the other night, on Insolvency and Sweating. He
argued—and let a Judge’s note be made of it—that sweat-
ing resulted from the insolvency laws not being keen
enough. Thus men entered upon trade with the"deter-
iiiination of going insolvent, and who could compete with
them ?

The two chief promoters of the proposed N.S. W.
iron and st-el works, Messrs. Mitchell, ex M.L.A., and
Dauncey, C.E., have sunk something like ilO 000
and are forming a small syndicate to complete their
labors ere tapping 1 the English market. Various experts
in the old world and the new have favorably reported on
the proposed sites, where coal, iron ore, and limestone are
said to lie close to each other in phenomenal abundance
It is believed that several big Govt, orders for iron have
been virtually promised. The ores are reported to be of
such a superior grade, and to lie so close to other neces-
sary minerals, that, despite the higher local wages pros-
pects are bright. Several rich British ironmasters’ it is
stated, will bear a hand in the scheme.

The Vic. Railway Commissioners refused, in
marked contrast to the Postal Department, to back down
over their advertising ticket contract. Neither will they
study the temperance bodies by prohibiting whisky and
tobacco advts. on the railway-tickets. The department
will get 56 millions of tickets free of cost (four years’
supply). By the new arrangement the community will
be m pocket £8166 12s. 4d., during the next four years.
