The need for retrenching salaries in Melb. com-
mercial circles has caused the dismissal of many niantio-f-rs
and head accountants who have held their positions'for
years with their respective firms. Their places have been
taken by juniors drawing, in many cases, not a third of
the salaries paid to their predecessors. £5OO to £BOO yearly
was once quite a usual screw j now £250 per annum is
about the limit, and the billets are rushed by really first-
class men at that.

The newest fruit - preservative is alcoholised
vapour. It is said to be absolutely effectual and so cheap
that a ton of fruit can be preserved on a voyage from
Australia to England for about 3s. per ton. If this is all
true, it means another boom for the Accursed Drink.

* » » •
Miner's pluck—and carelessness. Martin,
working an old claim near the Day Dream, lit a fuse at
the bottom of the shaft, and got on to the rope to be
hauled up. Then, having got a few feet up, he suddenly
remembered that he had left an open canister of powder
at the bottom. Before he could signal there was an ex-
plosion, and he was horribly burnt about the arms and
shoulders. Yet he hung on to the rope, was drawn to the
top, and then walked into Broken Hill, 12 miles. Sad to
relate, he died three days after.
* ♦ ♦ *
The Broken Hill North Central has been re-
vived under circumstances that seem to need explanation.
Nothing has been done at the “ mine ” for many months *
it has been practically abandoned. Calls were made but
remained unpaid. Then the Central was sold for three
times as much as the aggiegate value of its shares when
the offer was first talked about, and there was a rapid
upward movement all round in Broken Hill shares. The
North Central, having some debts, was advertised for sale
by auction. On the day when the sale was to take place
it was announced that the calls had been paid on enough
shares to wipe off the debt, and that the sale would no t
take place after all. In making this announcement th
secretary remarked upon the great value of the mine ; it
adjoined “the recently-sold Central mine, the Block 11
of the Proprietary Co., and the rich Block 10” ; also “all
the evidence ” showed that the B.H. lode was not under-
lying west, so that “ the great probability is that the
sinking of the shaft (now down 374 ft.) will result in
getting the great Block 11 lode.” Forthwith these shares,
which had gone begging for a threepenny call, sprang, or
rather were worked, to the neighborhood of 3s.
Meantime a rumor was diligently circulated
that the Proprietary had discovered another big lode-
body to the far east, adding greatly, of course, to the
value of the North Central, supposing it to be true that
the Broken Hill lode does underlay to the east. Whether,
however, it does underlay east or not, it is positively not
true that “all the evidence” is against a western under-
lay. It it does go east, it is certain that the lode must
turn in order to do it; for the first few hundred feet it
unquestionably goes west. The chances, then, of the
North Central getting the Block 11 lode are very remote
indeed ; the chances that it will get it at a workahledepth
are remoter still. And, it is worth remembering, the lode
at 1200 ft. in Block 10 (it hadn't passed out into the next
claim east at that depth) was, with our present Imperfect
knowledge of sulphide-treatment, worthless. Further
than that, the big find in the far east of the Proprietary
proves to be a small thing.
Anyway, here is the North Central co. hold-
ing a block of land which must absorb tens of thousands
of pounds in prospecting if the prospecting is to be Worth
anything ; yet it is in the hands of a co. which can just
pay its debts, and that is all. There is nothing, there-
fore, for the shareholders to do but go on paying call
after call for month after month—which, of course, share-
holders won’t do ; as a rule, they won’t pay a penny after
the stock has ceased to be quoted. If, then, the North
Central was not revived merely for the sake of having
more cheap gambling counters in the market, appearances
are deceptive ; for it is hardly credible that the directors
expected another “ American syndicate ” to relieve them
ot the claim.
• ,^ e same time, in Sydney, there have sprung
mto life the Junction Extended, of even the whereabouts of
which Broken Hill seems to be a little uncertain. Quota-
tioiis of both North Central and Junction Extended are
confined' to Sydney, Fortunate Sydney—her silver-mine
speculations have always been the rankest takes-iii and
wil' ,reB Bun through the list of the last few years—
White Rock, Lewis Ponds, Tom’s, Mt. Stewart, even
bunny Corner, Phoenix, Lubra, &c. They lost the B.H.
Central a,nd the South, the best things they had, before
these claims emerged from darkness.
The North Central was, it is suspected, floated
off (perhaps one might even say jettisoned) when the
Central was terribly hard up, just as, on an earlier occa-
sion, the Central Blocks was got rid of. It was regis-
tered as a no liability 00., and with such wretchedly
insufficient capital that failure was a foregone conclusion.
But what should the promoters care for the failure of the
pup when the parent was in danger I
Soon a tremendous effort may be made to twist
the northern part of the Broken Hill lode about. The
discovery of the new body in the Junction has opened up
a new field for speculation. This fact may be worth
remembering all along. The North and the Junction
North are nob end-on, but side by side ; the Victoria
Cross and the North are end-on.
The carrion is scented. Adelaide men are
taking up *•’ l niu”79ken Hill. But Broken
Hill looks a« : > ui - stand any boom tricks.
Two mt,. were blown up in the B.H. surface
outs, work at which is probably the most dangerous occu-
pation in Australia.

* » • *
A new and important development has taken
place at Oobar, N.S.W., on an extension of the belt of
country to which the whole of its gold-mines are confined.
Principally they are low-grade, but at this particular spot
something in some respects resembling the' Drysdale has
been discovered. The finders got a small alluvial patch
and found its source to be a body of soft micaceous slate
and felsite, 18in. of which carries wonderfully rich gold.
The first 8 tons went over lOoz. to the ton, and the next
20 tons over 140 z., and the stone they are now raising
seems milch richer than even the last. ' The richest of the
gold is now occurring in little quartz-and-ironstone seams,
and the formation outside for eight or ten feet contains
payable gold.
* * • »
The Mt. Drysdale Co. started its mill last
Saturday. It will probably stop this Saturday, having
only about a week’s water. It is tune it stopped-
“monkeying” and started to work its show with some-
thing like system. The point is-does the formation nut-
side the rich narrow streak in the mine carry payable
gold ? If so, the Mt. Drysdale will probably have a long
div.-life ; if not, its life will be short, and the persons who
paid from £7OO to £BOO each for shares will continue to
curse the mine instead of themselves.

Millions have been subscribed to work Wes-
tralian gold-mines at deep levels. By some miracle it may
all come out right; yet two proverbs, well-known to ex-
perienced Westralian miners, are “Never work below
your armpits,” and “The best gold is what the sun shines
on.” For confirmation vide Wealth of Nations, Bayley’s,
the Plunderderry, and 40 more.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,—" A Golden Shanty ” is paro-
died at Cue, Westralia, where the church is partly built
of quartz blocks donated by claim-holders whose belief
in Providence was apparently greater than their faith in
the British purchasing public. As many of these speci-
mens show shotty gold, the steady church-goer has a
chance to get even on the collection-plate with the point
of his pocket-knife; and should the field ever go bung
the parson can, by dollying up the sacred fabric and
panning it off in holy water, get enough to pay his coach-
fare to the coast. Wonder some sacrilegious fossicker
has not pegged out the gospel-mill as a quartz claim ere
this.—W.W.

W.A.. news is most conflicting. One day
it is wired that the Govt, has a big water-scheme,
the next that the Premier, speaking at Northern said—

not contemplate moving further in that direction until
they had exhausted the possibilities of deep boring and
surface conservation.”
♦ • * •
Ten-head : “It makes me tired to read of the big
and numerous water-schemes for Coolgardie. Sydney D.T.
says there will be a great increase in gold-production
there as soon as the water-famine has been “adequately
relieved.” That water-famine has lasted over three years.
It is about time to think what the place will be when it
does strike a drought. Where, in Heaven’s name, are
they going to get water in a region where there are many
hundreds of miles without a river and “lakes” without
water, a country with a rainfall of a few inches yearly,
and land so spongy as to absorb water as fast as it falls.
A large percentage of even that recovered is lost by
condensation. At sixpence a gallon they manage to get
sufficient for domestic purposes, but a 10-head battery
will use much more water than 1000 men can drink- It
is a gigantic difficulty which cloaks gigantic
mining swindles, but it must be faced. The ocean is the
only certain unfailing supply, and to bring .it in ,wiH» take
years.”

If the papers persistently tip certain shares as
likely to rise in the market there will be a demand for
them and they will rise in the market. Always remem-
ber this when tempted to buy on tip, and don’t forget
that if those particular shares don’t happen to be on tip
just when you are wanting (o sell, you may have reason
to curse the day you were born. Or the day those
mining tipsters were born.

Every mine has its day of reckoning. That of
the Plunderderry has arrived. The old owners, Mills,
Huxley, and party, are calling on the Earl of Fingal for a
declaration that they are entitled to the £30,000 long
ago deposited with the Union Bank, pending some
arrangement in re short acreage. The shares repre-
senting their sixth interest have not yet been released.
The specimens (worth from £15,000 to £20,000, and includ-
ing Big Ben and others “ on the gold knobs of which
you could hang your hat”) having well played their part,
have been returned to Australia. How many of the
people who gazed on them in London ever suspected
that they represented the big end of the mine, or under-
stood that they were still the property of the finders?
And how long will it be before Mr. Bull will take another
mine at £700,000, under like circumstances?

Vic. Secretary for Mines Howitt says there are
still remaining in that province really immense tracts
where alluvial gold exists in “ deep leads,” bub that there
is little probability of the discovery of extensive surface
alluvial Helds. Govt, drills have proved 40 miles of leads
in w'ell-defined auriferous belts on the northern side of
‘.he Dividing Range, while to the south the southerly
fxtension of known leads remain to be proved. Vic.
iculd do with a few more Berrys.
« • « *
Straws point to Vic. in the near future wiping
out, or at least much curtailing, the iniquitous royalty
tax on mining. As an example of how the land-owner
scores, note the £250,000 exacted by the Seven Hills
Estate Freehold Co. from the famous Berry group. As
gold is really the property of the State, it is a gross im-
pertinence for the landowner to demand excessive
royalty, or even royalty at all, although, perhaps, it is
only right liberal compensation should be paid for surface
damage. It is time for legislation.

Melbourne drapers now employ fiends who
canvas for orders from door to door, offering £1 worth of
goods for 2s. down and a shilling a week. This is the most
powerful temptation the wives of the workers have to
combat.
The Mallee Bill, as introduced by the Turner
Government, provided for selection and for terminating
leases. Selection meant ultimate alienation from the State
and the leases meant a limited tenure. The Labor corner
declared it would oppose alienation in every form and
degree. Irvine succeeded in finding a middle course,
lie provided an option between freehold and perpetual-
leasehold. The Tories in the House could conceive of no
rent not rack-rent, and bitterly opposed the proposal.
Irvine was able to enlighten them as to a rent that was
not raised with the improvements effected by the occupier.
He preached to them the old, old story of unimproved
values. The verbiage of the amendment he got into the
Bill is an object-lesson for Parliaments: “The board
shall, in fixing the amount of rent payable in each case,
estimate the value of the land comprised in the perpetual
leasehold as if the same were freehold land not cleared or
cultivated, and without any' fences, buildings, or other
improvements thereon, and shall fix the rent payable at
an amount equal to 3 per centum of such estimated
value.” The House then substituted 2 per cent, for 3 per
cent.

The old Magdala, at Stawell, has turned out a
fortune to its present proprietors. Mrs. Hobbs owns
three-fourths of the whole, and is richer than seme
“ silver kings.” All the quartz-mines around the “ Mag.”
are rank duffers, and of the scores of alluvial ventures in
that district, only one, the John Woods, has turned out a
success.

♦ * ♦ *
The Vic. potato farmers have lost a fortune by
letting their crops rot in the ground. Potatoes are now
worth £5 a ton, and going up. So plentiful were they
that the pigs almost refused to eat them.
« * * ♦
A Chow had fat luck near Bendigo recently.
John was fossicking, and gathered a tub-full of dirt from
about an old stump. When puddled and panned off the
stuff went a hat-full of sovs. to the tub. The coins were
dated about 30 years hack. No rush has set in as the lead
does not promise to extend.

Towers coining to life again. Four promising
mines said to be nearly on it, “It” being the famous
Victory reef. Queen Cross reef raising 3Joz. stone.
♦ ♦ * •
The Angipena gold-mine (S.A.) is alleged by
the new manager to he a salted concern. The deluded
syndicate which floated it consisted of 40 M’s.P., and
they are said to have divided 10,000 gratis shares among
them. If the salting allegations prove true 40 taken-in
M’s.P. will be viewed with grave suspicion by those who
don’t know them, and a lot of hard explanation will be
necessary. The odour of decayed skunk about Angipena
recalls a certain very-long-deserted Maoriland mine which
an enterprising local speculator resurrected and floated
in a certain boom time. The board and the new manager
came up to inspect the property, and the manager was
lowered down the ancient shaft. As he went down he
also met with an odour as if all the dead horse and sewer-
gas in creation were waiting at the bottom, and he
returned to the surface in disorder. Inquiry revealed
the fact that an adjacent town had for two vears tipped—
well, various things down there. Tub Bulletin forgets
exactly what became of that mine ultimately, but its
shares haven’t been quoted for quite a while.

The Hobart whaler, Waterwitch, has returned
from a cruise with over 58 tons oil on board. She started
out, Feb. 22, ’95, “ rising ” her first whale March 24, and
her last Oct. 5. All her “take” was effected in the
vicinity of the S.W. Cape, Tas. It is, as yet, undecided
whether Aliek M‘Gregor will re-commission her. This
old ship was formerly H.M.S. Falcon, and was launched
at Plymouth in 1820. As a whaler she first sailed out of
Sydney under the fl ig of Capt. Chas. Smith, of M'Donald,
Smith and Co., and was bought by a Hobart speculator in
’6O, since when she has remained in active service. She
shows signs of age and wear after her 70 .years of work.
During greater part of her present cruise she was in com-
pany with the Sydney owned Phyllis, which returned
home lately with no “ile.”
* * * *
The Cassels Gold Extracting co.’s draft agree-
ment offered to one set of M.L. mine-owners stipulated
for 7J per cent, royalty on the gross yield, crushed or
cyanided— i.e., the owners might knock 2oz. per ton out
of the stone by battery, and foz. out of the tailings by
cyaniding, and Cassels wanted royalty on the whole
2Joz. ! Cassels also reserved the right to enter, inspect,
and assay at any time ; required that all cyanide of
potassium used should be bought from them ; and
exacted that the owners shouldn’t at any time attack
their patent. If that patent wasn’t declared invalid by a
M.L. Court, the royalty was to be paid irrespective of the
decision of the English Court. The mine-owners decided
that they weren’t taking any, thank you.

George M'Leod, who found the famous Tokatea
mine, Coromandel, still sticks to his pick-handle. Though
he now consists mostly of big beard and big feet, he is by
no means d'>wn. He has a good claim at Castle Rock, one
of the most picturesque peaks on the Peninsula.

• * * *
One of the boom gold-mining companies on the
Coromandel (M.L.) has struck a snag. Its ground was
pegged out in haste without survey, along with several
dozen other leases. Tlien a co. was floated and shares
were rushed to a premium, and were largely dealt in on
the Exchange. Just about this time the Govt, survey
was made ; it was found that dozens of claims overlapped
each other in ail directions, and when matters were
sorted out this particular co. had aljout 12 sq. yds. of
ground—not room enough to sink a shaft. So all the
shareholders want their money back, and there Is much
pandemonium and riot and Kilkenny cat.
* ♦ * *
Geo. Edmonds, original prospector of the
Hauraki, Coromandel, had for his first year’s work the
gorgeous income of £2 10s. When the boss of the score
was out, Geo. used to turn up and get his 50 o’ flour from
Mrs. Storekeeper. When the tide suited he would nip
across the range, clamber out on the old Green Harp
workings, and capture the necessary schnapper. Could
he have raised £3O to pay court fees, the whole mine
would have been his. Hearing he was getting a little
gold, the 00. (English) secured its abandoned ground
and granted tributes. An old chap named Legge (very
hard up) took up one of them. In putting in a level to
get at bis own piece, George had to pass through Legge’g
block. Here he struck ‘‘a reef of solid gold.” Old Legge
had only to haul out the metal to the tune of £12,000.
George had to pass on, but he cleared £2O a week for 12
months out of his own tribute. When the time was up
the co. cheerfully stepped in, and has got out during
the past year £79,912 worth of gold. The stuff at present
runs over 30oz. per ton, at £3 per oz.
• • » »
The real inwardness of Bamako’s great African
Bank is this: Barnato had mining scrip of the nominal
value of perhaps £6,000,000, but in the shaky condition of
the great African bubble an attempt to sell out might
have burst the market, and values would probably have
gone down to half-a-million. To hold on would be equally
fatal, as the burst must come sooner or later. So Bar-
nato made a bank and sold his load of scrip to it at half
its nominal value. Then he sold the bank-shares to the
public at two or three times their face value, and thus
got more for his scrip than even its inflated boom price.
When the mining scrip goes down io something like a
reasonable figure Bamako's Bank should make its share-
holders wish they were dead.

Before the South African boom partially burst
in London, the other day, the market-value of the Rand
mines was run up to £150,000,000, which means, if
the gold-yield for '95 is 50 per cent, belter than '94, a
possible div. of 1| per cent, per annum. The paper value
of Rand mining, land and trust co. shares ran up to
£210,000,000, but when the great drop came £80,000,000
of this paper wealth vanished in about a fortnight.
♦ * * ♦
The celebrated Rand (South Africa) reef extends
for 50 miles, and has been proved for half that distance
not to contain a fault. The quality of the ore is remark-
ably uniform, and Dr. Schmeisser and Mr. Hamilton
Smith concur in estimating the gold to a depth of 1200 ft.
at from £300,000,000 to £350,000,000. The labor at pre-
sent employed is 50,000 natives and 8000 Europeans, and
the 2700 stamps on the ground, aided by cyaniding, can
turn out 200,0000 z. monthly with ‘‘literally no possi-
bility of a disappointment.” There is a theory that the
reef is only the outcrop of a basin connecting with the
reefs of Heidelberg and Klerks-dorp, and that the whole
might pay to work, yielding thousands of millions ; but
this theory is wholly untested, though there is more to
be said for working to a .greater depth than 1200 ft. A
ton of ore yields on the average £2 6s. 6d., to extract
which costs only £1 10s. 6d. Hence over one-ihird of the
gold produced is profit. In '94, with a production of over
2,000,0000 z., worth £7,500,000, the industry yielded, there-
fore, a profit of over £2,500,000, of which £1,580,000 was
paid in divs., the remainder being used for improvements
or development of the properties; this year the output
will be 2,500,0000 z., the profits £3,250,000 ; and the div.
perhaps £2,500,000 ; next year—the increase in the num-
ber of stamps warrants this assumption—there will be an
output of 3,000,0000 z., and £3,500,000 may be paid in div.
* ♦ * •
Dear Bulletin, —Sawdust may yet supersede
metal and gravel in road-making. An intelligent N.S.W.
man, noticing that the timber-waggons never bogged
near the mills, dumped a few loads of sawdust round his
house, mixed it with sand, and in a little time had a firm,
clean, elastic yard. The atiention of the road-super was
drawn to it, and he experimented on a bit of road with
satisfactory results. It binds well, is porous, doesn’t cut
up, and there is no rumbling, jolting or vibration. In
fact, you don’t know what driving is till you’ve been along
a “dusted” road. There are thousands of tons of the
stuff on the coast rivers, and the coastal roads will soon
be paved with it.—Ponte.

• * • •
Latest hotel-brokers’ move for raising the wind.
Broker has a friend running a pub. Latter places it in
the hands of former for sale. He finds a buyer who
deposits, say, £5O. Vendor then refuses to sell, and
buyer demands money back, but agent can’t part, having
disbursed it. Only remedy is to sue vendor, who is
always under bill of sale and penniless. Buyer loses
deposit and law costs, and broker and publican divide
the stakes.

The Neiv Governor’s Reception .
The reception given our new Governor, Viscount
Hampden, was a very slow function compared to
the gorgeous demonstration, a few weeks ago, in
Melbourne. The public lined the streets along
which the procession travelled, but there was an
absence of vociferous cheering, and instead of look-
ing delighted, people mostly seemed woebegone
and, at any rate, listless. Our representative of
Royalty will, however, be pleased to find that he
can obtain at the leading tobacconists in Sydney
those delightful smokes, B. L. Corto Rothschild
cigars, the'same quality and price as in London.
B. L. Corto Rothschilds are obtainable at all
first-class tobacconists or hotels for fid, each.*-

The street welcome given to Sydney’s new
Governor was participated in by a moderate
crowd. There was a fair display of flags ; the
Corporation, or somebody, had tied red handker-
chiefs round some of the telegraph-posts, and the
banks hung the_overdraft out of the window by
way of decoration. The gathering on Circular
consisted mostly of women, and each woman
who had a clean handkerchief waved it. Then
the crowd started off to intercept His Ex. and
have another look at him. A great cloud of dust
rolled up Pitt-street, and running in the midst of
it came small boys, and pickpockets, and dogs,
and women of dubious character with their boots
unlaced, and a cow, all bound for the next cor-
ner. This group was the only one which put on
anything like «- real spurt to see His Ex.

The route selected for thb new Governor’s
triumphal entry into Sydney was about the worst
that could have been chosen—the one least cal-
culated to give him an idea of what Sydney is
really like. His Excellency should have been
taken up Johnson’s Bay in the Annandale ferry-
boat the dirt-coloured one—past the dead dogs
and the gas-inflated sheep which fell overboard
last month, and landed at Stephen -street jetty.
Then the procession should have struggled through
the maze of murky lanes behind the local town-
hall, along Smith-street and Mullins-street to the
abattoirs where the smell of offal is always in the
air, and across the bridge into Pyrmont, thence
along the edge of that Great Dismal Swamp
known as Blackwattle Cove, and down the right-
of-way between the threepenny pub., where the
fight is always going on, and the coal-shed kept by
the man with the wooden ieg - . This would
enable it to take a short cut through the
back way where the stables and the marine store
and the hide-and-skin depot are until it emerged
through M'Ginnis’s side-alley into Union-street.
From there it would n'turally go across the
bridge into that waterside region about the

of the opera by the vice-regal presence ; when the

monarchy not to be “ so confoundedly condescend-

an( l grinned meaningly.

The mayor and councillors of Adelaide
lately interviewed Gov. Buxton, and after
swapping platitudes about their mutual desire to
promote the welfare of the province, liberal re-
freshments were produced and conversation set in.
Under cover of the hum, one councillor remarked
to another that Gov. B. didn’t seem to keep
samples of his own ale on the premises. Un-
fortunately, just then there was one of those
sudden pauses which arise no one knows how,
and the dreadful observation stood out as dis-
tinctly as a naked nigger against a background of
whitewash. The rest of the proceedings were cold
and formal.

* * *
One day last week five women, unassisted
by lawyers, appeared in person in Judge Win-
deyer’s Court to claim divorces, and it was made
a matter of remark that they got their cases suc-
cessfully through with more expedition and
smoothness than the solicitored and barristered
suitors. “Don’t go away with the idea,” said
a court official, “that women are as clever at
other law business as at divorce. The smartest
of these divorce women couldn’t fill up a dog-
license. But once they take divorce in their head
they study the whole thing like a catechism.”
9 * m
In a lecture at Sydney the other day,
poor parson Clarke remarked that under the
administration of the N.S.W. divorce-law, accord-
ing to events that have occurred during the past
few years, the rich man was treated differently
from the poor man. Di vorces, in several instances,
were granted in camera, apparently because the
petitioners were prominent people, and the poor
man was compelled, seemingly because he was
without influence, to find his private life made
public property. The granting of a divorce in
camera, according to a ruling of theChief-Justice,
the lecturer stated, was not permitted by the Act,
yet men who were influential enough obtained
divorce in that way.

Col. Sec. Tozer is remarkably anxious to
prove the Kanaka perfectly clean and inno-
cuous, and has just repeated, in refutation of
Drake, his statement that Q. has only four Kanaka
lepers out of a population of eight thousand.
Drake asserted (with perfect correctness) that the
Kanaka is peculiarly liable to leprosy and skin-
disease, instancing in proof the Sandwich Islands,
on one of which 75 per cent, of the people are
lepers.

An eminent European medical authority
recently wrote a monograph on leprosy—asserting
that a predisposition to that disease only exists in
races such as the Chinese and Hindoos, among
whom tuberculous troubles are rare or unknown.
It may interest students of this question to learn
that Polynesians are peculiarly subject to both
of the above-mentioned diseases.

Dear Bulletin, — Re the Mauritius brown
man whose skin turned white in Adelaide (S.A.)
Hospital, one published advice is—“ Such freaks
of nature are so rare that, so far, only one other
authentic change has been discovered.” Indeed ?
Ho_w comes it, then, that “ leucoderma,” as this
whitening of the cuticle is physiologically called,
was known to the Mosaic dispensation ? Seethe
tokens, &c., of leprosy—Leviticus xiii., 12, 13.
There, leucoderma is evidently distinguished from
all the then so-called leprosies. Yet I have never
noticed that any medical man or medical work
has pointed this out. —O.

THE GREAT SHEEP CONTINENT.
Once, Lady Carington decreed
That just a native Cornstalk weed
Was quite a model flower ;
She put it in her bosomed vest;
Without it she was never “ dressed ; ”
And, from that very hour,
’Twas plucked, and sold, and bought, and shown,
As if’t had specially been grown
Within a hothouse bower.
And poets since have turned their “ feet,”
And orators their phrases neat,
On New South Wales’s “star-like,” sweet
And glorious flannel-flower!
And yet—and yet—and yet, indeed,
It is the same old sorry weed.
The Brasseys and the Hampdens, too,
Belong unto the cycling crew ;
And, following their likes,
With men and women ’tis the rage,
Or stout or slender in the gauge,
To be a guy on bikes !
Thus Fashion tyrannises wit,
The madder and more hideous it,
The worse you see the public bit.
0 ! would but Hampden walk on stilts,
All garbed in loud, short-waisted kilts,
I’ll bet you a bawbee
The population would go mad,
Nor stilts nor kilts could soon be had—
You’d see what you would see
And, oh ! would Brassey don a tail,
And hop upon an “ old man’s ” scale,
Tattoo his face in shapes,
And 101 l his tongue—it seems to me
By tens and hundreds soon we’d see
His imitating apes !
1 knew a man—he was a man I
Who never, never, never ran
Where other people led ;
Who never honored Fashion’s whim ;
I had a real respect for him
Alas ! that man is dead !
The moral makes my flesh to creep :
Can Nationhood awake from sleep
Where even biped males are— SHEEP?
C.

LIMITED,
[Established 1853.]
The N.S.W. Crown Law Office views with horror
the suggestion to borrow a Judge from another
province. The last criminal trial of any import-
ance in which his Honor took part was the Louisa
Collins poisoning-case..
* • *
Loudon Spectator, of Sept. 21, has an
article on “ Professional Secrecy ” which miglit
have been written with a special view to the Dean
case developments. Quoting Dr. Johnson: “To
tell our own secrets is generally folly, but folly
without guilt ; to tell those which are entrusted
to us, is always treachery, and treachery for the
most part combined with folly,’’ the writer
declares that “ men want to feel, not that the
doctor or lawyer will keep secret what he regards
as secret matter, but everything indiscriminately
that comes to him in his professional capacity.
They do not want their adviser to judge whether
the rule of secrecy applies, but to make the con-
sulting-room like the confessional-box —a place
from which no news issues.”
m # #
The weary Speight-Syme libel-suit was
again heard of the other day, when the ex-Com-
rnissioner’s application for some more new trial
was dismissed. The hard-fighting Speight, a
warrior by instinct, is now considering whether he
will go to the Privy Council. If he can find the
means he will almost certainly do so. There have
been 3| years of litigation over the business*and
about .£50,000 of costs, and, save that Speight and
Symo are both older and poorer and worse-tem-
pered, the results are nil.
* » *
A pious Melb. drapery firm is lamenting
the sinful lapse of its respected caretaker, vvho
used to take a Sunday-school class and sing
praises to the Lord. At latest dates the valued
servant had been detected in the indiscretion of
assisting a married woman to rob the store.
The misguided female was wont to visit her friend
the caretaker at night, and combine unhallowed
love with felonious “shaking” of the goods. It
had been observed that ladies of “ill-fame”
were in the habit of conversing with the caretaker
at the door, and he was spoken to on the sub-
ject. And what did he reply, dear friends ? He
replied, with tears in his eyes, that he was
“ striving to turn these unhappy sisters from their
evil ways.”
# * *
An acute conjugal affection was the matter
with the Dacres throughout their married life.
They desired not only to live and die together,
but to “star ” together—emulating the Kendals—
as long as they remained in the theatrical pro-
fession. English engagements for one or other of
them separately were sometimes available, and
they refused the chances offered, preferring to
wait for the successful joint engagement which
seldom came, and shutting their eyes to the fact
that time was discounting their financial value.
Australia seems called upon to excuse itself, in a
measure, for not having been attracted by
the sound dramatic talent of Amy Roselle,
in conjunction with Daore’s uneven ability.
Several circumstances accounted for their
failure to draw the public, but the lady s
buxom figure was perhaps the first. Ex-
cellence in the art of acting doesn’t go for
much with an Australian audience if the per-
former be physically unconvincing. Amy Roseiie
was too matronly a heroine for' them to rave
about; also Arthur Dacre, whom the writer re-
members as a slim, romantic type of beauty-man,
was rather too large to please the multitude.
Husband and wife aged unnecessarily within a
few vears. because they were so often “ resting ”
together instead of acting apart from one another.
Their mutual belief in “ the Dacres” told against
them every way. It kept them idle and weakened
their capacity for rapid study of new parts. Here
was one immediate cause of their melancholy
end. Dacre railed wildly at the Melbourne
Bijou management for changing the bill
every fortnight, without pausing to consider
that a manager’s policy emanates from the
pay-boxes. If a show is making a profit it
doesn’t get withdrawn these times. The Dacres
pieces were invariably run at a loss, therefore, it
may be remarked in parenthesis, they never had
real grounds for complaining of unfair treatment.
At the Bijou they—or rather husband Dacre, who
spoke for self and wife—would have refused to
undertake “ more study ” once a fortnight but for
the manager’s blunt statement that he must
change the bill or close the theatre. Doubtless
more study came very hard on these artists who
had learned so few parts of late years, Amy
Roselle, an actress from her childhood, may not
have found the strain very severe, but Dacre did.
His brain began to give way. Everything worried
him more than ever, especially new parts. He
began to think seriously about suicide, and the
sympathetic Amy shared this along with all his
other thoughts. It needed only a little more
study strain on poor Daore’s mind to bring the
devoted couple to the point where life seems no
longer worth living.
When Dacre was required to master the long
part of Dean Maitland at a short notice he got
his death-warrant. Then his exaggerated sense of
misfortune became insupportable, and that shock-
ing Sunday tragedy came to pass. It is easily
traced to an acute conjugal affection. The Dacres
inordinate mutual love and esteem kept them
from acting apart, and made them amiable crea-
tures though they were in private hfe-almost
“ impossible ” people to deal with professionally.
Long absences from the stage impaired the hus-
band’s power of assimilating words, the worry of
an unusual (for him) amount of study drove him
mad, and “The Silence of Dean Maitland
brought his madness to a crisis. Amy Roselle,
for her part, seems to have accepted cheerfully
the idea of dying at the same time as her husband
under whatever circumstances he might care to
select. # , #

Brisbane is now engaged in enacting Jule
Joubert’s screaming farce (after Mohere), Exhi-
bitors in spite of themselves. Public sentiment
is cold, very cold ; the papers print batches of
names of people who refuse to support the ve
ture ; but Joubert is so energetic, and his bald

so fiercely, that it’s odds he’ll get through yet.

A Maoriland genius
wants all clocks shoved on
two hours by Act of Parlia-
ment, so that the man who
nominally goes to his office
at nine will really get there
by seven, and the man who
arrives at his job at seven
will really be there at five,
and people who get up at
five will, in actual fact, do
it at three, and the hotel
breakfast will be at six a.m.,
and the milk will come round
about 3.30, and everything
will be two hours earlier.
The idea is to violently
shove the people into early-
rising habits, and make
them live by daylight in-
stead of gas. Early rising,
so far, has been a failure
because the average man
wants to have his leisure
after his day’s work is ended,
not before it is begun, but if
everything was put forward
two hours he would rise
early and still have his rest
at the right end of the day.
The Wellington (M. L.)
Philosophical Institute is
thinking over the matter,
and talks of bringing it
before Parliament. •

“ Pinafore ” reminds one
that the Rackstraws, Bob-
stays and Buckets of Aus-
tralia’s Imperial navy don’t

H.M.S. Wallaroo the—haw !

that night-leave has been

may feel quite fresh at 4 a.m.
when they are called from
their hammocks to scrub
decks. Also, it appears that
a couple of other naval com-
manders on the Australian
station are not extravagantly
praised by their subjects.
Instead of singing joyful
choruses to these potentates
the men bemoan their lot in
a minor key, and call upon
the cherub who sits up aloft
to tell ’em whether this ’ere is
a convict-ship or a bulwark
of Hingland’s greatness. All things considered,
The Bulletin would be sorry to hear of the
Russians bombarding Australia just now. If
some of our warships went into action, and two
or three superior officers were carried away by the
first cannon-ball, the jolly tars would refuse to
make any more war upon the bloomin’ foreigners
who had done them such a favor.
AND THEN HE WAS ANGRY.
HE : “So you consider a dog necessary for an escort?
SHE : “ Yes, indeed, Mr. Cycle-Smith. Come along!”
Within the past week various Melb. news-
agents and paper-runners have been interviewed
bv constables and cautioned that if they continue
the sale of certain locally-printed newspapers
they will be prosecuted. This proceeding is so
unlike the usual summary methods of the police
that it becomes plain that the threats in question
are mere “ bluff,” unjustified by the provisions of
any statutory enactment. Under the circum-
stances, and with a view to preventing the estab-
lishment of any sort of a police censorship, iHE
Bulletin trusts that the police action will be
defied. The defeat of the authorities m such an
issue would compel the Puritans to take their
grievance to the proper place, Parliament, where
t could be thrashed out openly.

name given to a portion of U.S. President Monroe s
message to Congress, Dec. 2, 1823. Its essence is that
the U.S. will consider as an unfriendly act any interfer-
ference by European powers with acknowledged American
governments. “ With the existing colonies or dependen-
cies of any European power,” added the President, “ we
have not interfered, and shall not interfere. Since his
day however, the doctrine has been so extended that in
common parlance it means that the U.S. bosses the con-
tinent in a general way, is its arbiter if not its ruler, and
other nations must keep out of the road or they 11 get run

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and-away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone whomak s
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats,
SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Lyceum “ The Carson Case."
HbrMaiesty's “ His Natural Life."
Theatre Royal “Silence of Dean Maitland.”
Tivoli Rickards’ Variety Co.
Sydney Music Hall Variety Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess's Theatre “La Belle Th6r6se.”
Theatre Royal George Rignold’s Co.
Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
Among the Sydney shows, this is a dull,
dead season, when very little happens, and
even that little happens in a listless fashion,
and generally proves, when it does happen,
to be an old thing which happened before
a long time ago. The Criterion and the Opera
House remain closed, which is their usual con-
dition nowadays, and so do most of the small,
moth-eaten establishments which, in better times,
are wont to break out periodically in charge of
some wild-cat company and wrestle with the gas-
bill. There is not a comedy, or an opera, or a
society-play left in Sydney ; the three big theatres
are all grappling with melodrama, and bashing
the skull ot the injured heroine with a sickening
biff against a hard world, and the only difference
is that the Lyceum has a sneerful, cynical villain
in store clothing, and the Royal has a clerical
villain in a white choker, and Her Majesty’s has
a hard-faced villain in oaths and high boots and a
colored shirt. It is an oppressive state of things.
** * *
The Firm withdrew that bright little
comedy, “One of Our Girls,” after one short
week at Sydney Lyceum, and in a wholly un-
accountable fashion put up in its stead the
“ Carson Case.” This latter is a crude American
shriek, which has been localised so far that the
hero is falsely accused in Balmain, and put in
Darlinghurst Gaol, and the sneerful villain insults
a helpless woman in front of the G.P.0., and runs
in disguise through the Domain, and is finally
arrested on the wharf at Woolloomooloo. Other-
wise, there is nothing specially local about it.
For plain, unassuming flatness, which sinks down
restfully like a mass of mud upon the soul, the
“ Carson Case ” ranks very high indeed. A vast
steam-roller has evidently gone over it, and has
reduced it to one solemn dead-level throughout.
It is the same thing which is marked on the map
as the Great African Desert. The plot is sugges-
tive at the beginning of the “ Silver King ”;
there is a weak husband, who is led into drink by
the rival who wanted his wife ,in her pre-marital
days; the rival is in the burglary line in both
cases ; and in both cases the husband recovers
from a drunk to find that a horrid crime—which
the rival really committed, and which is the same
crime in each case—is on his soul, and while he is
away the rival haunts his wife, and does the great
hog-act generally. The rest of the piece is mostly
a reminiscence of the “ Ticket-of-Leave Man.”
The dialogue is a good deal brighter in places
than might be expected, but the utter want of
originality suggests that the plot was purchased
in Mosenstein’s second-hand emporium.
# * #
Elton, who gets up as a striking duplicate
of B. Holt, and Miss Helen Dauvray brighten up
this lamentable affair with an unearthly bright-
ness which is not its own ; and Miss Grace Noble,
who has been driven into unaccustomed ways of
melodrama, makes a pretty and rather pathetic
little wife, but is bowed down by the weight of the
great four-act horrpr on her head. There is too
much of it for a girl of her size to support. King
Hedley is a moderately successful drunken hero;
and Scot Inglis is a nice, highly-polished villain,
in a tall, shiny hat—so highly polished that a lady
could see to do her back-hair in him ; and Miss
Polly Emery occupies a conspicuous Irish situa-
tion in the background with great advan-
t ige; ■ and Miss Nelly Mortyne and Cyril
Keightley both db' very good work; and
some other people are by no means so bad as
some other people. But, despite a strong com-
pany, and fine scenic effects, and the knocking
down of the villain in full view of the post-office
clock, and the arch-fiend escaping in disguise
through the Domain, the best that can be said for
the “ Carson Case ” is that it is a good drama to
go opt in the middle of and have a long rest.
Drama at the Lyceum is evidently a bad spec.,
and even though the management turned on the
new Governor a's an attraction, the first-night
audience was thin and low-spirited.
** * *
“The Silence of Dean Maitland”—the
last straw which broke poor Arthur Dacre—made
its postponed appearance at Sydney Royal on
Saturday. After sitting it out from the first word
to the last in all its profound weepfulness, and
listening to the murky soul-wail of the Dean, and
the ghosts that haunted him, and the voices that
gibbered at him in the night, and the footfalls that
were always arriving at the front-door of his soul,
and the visions that worried him, and the baleful
eyes which insisted on looking at him in his dis-
ordered imagination—after all this the writer
really wouldn’t like to say for certain that Dacre
didn’t act wisely in getting away from it all when
he did. The great fault of the drama is that
almost all the action in the original story is left
out. Everard’s trial, his unjust sentence, and his
20 years’imprisonment are mentioned at second-
hand ; there is only the murder in the prologue,
and thereafter four long acts are filled up with the
worm that dieth not. The Dean sits round
through these four acts and grows old with the
worm for company, and has visions and horrors
and shudders, and sees slimy things in his mind.
His friends mostly die—their deaths are mentioned
by someone in Act ll.—and he and his sister and
his blind son and the worm live together, and do
nothing in particular except wait. There is much
stupendous waiting in the drama, and not one
gleam of anything cheerful, save the agricultural
mirth of three who are having a pot of
beer in the prologue! Finally, a bad man, who
knows the guilty secret, but has kept dark about
it for 17 years for some wholly unexplained reason,
interferes, and the Dean dies a horrible death in
the big cathedral, in front of the stained-glass
window.
* * #
Only a very few people in the long cast
deserve serious notice. Miss Fitzmaurice Gill,
as the Dean’s sister, is bright and convinc-
ing, and plays her part with much force. This
lady has improved remarkably of late, and has
also developed a sudden and striking resemblance
to the stately Geraldine Oliffe. Cathcart plays a
small part in his usual excellent style; Miss
Gwen Dallas makes a rather nice Alma Lee ; and
Johnson Weir as the Dean—who is a good half of
the play in himself—is respectable in a mediocre
fashion, and hardly strikes one really high note all
through. The rest of the co., with one exception
in the person of Mr. Field, and a partial excep-
tion in favor of a certain agricultural grandfather,
is pretty much one dead-level throughout, and in

2.—And now he's “ sorry he spoke.”
some cases it is a distinct hollow. Still, at the
worst, Mr. Lewis Scott’s drama is a gentlemanly
production, and the wail is distinctly high-toned.

Nothing new has happened to “Robbery
Under Arms ” since last week, save that one
night the mail-coach, which Dampier sticks up so
persistently, made a strenuous and unrehearsed
attempt to escape into the orchestra, and carry
its elderly passenger to an unhonored grave among
the ruins of the bassoon. The bushrangers, how-
ever, buried their personal animosities and seized
the front horse unanimously. Business continues
remarkably good, and unless Alfred Woods’s
nigger gives out in the legs under the strain of
continuous bounding, it will run till Friday.
“His Natural Life” follows, and the rasping
sound as Gabbett sharpens his teeth can already
be heard all down Pitt-street.
** * *
At the Tivoli, on Saturday night, Miss
Alice Leamar received a soul-stirring reception
from the biggest crowd which could be inserted
into that building. The lady looks as young and
fluffy and golden-haired as she was some years
ago, and she still casts herself head-first into her
business with a frenzied energy which appeals to
the great soul of the gallery. It was when she
appeared in the rational dress of the advanced
bicycle female, however, that the enthusiasm
reached its highest point —evidently the gallery
prefers its Alice in pants, though it also views her
with high approval in skirts and a big hat. The
enthusiasm went as far as six consecutive encores,
and a dreadfully exhausted goddess was the natu-
ral result.
** * *
A good business is still doing at the Sydney
Music Hall, and sundry new artists turn up each
week to lend variety to the bill. The harmless,
necessary corner-man gets some new ideas now
and then, and aims quaint conundrums at the man
in the centre, and some good singers and dancers
brighten up the proceedings, but nothing very
dreadful happens. Possibly, considering the
pressure of competition just now, the manage-
ment would do well to get hold of something
dreadful, and make it happen at once, for the
satisfaction of the ghoulsome public. Miss Amy
Gourlay and her brother Bill have joined the co.,
and make it a good deal stronger than it was
before, and nothing in the programme calls for
particular execration at present.
** * *
“ The Yeomen of the Guard " revival, at
Melb. Princess’s, is about as many degrees above
the W. and M. Opera Go’s average success as the
recent “ Pinafore ” performance was below the
standard. Perhaps it is too late in the day to
convince Australia that “ The Yeomen ” is Gilbert
and Sullivan’s masterpiece, for hitherto thecharm
and pathos of the libretto have not been brought
home to the public soul. Now, for the first time,
this serio-comic opera, containing no extravagant
topsy-turveydom, topical satire, or sunshine,
seems to reveal the collaborateurs at their best.
The performance isn’t perfect, but much of it is
unusually admirable and the balance quite good
enough. If all Melbourne were to pray for a
better show, on a Sunday set apart for the pur-
pose, the chances are that Providence would con-
sider the petition unnecessary and ignore it
accordingly.
* * *
Howard Vernon, as the gruesome assistant
tormentor, puts a little more depth, here and
there, into his original, fine, broad effects, some
of which, perhaps, would be finer if he narrowed
them down. Miss Graupner, whose voice pro-
mises to recover its lost form, sings more cleverly
and acts the part of Elsie Maynard rather better
than of old. The same remarks, multiplied by
two, apply to Miss Ida Osborne. Her character
(sister Phcebe) is full of acting opportunities, and
she is now a distinctly brighter and more
capable artiste than when she formerly tackled
her task-in a somewhat perky spirit. George
Lauri, too, has developed the love-lorn jester
into an object of general sympathy, and the heart
of the man in motley breaks with an impressive
snap at the finish. Lissant is a well-spoken,
natural Sergeant Meryll, Miss Cameron is always
an effective Dame, and Miss May Pollard fills the
little part of the Dame’s daughter in a valuable
manner. Also, the First Yeoman does great
credit to the chorus «■’ en "ailed upon to step forth
and sing a solo. ’ Tw str . ingly-fresh features of
the performance are W.o.ace Brownlowand Cour-
tice Pounds. The baritone is chiefly required to
talk historically and flaunt fine clothes as a
medieval Governor of the Tower should. Brown-
low does these things with commendable earnest-
ness.
* * -■< *
Courtice Pounds appears as the hero,
Fairfax, and except that he is more plump and
cheerful than one would expect to find an amateur
alchemist, the new tenor deserves enthusiastic
praise. He gives the full meaning to his lines,
puts a sharp point to the occasional jokes, sings
spiritedly, and makes love like a man. Tenors, as
a rule, go courting in a sentimental ballad bass.
Take him altogether, Courtice Pounds represents
fifty per cent, of the improvement in this per-
formance of “The Yeomen’’over all that have
gone before. Unfortunately, the revival is for
one week only.

On Saturday, a new comedy-opera, “La
Belle Therese,” will be produced at the Prin-
cess’s. It is, as its name indicates, very Frenchy,
and comes with a large reputation from its native
gay Paree.

George Rignold, up to time of writing, had
resisted all temptations to prolong his season at
Melb. Royal, so, unless Lord Brassey or some-

another spectacle, George is likely to make a fare-
well appearance in “Youth” this week. Last
Saturday there was a distribution of souvenirs
among a crowded audience, and a widow, who
had purchased a shilling’s-worth of pit, had the
felicity of winning a handsome gold watch with
her ticket.

The arrival of John Coleman at the Opera
House on Saturday was found to be a cause for
considerable joy. The comedian from England
caught on.' Also two other new-comers, indigen-
ous to the soil, knocked one another about with
success, and the bear proprietor worried his poor
unassuming beasts in a way pleasing to some
people. Talking of cruelty to animals—-mesmerist
Kennedy is concluding a not very popular season
in Melbourne. The cataleptic person will remain
in his glass case (unless the police do their duty)
until Saturday, and Kennedy invites the lower
orders of humanity to come along in the meantime
and see what is “ practically a corpse.” The
Gaiety still struggles bravely on with a variety
show,

Letter from New Guinea:—“We saw
three or four hundred fighting men, going up in
canoes, evidently on the war-path. The Governor,
with all his force, started after them up the river
but was too late. He met them coming back with
canoes loaded with spoil. By the amount of
human flesh aboard they must have wiped a
village clean out. They had men, women, and
children all roasted ; one little girl was tied to a
pole the same as they do with pigs. The Govern-
ment men fired at them, killed three, wounded
others, and broke up the party.”
Ex-Railway Commissioner Speight grinds
his teeth over one passage in the judgment of the
Vic. Full Court, dismissing his application for
another go at the Age. He was informed that
he might perhaps have done well in his libel-
actions by contending that some of the Age
statements libelled him in his private, rather than
his public, capacity—but the point wasn't raised.
Then the judgment, having pointed out the chance
he had missed, refused him a new trial.
Ed. Bulletin, —Let me straighten up
your facts in the matter of that writ which you
say I served on Gov. Darling. In the “Darling
crisis”’nearly 30 years ago, W. J. Gerard sued
the Crown, under the “Crown Remedies Act,”
for the return of duties which he alleged to be
wrongfully paid. He obtained a verdict for the
amount, and his solicitor, T. J. Wyburn, received
the piothonotary’s certificate for payment. One
Richard M'Millan, by direction of Mr. Wyburn,
went to Sir Charles Darling at the Treasury on
the advice of the late Mr. Fellows, and asked for
payment. This was a cause of annoyance to the
Governor, and was commented on at the time by
the liberal press. I was about the same time pro-
fessionally employed as a solicitor in an intended
application to the Supreme Court by Messrs. R.
D. Ireland, Fellows and Ward, against the then
Treasurer, Attorney-General, and manager of the
London Chartered Bank, for subverting the Audit
Act in confessing judgment and obtaining the
Governor’s authority for payment. This applica-
tion, through disagreement amongst the counsel,
was abandoned on the morning it was to be heard.
In-after years, without any justification, these two
matters became somehow jumbled in the public
mind, and I was charged with serving a writ on
the Governor. No writ was ever served on th-
Governor by me, or by anyone else.—George
Godfrey, Melb.

Ass is tbe lately-formed Taxpayers’ Association,

The chief business of N.S.W. Labor-party
in Parliament seems to be to roar loudly against,
and vote solidly for, tbe Reid Government. Reid
smiles back fatly.
Queensland’s departed Governor, Norman,
says that “ the feeling of Australian loyalty to
England is so general that there is practically no
other side to the question.” Pooh! Indifference
is not loyalty.
In a full House, the S.A. Leg. Council
defeated by only 12 votes to 11 a proposition tabled

open up negotiations with Great Britain for the
purpose of getting rid of the Northern Territory.
Twelve votes to eleven !
Talk of Vic. Government’s intention of
pensioning off Speaker Berry calls forth such
vehement protestations of innocence from the
Ministry as to suggest the galled jade. _ Yet
Berry deserves a pension, not for what he is, bub
for what he was.
Jack Hamilton’s rascally motion to make
the life of Q. Parliament five years, instead of
three, naturally meets with the warm approval
of the Boodle wraith. Anything which puts elec-
tion-day further off, which makes Parliament a
more irresponsible body, and gives freer play for
Boodlewraith manoeuvres, is bound to have the
warm approval of Q. Govt.

Federation when she placed this continent by
itself apart from the rest of the world and left
its interior entirely without natural boundaries.
< You are here by yourselves,’ she said, ‘ and you
have to look after yourselves and stand by your-
selves as one. country ; everywhere with common
objects, common sympathies, common conditions
of life.’ Nature’s own voice bids us federate.” —
j. \y. M'Cay, M.P. for Castlemaine (Vic.).

Schey’s Bill providing for the amalgama-
tion of the two branches of the legal profession, so
that a suitor can tell his tale of Woe directly to his
advocate, instead of hiring another lawyer to
explain it to him, and paying both of them, and
further providing that a barrister must do the
work he is hired for or else return his fee, has got
through its second reading in N.S.W. Assembly.
But already a strange, gritty sound can be heard
in the air as the sharks of the Upper House
sharpen their teeth on the grindstone.
“ General ” Booth, under the patronage of
Horace Tozer, has been touring Queensland in
connection with his pauper-colonisation schemes.
The General professes to have his eye on bouth
Africa as the most acceptable dumping-ground,
but he’s touring Bananaland all the same. Africa
will undoubtedly suit him best; no turbulent,
jealous democracy ; no anti-alien agitation ; only
a happy hunting-ground for the syndicate and a
pasture-ground for its obedient slave the pauper
immigrant.
New South Wales once had a 40 years’
experience of an undivided legal profession, and
the cry which led to the severance had just as
much of the public voice in it as has that which
now seeks amalgamation. In 1829, when the
division into barristers and solicitors took place,
those then practising were allowed by the Supreme
Court to choose the'braneh they considered suited
to their tastes, and so the rift was inaugurated.
The public of ’29 believed the division would
cheapen law; now the public of ’95 appear to
think amalgamation will do it.
Q. Govt, excels in throwing apples of dis-
cord. The proposed extension of the Northern
railway from Hughenden to Winton—from
Northern into Central Territory—has badly
damaged the cordial understanding desirable
between Central and Northern Separatiomsts ;
and the batch of bribe-railways—ls in all—dangled
before the country in view of the coming elections,
has utterly demoralised the Labor-party. That
party generally votes solid, but in the bribe-rail-
way" division its members Were heads and tails,
“ Ayes ” and “ Noes,” all over the lobbies. Even
a Labor member’s principles melt in the glare of
the eye of the enraged constituent who wants a
railway “ agin his own door.”
N.S.W. Lands Minister Carruthers is pro-
moting a bill to reduce the instalments payable by
selectors from Is. to 6d. per acre per annum, and
also to reduce the interest on unpaid instalments
from 5 to 4 per cent. This means a loss to the
revenue of .£lOO,OOO a year, but it will also mean
a lot of relief to many struggling selectors—like-
wise many struggling dummies. At the same
time, it isn’t entirely the 5 per cent, instalments
to Govt, which oppress the struggling selector;
the 10 per cent, interest on his too-frequent mort-
gage has often a lot to do with the matter, and a
State Land Bank would be quite as much to the
point as a reduction of instalments. Moreover,
how does this subsidising of the agricultural
industry by .£lOO,OOO a year accord with the great
and beautiful Freetrade policy ?
Writes “A Cudgen Kanaka” to Tweed
(N.S.W.) Herald of Nov. 8
The majority of white men of this district run down the
colored men on account of wages. Let me inform you,
Mr. Editor, that the Kanakas all stick for high wages.
Almost all of us at Cudgen receive higher wages than the
white men employed there, in fact we would not work for
the pittance some white people of Cudgen receive . . .
The Kanakas in this district have always kept up wages,
and that is the great thing for all working-men to do.
You of course, remember the Kanaka strike at Oudgen,
two or three years ago, when the “boys” struck for
higher wages. That strike was not a success, because a
number of white men took the work at a great deal less
than the Kanakas.
This should help to keep the white Australian in
his proper place —the Kanaka s cheap laborer !

It needed a war of independence to con-
vince the United States of the economic utility of
Protectionism; and it will probably require a
movement for Independent Nationality to apprise
Australia of the full value and emergent sufficiency
of Protectionism. Major-General Hutton s reqm-
sition for a N.S.W. small-arms, ammunition and
powder factory is one of the signs of the times.
Another is that even Kanakaland proposes to pro-
foSS have constructed within its own
borders 40 new locomotives on which it has set its
heart. The first requisite of an independent
nation is that it must be able to be entirely self-
sunplying in the time of war; self-supplying in
arms, ammunition, food of all kinds (else a
blockade will starve it out) railway material,
ships, clothing, boots—for a naked and shoeless
army is next to useless ; and many other things.
And a time of war may be any time while the
English connection continues.

Last year, the Turner (Vic.)
Government endeavored to pass a
Bill providing that the unfinished
business of one session shall he
continued in the next at the point
where it left off, instead of being
begun all over again. The Coun-
cil dropped this valuable measure
under the table, but another at-
tempt is to be made to pass it.
The present system has absolutely
nothing to recommend it. If a
private co. burnt all its unfinished
work at the end of the year and
started everything afresh on Ist
of Jan., or amanufacturersmashed
all his unfinished products regu-
larly on 31st of Dec., or an author
burned all his uncompleted MS.
on the last day of the year, or if
all uncompleted, buildings had to
be pulled down bn the last day of
the year and started afresh, simply
as a matter of precedent, it is just
possible that the whole vast stu-
pidity of the business would begin
to become apparent. -

At Cathkin station, Alexandra, Yic., a
sheep which, had not been fleeced for two years*
and whose Wool was therefore long, was being
shorn, when a big tiger-snake suddenly crawled
out of the fleece on to the board. Several half-
shorn sheep got away, one man cut his hand badly
with the shears, and most got a severe fright.
Wise is the race of Cohen. The Saltwater
River (Melb.), haunted of anglers, has lately been
horribly stenchful to fishers, and particularly
deadly to fish. The anglers complained and a
Stock department official went to enquire. He
found that on the banks of the Saltwater are the
pig-farms whence Melb. Christians derive the raw
material of their pork, ham and bacon; that the
pigs were fed on slaughterhouse offal, mostly
putrid and extensively maggot-filled ; that their
drinking-water is that of that awful “ noxious-
trade ” river ; that at the shore of their paddocks
the pigs had converted the banks into a filthy
wallow; and that stomach-ached pigs chose this
gluepot for suicide. He saw also that many of
these pig-farm swine looked “ very peculiar.’
Slain examples showed spleen and liver encased in
dropsical inflammatory cysts ; as for their lungs—
well, even the hardened stock-inspector stuck at
describing them. Thus prophesied Isaiah about
2500 years ago, of the Melbournites “ which eat
swine’s flesh, and broth of abominable things is
in their vessels.”
Zimmerman, called by Americans “ th e
Speed King,” and by Frenchmen “the Flying
Man ” says that one of his severest races was the
Springfield (Mass.) Handicap. Then he won the
dB2OO first prize by two or three lengths from
Windle, Tyler, Sarger, and Johnson. At Clifton,
Jersey, in ’93, Zim., giving Banker and Wheeler
25 yards in a mile handicap, and other of 68
starters up to 150 yards, won in 2min. 19sec.
Last year in Paris he won the mile handicap from
scratch; there were 28 starters, and Zim. had to
ride at his top the whole way on the outside. But
he won by eight yards in 2min. 9seo. In his races
he has generally made the pace throughout the
last quarter mile, while rivals tried in vain to
hang on; he has come in 10, 20, 40 yards
ahead of the best in that sprint. English cyclists
called Zim. a sprinter, and said he could not last
any distance worth mentioning. Zim. took up
that gauntlet, and won the five and forty-mile
championships from them. Things in cycling
England were quieter for him after that, and
quieter still after he had won 100 races in one
season there. In driving his machine, Zim. and
the machine are one; not an ounce of power is
lost, so precise are Zim.’s movements, and his
path is as straight as the arrow’s flight. He
claims to have considerably revolutionised cycle
construction, and says that his success is due to
the virtues of his machine. Cycle-riders every-
where have adopted Zim.’s innovations, and in
Australia they are shifting their saddles forward
h la Zim.
The Nimrod-Zimmy triplet was brought
from America by the speedy Zimmerman to teach
Australian cyclists the science of pacing, and to
aid them in lowering all previous Australian cycle
records. A triplet first came on American tracks
two years ago, and Zim. has been paced hy one
there over a hundred times. He has raced behind
Photo. by Kerry.
ZIMMERMAN , Champion Cyclist.
this particular one some half-dozen times. Zim.
says that for 100 yards neither triplet nor tandem
can go faster than a single, but the tandem, with
its two riders, can last at top speed twice as far
as a single, and a triplet three or four times
further. Hence the triplet’s value as a pacer, and
its position, with the u quad, y as the favorite
American pace-maker. !
Mahomet, the talking stallion of Fitz-
gerald Bros.’ show, though tractable now as a
well-trained child, was at two years old an out-
law. He was (so goes the story) found in his
stable pawing the senseless body of his groom.
Mahomet was driven back with a pitchfork, but
the groom died shortly. Two months afterwards
(continues the story) Probasoo, who was born
within fifty feet of a stable-door and had taught
many horses circus work, went into Mahomet s
stall and had a desperate tussle. Probasco was
bitten on the hand, ear, chest, and back ; and one
of his teeth was knocked out by the horse striking
him —Mahomet meant that blow to settle Pro-
basco. But Probasco got Mahomet down and
was master. Eighteen months later saw Mahomet
winning first prize at Chicago for dancing and
rope-skipping. At the end of three years he could
unerringly perform the calculating trick. Now he
seems to have forgotten he is a horse at all he
never whinnies to other horses. He lives on oats,
bran-mash, and hay, and gets three miles walking
exercise per day. He hasn t had a morning
rehearsal for two years past. He has shown all
over Europe, America, and India, and he loves
arge audiences.

HIS SPECIALITY.
SWAGGIE : “ You see, Old Dogleg wanted me to work outside my speciality,

Prince Luigi, when in Melb., was much
smitten with the charms of Grace Palotta, and
constantly exchanged ogles from the box with the
charming Austrian on the stage. Ib was perhaps
fortunate that Grace was old enough to be the
Prince’s mother, and sufficiently experienced to be
his grandmother.
Melbourne lost a useful city councillor,
the other day, in old Johnny Walker. There
were no gaudy points about Johnny, and he
didn’t make speeches except under strong com-
pulsion. He was just a level-headed, fairly
honest ex-contractor, compared with whom the
average, flatulent alderman is a highly objection-
able ass.
W. P. Reeves, Minister for Education and
Labor (M.L.), is almost booked as the new
Agent-General. He is a journalist, a very fair
poet, a socialist, has a pretty wife, and a good
deal of money. The Tories and weak-kneed
Liberals have had their knives into him for a long
time. He called ’em “Social pests” long ago,
and they don’t forget it.
Imagination depicts “General” Booth
tall and commander-like; vision sees him ordinary-
sized, vulture-beaked, with deepset eyes and almost
vulgar. Out of the first score in any chance pass-
ing crowd, sight would select at least three as
more promising than Booth in the husk. Also,
one might easily mistake him for “ an Israelite,
indeed,” but scarcely for one “in whom there is
no guile.”
Mr. P. N. RUSSELL, donor of £50,000 endowment for
Sydney University Engineering-school.
Mr. P. N. Russell, founder of the old-
time Sydney engineering firm of P. N. Russell
and 00. (of whom an excellent portrait is here
printed), has munificently given the sum of
£50,000 as an endowment for the engineering
school at Sydney University. Mr. Russell has
long been resident in England, whither he retired
after the memorable dispute with his workmen
which resulted in his large engineering works
being shut up for ever at a few days’ notice.
Sydney University has now at its disposal for
special teaching purposes no less than £280,000,
but Mr. Russell, in making his splendid donation,
has expressed a hope that the gift will in po,way
prejudice the University’s claims to a continued
liberal State subsidy.
Sydney’s teetotal Mayor, Sam Lees,
M.L.C., on '‘General” Booth (in effect) : “I
revere and honor the man whom God has placed
in such an honorable position.’’ And now will
Sam Lees please tell why he revered and honored
the visiting “ Gaiety Girls ? ” A nose or a leg, it’s
all the same to his Sunday-school Worship, so
long as it be voluptuously rounded.
Gov. Brassey attended his first Masonic
shivoo in M«lb. last week. He was “made”
when a boy, at Oxford, 40 years ago, but has not
taken an active part in it since, until Albert
Edward made him a Grand Warden or something
prior to his departure from England, with a view
to his becoming a Worshipful Master and other-
wise taking a prominent place in Yio. Masonic
antics.
Coroners, like Judges, consider it the
dignified thing not to know anything which isn’t
formally brought out in evidence. When the
Daore inquest was about halt through Mr. Woore,
the Sydney corpse-investigator, had a sudden in-
spiration, and stopped the proceedings to inquire
if the deceased lady was an actress. Hearing that
she was, a great doubt was lifted from his mind
and the inquiry went on.
What caused the feeling in the recent row
about Vic. military precedence was the fact that
Templeton turned up at the headquarters office,
and, to prove his authority, commanded the
alleged interloper, Col. Hoad, to go down town
and buy a packet of tacks, a toothpick, a Gatling
gun, or some other weapon of warfare. And
Hood had to go or be shot, and when he came
back “ the band began to play.”
Treas. Ward’s libel-action against Wel-
lington Evening Post, settled last week by
the complete cave -in of the paper, was in
its subject-matter identical with the charge
brought by M‘Culloch against Melb. Akgus in
the Hugh George days. In both cases the news-
papers ascribed Parliamentary unveracity—a sort
of accusation about which politicians are not now
so thin-skinned as they once were—but whereas
Ward appealed to the law, M‘Oulloch had up
George for contempt' of Parliament, and actually
imprisoned him in the House for about three
weeks.
Dear Bulletin,—Re Huxley’s courtship.
T. P. O’Connor is not quite correct in his
details. Mv father, Charles Mark Palmer (for
the last 35 years of the Bank of N.S. Wales, and
for years its secretary) was, in 1850, a lieutenant
in H.M.S. Meander on this station. He ex-
changed into the Rattlesnake in which was
Assistant-Surgeon (afterwards Professor) Huxley.
My father joined the Rattlesnake here in Sydney
and sailed direct to England in her. His recol-
lection of Huxley is accentuated by the fact that
Huxley was the only man who ever pulled a tooth
out of his head.—Yours, W. H. Palmer, Sydney,
22nd Nov., ’95.

The fame and fortune meaner art may reap.
And her husband, Arthur Dacre.
Each loved the other well ;
In the same dark hour they died.

long life, his pulse in-
variably beating with
v hat was respectable
and influential. Pro-
bably the 20 years he

ago precursor of the
current Northern Terri-

cate —gave his mind the

Gov. Hampden hasn’t yet said that he will
stay in Australia for the full gubernatorial term.
Gov. Duff said so on the day he landed, and kept
repeating the statement almost till he died. Jersey
also gave it to be understood that five or seven
years would stand to his credit as Governor, but,
like poor Gov. Duff, he disappeared in about two.
Gov. Macquarie, who said he intended to stop
only twelve months, stayed longest of all—eleven
years.

able through the tell-tale telephone. A day

got tangled when he was speaking and published
interesting patches of Govt, policy. Quoth the
’phone: “Burr-r-rr! Yes. I shall be aboard the
Premier, and the other Ministers on the Burr-
rr.r_pop i Of course I shall bring his lordship
ashore with me.” Then : “ For God’s sake, ring
off, Georgy. You’ll choke.”

written when he was awaiting at Naples instruc-
tions to sail for Melbourne, were quite painfully
high-pitched. “My God ! this suspense is ter-
rible !” he would write. Dacre looked at life
through a magnifying-glass. A medium-sized
misfortune was a gigantic calamity, a serious loss
was an overwhelming disaster, a man who crossed
him in business was an unrelenting foe. On the
other hand, a kindly acquaintance was a dear
friend, a pleasant experience a joy unutterable,
and a talented wife the most wonderful woman
under God’s heaven. Nobody spoice harshly of
Dacre, not even the people he came to loggerheads
with. The cold-blooded world has a sneaking
regard for enthusiasm.


How to Dress.
There is a great diversity of opinion on this im-
portant subject, and even such philosophers as the
great Carlyle have not deemed it unworthy of
their attention. Perhaps the suggestion that
when a lady is really well-dressed nobody notices
the details of her attire, is truest of all. In this
case absolute harmony of coloring and perfection
of fit must have been achieved. Messrs. Manning
and Co., of Collins-street, are now the most popu-
lar firm in Melbourne in this special department.
Their designers are the best artistes procurable,
and their materials the choicest. Hence the repu-
tation they have deservedly won in the Southern
city for dress and mantle making, millinery and
whitework, &o. Moderation in cost is also one of
the trade ethics of this firm. Costumes, for
example, are made (complete) in the best style
from Two Guineas. Their specialties in millinery,
beautifplly cut mantles, capes and blouses excite
much local admiration, and are now being sent to
all parts of Australia. Samples, estimates and
instructions for self-measurement will be gladly
sent anywhere on application. Orders to the
value of £5O and upwards are sent carriage free
and duty paid to any Australasian colony ; also
a discount of 2A per cent, is allowed for cash.
The address : —Manning and Co., The Block,
Collins-street, Melbourne.*
A PUZZLE.
Readers of The Bulletin in the country are often
puzzled to know where to send their orders, so
many drapery linns are advertising what they can
do, but few care much how orders are executed.
There seems to be a general idea that “anything
will do for the bush,” all forgetful of the fact that
ladies, even in the remotest backblocks, like to
have the latest styles and the freshest of goods.
Messrs. Riley Brothers, of 570 George-st., Sydney,
have for the past 30 years given special attention
to their Country Order Department, and know
intimately the requirements of every part of the
colony—North, South, or West. If the lady
readers of The Bulletin will look at this corner
of the paper every week they will see what we are
sending away every day. Gentlemen will find
articles for them in the corner of another page.
