Sydney,
~ November 26, 1895.
My Dear Moorabinda—
The landing of Gov. Hampden last Thursday
naturally drew a big crowd, for Australians are
addicted to circuses, especially with lions—and
the V.R, cortege was all that. Besides, it was a
new departure to trail a live Gov. round the town
—officialdom usually reserves that sort of thing
until H.E. is dead, and can’t see how many offices
are to be let and how many banks are liquidating
up the side-streets. But the rule was broken for
Gov. Hampden, who was taken anything but a
short-cut to Guvmentouse, through layer-on-layer
of goggle-eyed, self-possessed sightseers, backed
by bunting and horsebed ferns at various spots en
route. I thought there was more tail than head
about the procession itself—all the interest cen-
tred in the new Governor’s barouche. In that
sacred vehicle—drawing attention from His Ex.
himself—was the most glaringly-dressed youth I
ever saw outside a circus. He was cherry-ripe
from head to heel with a criss-cross pattern of
gold lace, and his hat culminated in a wild whoop
of red feathers, suggestive of an Australian grass-
tree on fire. He really looked as if he had dressed
himself to frighten the blacks, on the debbil-debbil
principle.
Gov. Hampden struck me as being a somewhat
larky potentate. His smile is rather reminiscent
of the vacuous smirk of—well, a very well-adver-
tised person, still more or less before the public—
and he is a medium-sized, lightly-timbered
Governor, with a moustache like a well-kept
hedge and side-boards of grizzled hair. He smiled
perpetually as he passed along, and appeared to
take stock of the women on the balconies with the
eye of an expert. The best introduction Gov.
H ampden could have had was that Melb. Herald
print of him. After that, the original—who is
just an ordinary sort of plain man—comes quite
as a relief.
Lady Hampden accentuated her striking profile
by wearing her very fair hair crimped in bandeaux
—a style which is not intended for a face like Lady
Hampden’s. She should wear her hair piled high
over her forehead, unless she wants to make her
hereditary nose look like a bowsprit. Her Ex.
wore a black corded silk dress with a small black
bonnet trimmed with pink roses, and her lap was
covered with a huge bouquet of actinotus. Lady
Hampden, who bowed and smiled in the custom-
ary manner and looked about her in a politely
interested way, seems to be a very capable person-
age, who probably has a fad or two concealed
about her person which she would like to practise
on somebody, and who will not let the Guvment-
ouse clique over-manage her. The three vice-regal
girls wore simple white dresses with picturesquely
floral hats for the landing, and looked somewhat
out of countenance— owing to having been so stared
at, I suppose. They all appeared to droop under
the large eye of the public. A great many flowers
were left at Guvmentouse for the vice-regal ladies,
the tributes ranging from a huge basket of actino-
tus, tied with pale-blue ribbons, to a hurried-
looking bunch of Xmas-bush tied with strings.
But whether they came from society or the
florists’, the main idea was the same. All the
donors were after an ad.
There was a great deal of tail to the vice-regal
procession. Gazing on Sydney’s public men en
masse moving along in hired carriages—some of
which were obviously mourning-coaches split open
and trying to look festive—l couldn’t help think
ing what a pity it was that some great men don't)
keep good-looking and well-built understudies to
go out in purple and gold and tights and the rest
of it when duty calls’em to a circus-parade “by
the full strength of the company.” And if they
will star themselves, they might at least rehearse
their parts. I saw some of the scarlet-gowned
Judges—like so many Katishas—getting out of
their carriages with thejudicial petticoatinafright-
ful muddle. They should borrow a skirt from some
of their lady friends and put in some practice
beforehand if they can’t make a more elegant
descent than that. One often hears of limbs of
the law, but on these formal occasions one would
rather not see quite so much of them.
The military certainly made a great show in the
procession, and if “Uncle” Hutton has done
nothing else he has certainly polished up his
army until it is really as bright as a button. If
we must have this sort of pretoy paper frill about
our peaceful Australian civilisation, it’s just as
well to have it clean and combed-up, and I only
hope when the General goes in March that there
won’t be a great sartorial backsliding. On dit
that Colonel Spalding is the politically anointed
to take Major-General Hutton’s post as Com-
mandant.
I hear that Gov. Hampden did not make him-
self severely popular on the voyage out. He was
amiable to all sorts, and he played ship’s quoits
and billiards, losing more games than he won, but
he did not obtrude himself, and impressed his
fellow passengers as a personage who longed to go
through life in carpet slippers. When he was
invited ashore at Adelaide he meekly remon-
strated against the bother of the whole thing, but
he went, all the same, which is a point in his favor.
He also retired to bed at a very early hour all
through the voyage, though Miss Alice Leamar
came out by the same tub, and used to give ’em
“ Whackey Whack ” in the small hours. Now,
Gov. Hampden looks like a man who wouldn’t
miss “ Whackey Whack ” on any account, if left
to himself, and his early retirement, under the
circumstances, to his cradle on the deep looks as
if Lady Hampden’s fad is Home Rule, don’t you
think? It was not a festive trip over—except for
the stewards. Of the .£7O subscribed for amuse-
ments only half was spent, and the rest divided
among the knights of the basin. The Gov.
didn’t have an enlivening effect, evidently, and it
was a great disappointment when he turned up at
the fancy-dress ball in evemng-dress.
The engagement is announced of Miss Maud
Roberts—one of Sydney's prettiest girls—to Mr.
Stanley Mylins, an English artist of repute. The
is arr.anged for February, after which
the principal parties m it will leave for England
and a tour round the world. Miss Roberts has
quite recovered from the severe horse-accident she
met with a few months ago.
Gov. Hampden’s first visit to Sydney Lyceum
led to a great rush of good-looking decorations to
the front—all four stage-boxes being draped in the
Union Jack and adorned with potted plants tied
up in scarlet as if they had a toothache ; while
the profusion of bouquets on both sides of the
house looked as if the management expected a
boarding-school. In the centre of the do. a thing
like a huge green caterpillar and more Union Jack
testified to the joy of the occasion, and the
Lyceum marble stairway blossomed like Aaron’s
rod in honor of H. 8., who arrived with punctu-
ality. It was a disappointment to the gaping
brigade that the V.R. party weren’t set out like
prize shorthorns in the middle of the. d.c. ; but
Gov. Hampden admirably did his part (as a per-
sonally-conducted show) —changing his seat several
times to give all the opera-glasses a chance, and
walking round the circle ’tween acts to visit
ex-Actmg-Gov. Darley, who squired a covey of
daughters, one of whom, in Japanese mourning,
looked as if she deplored the family’s fall from
grace. Seen at short range, Gov. Hampden is a
tall, slight personage with an indolent style and a
graceful manner of walking. He is quite bald
enough to justify his position in the front row on
all occasions, and he has a most elegant way of
scratching himself behind the ear with his little
finger. He wore evening-dress with no bouton-
niere—-a tip for the johnnies—and carried.his white
gloves in his hand. Both he and Lady H. use
pince-nez when struggling with print.
Lady Hampden, who appeared to be somewhat
disconcerted by the owl-like opera-glasses of a
very third-rate d.c.—her Ex. isn’t half as cool
under a fire of eyes as the Gov.— wore a grand-
motherly gown of black silk made high and with
long sleeves, and what really looked like a cap on
her head. This will fetch all the matrons out in
head-dresses, no doubt, though the Sydney house-
mother is not addicted to capping herself, no
matter how broad her parting is - perhaps because
she looks so cooky-tied that way? Her Ex.
tried to hide behind her programme and her
bouquet to get away from the battery of eyes, and
at last changed her seat and retired behind the
curtain for the rest of the evening. A Governor’s
wife who objects to be rudely stared at has a
very hard row to hoe.
The two vice-regal damsels wore white silk
gowns with huge sleeves, and their light-brown
hair very pleasantly dressed. They are pretty
girls with the usual well - broken-in appearance
peculiar to English maidenhood, and they both
looked as if they would want very safe mounts
indeed. The Hampden suite (five in all) look
young and frisky ; with Family Herald legs, for
the most part; and blondehairimmaoulatelyparted.
They all have nice, pink complexions which many
a Sydney girl would like to find in her own mirror,
and, taking them en masse, they appear to be
thoughtfully christened. “Freddy,” “Cecil,”
“ Algy,” and “Francis ” are the Christian names
of four of the staff, and I really must say they
look it.
The play—that old and hoary American horror,
“ Power of the Press,” under the new title of the
“ Carson Case ” —was certainly not the thing of
the occasion. There was no dressing on the stage,
and the local scenery was the only enthusiasm.
It is strange to hear people wildly cheering the
appearance of the G.P.O. when they pass the real
thing every day of their lives without looking at
it, much less saying “ hooray ; ” and it was a still
more unaccountable thing to hear the enthusiasm
with which a set of Darlinghurst gaol was greeted
by the enraptured gods. A stranger might have
mistaken it for a popular boarding-house, it got
such a cordial “hand.” Really, the most soothing
thing in a singularly bleak performance was the
music supplied by the orchestra between the acts.
A feature ot interest in the Lyceum d.c. on
V.R. night was “Zun.” and his bear-leader. I
think the evils of over-cycling are written large on
the ’Melican wheelist, who has the stoop of an
old man, and the concentrated, heavy expression
which comes from biking, so they say. I thought
I detected several physical aids to fame about
Zim. For one thing, he has loosely-hung ears
which would lead to no small increase of impetus
if the wind got behind them at a hot finish, and
then he has a long, thin neck, which is another
advantage where people win by an eyelash very
often. And this long neck gives Zim. an oppor-
tunity in private life for wearing a most elongated
collar—the nearest approach 1 have ever seen to
it is the tin collar they sometimes put on refrac-
tory goats. I hope this isn’t blasphemy.
The final bicycle championship meeting at Syd-
ney Association-ground, last Saturday, drew a
tremendous crowd, and would have made up into
an imposing show if it hadn’t come on to rain.
The V.R.-box, in the centre of the stand, was
rather cautiously‘draped with “jack,” as if the
management were anxious not to overdo it, and
you couldn’t see how the lawn was looking for the
press of people. But the rain spoilt everything,
and the spectacle of soaked champions, with their
silk clinging to their biceps, tearing round in the
mud while a damp crowd shook its glistening
umbrellas at them, was enough to set one sneez-
ing. In the ladies’ stand, rows of foolish women
sat out in the rain and soaked, while the wise
virgins who couldn’t get a seat on the stand just
sniffed contemptuously at the bad management of
Jupiter Pluvius, opened their umbrellas viciously,
and went straight home again. Gov. Hampden
and the hon. Misses Margaret and Alice Brand
were the V.R. gaping-stocks on this occasion.
Acting vice-royalties—who occasionally put on
more frill than the genuine sort, just as an
amateur actress generally sports more paint than
does a professional—have been having a harassing
time all over Australia, lately—with so many new
Governors coming and showing the candle in the
turnip, so to speak. Really, acting-vice-regal
people have a distressing time, taking it
altogether. For an uncertain period they are
kow-towed to and run after ; they learn to enter
public buildings stepping in time to the Anathema,
and they leave off providing their own bouquets
or rushing to reserve front seats ; and then, at a
blow, as the French say, the whole situation
stands on its head—the new star arrives and they
fade away to rushlights.
I am sure the ladies in the case must have a
twinge under the circumstances—indeed, I have
seen them throwing mournful, Peri-outside-Para-
dise kind of glances at the vice-regal box in which
they once held sway, but which knows them no
more, and I am quite sure that many an ousted
acting-governoress, when she hands the incoming
real Jamaica Madame the stereotyped bunch of
flowers on the threshold of Government House,
would very often much rather hand her a bunch
of scorpions. For even acting-governoresses are
human, and I have never met a woman yet who
could take pleasure in being deposed.
The great gathering of the clans “ to meet Card.
Moran and the hierarchy of Australia,” at Sydney
Town Hall, on last Monday evening, was most
successful, with Gov. Hampden discovered on a
dazzling dais, all draped and festooned with
“jack” and lavish decorations of jamias and
hydrangea all round the galleries. Meat and
drink were lavishly laid on in the vestibule, and
VViegand at the organ filled up the gaps. I
thought the reception looked as if the Union ot
Christendom was a settled thing—socially, at all
events—for all sorts of people were there. Gov.
Hampden was accompanied by one pretty
daughter, in white silk (do the V.R. girls always
wear white silk?), who was chaperoned by the aet-
ing-Governoress, whose very decollete black silk
probably accounted for Card. Moran not figuring
prominently on the dais. I believe that His
Eminence will not hold parley with the V-shaped,
and once when a Governor’s wife in evening dress
removed her wrap while conversing with him the
Card, immediately bowed and left her. So most
of the people had a tucker in their frocks, and
many came with their dresses tickling their ears—
but Society won by a neck, as usual, and there
were really a good many handsome modern
editions of Mother Eve’s fig-leaves on tap
The many priests—some in their “ fariola,” the
botanical name for a clerical garb which only
wants a frill of lace and a dart or two to make a
novel and rather melting tea-gown—gave pictur-
esqueness to the huge crowd, and the Cardinal’s
scarlet robes lent the necessary warmth. Every-
thing was abnormally full fig. Every man who
had a brass collar came out with it on, and even
the women tried to look official. One very portly
consuless in black sported a maroon and cream
sash over her shoulder, and tied at the side —
giving her rather the appearance of a welter-
weight jockey or a Mason at a funeral.
The dressing generally was very good. Lady
Innes wore pompadour brocade; Mrs. Eddy looked
well in salmon-pink ; Mrs. Scot-Skirving was in
white brocade and chiffon ; and Mrs. James Allison
(Miss Burdekin) in eau-de-nil, with a very tricky
bodice, and diamond flowers in her hair. There
really were many unobjectionable gowns, so I
forgave a fat lady dressed in wall-paper, also a
sprinkling of females in bonnets, who must have
had bald heads underneath, or they would cer-
tainly have taken them off—l mean the bonnets—
when they saw how the evening was wagging.
The new Gov would seem to be starring in the
title rble. All the consuls—under the wing of
Mens. Biard d’Aunet, Consul-General of France—
called at Guvmentouse recently, and, although
they were all photographed, with his Ex. in the
centre, at the conclusion of the visit—still they all
came away enchanted with the goings-on of the
Brand new household.
SAPPHO SMITH.
“In England the most conspicuous edifices
you see, approaching a town, are steeples. In
America, they are centres of intelligence.”—
Huxley.
A kindly-disposed Melbourne pastrycook
who twice a week distributed to the needy all his
broken and spoilt pastry, has been compelled to
give up the practice because of the tremendous
rush of applicants. The rush for the “ spoils.”
Victoria, with 73 medical men for every
100,000 of population, has the second best record
for number in the world, beating Europe hollow.
But England has a doctor to every four sq. miles,
whereas Victoria only has one to every 105 miles.
Jewish veterans of the German army, to
the number of 500, gathered in a Berlin cemetery
in connection with this year’s celebration of
Sedan. This had considerable significance,' be-
cause of the anti-Semitic agitation, and the rabbis
who spoke enlarged on the fallacy of supposing
that the Jews stood apart from the defence of the
country. Nevertheless, the newspapers of the
other side returned to the charge with extra
fervor, showing the paucity of Jewish officers.
The Bulletin Christmas Number will
this year be published Dec. 12. It is a hard
matter to outdo previous Bulletin Christmas
Numbers in point of interest and variety, but if
that feat can be achieved it will be achieved on
Deo._ 12. The letterpress and illustrations are of
particularly high literary and artistic value, and
promise to move the great soul of the community
as that organ has rarely been moved before. The
price, as usual, is 7d. post free from this office to
any address ; and wholesale agents must positively
send in orders at once to ensure full supply.
The Phillip Ward (Sydney) aldermanic
contest presents a somewhat curious aspect.
Alderman Johnston (a native Australian and 17-
years’ Sydney resident), who has represented the
ward for the past 12 months with vigour and
credit, is opposed by Mr. Sam Bowen, whose recom-
mendations for the control of municipal affairs
appear to lie in the facts that he is a popular
young man and a very good tailor. The one point
alleged, singularly enough, against Mr. John-
ston’s candidature is the vote which he gave
for temporarily reducing some municipal wages
by 4d. a day—when the alternative was blank dis-
missal. The Council preferred to dismiss 96 men
altogether rather than reduce the wages of all by
a small amount for a short time—an unwise and
unfair action, as it appears to The Bulletin.
This paper, therefore, is quite unable to see why
the ratepayers should prefer a young man with no
apparent municipal merits to a tried aldermen of
sound business capacity and good democratic
record.
From “ Memoirs of a Cadet ” :
One evening as our party (in India) were strolling on
shore, after the day’s journey, we observed a native of
most filthy appearance engaged in what appeared to us a
very extraordinary process. He repeatedly laid himself
on the ground at full length, making, at the same time,
a mark in the sand close to the crown of his head ; then,
rising, he placed his feet by the said mark, and lay down
again as before. This process he might continue ad
infinitum, for aught we know, as we saw no end to it,
and each successive prostration gaining one length of his
body (o the southward. We addressed him and inquired
his objects. He returned us no answer ; nor did he
appear to notice us, vl e then made the same inquiry of
some natives, who were also watching him, not with
curiosity like ourselves, but with the most profound
respect and reverence. He was a devout fakeler on his
pilgrimage to Juggernaut, whither he was making his
way in the manner described —namely, measuring; the
whole distance by the length of his body. He had
already thus travelled more than 300 miles ; and had 400
miles to go.”
Hodges, J. (loquitur):
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You have heard of the pleasant Sabbatical
Afterpoons spent by the Just;
Where religion’s expounded, fanatical,
And all sorts of topics discussed ;
Where we fight against all devil’s dodges
And Judges make statements so odd,
You’ve not yet heard God upon Hodges
But you’ve heard Justice Hodges on God.
Williams, J. is a bit atheistical,
A Beckett’s religion’s no good,
The Chief is proud, vain, egotistical,
Poor lost souls are Holroyd and Hood,
But I am the Judge of the Judges,
Their frail little foibles I prod,
Human weakness to my mind mere fudge is,
So I angrily criticise God.
. When the floodgates of Heaven are locked up
And the Lord for men’s sins sends a drought,
And horses and cattle are knocked up,
Why ! God doesn’t know what He’s about.
If Hh don’t send the rain when I ask it,
If He don’t let the showers begin,
Still I feel I’m “the pick of the basket ”
And God’s only a fiend with a grin.
I think nothing of tearing my Bible
If God doesn’t answer my prayer
When I ask Him to send to my kiddies
Enough for the day and to spare.
I’ve a temper that’s simply seraphic,
And when I’m laid under the sod
I’m sure there’ll be nothing more graphic
Than Hodges, J.’s judgment on God.

PROCLAMATION.
IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under (he brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ THZRZEIE CASTLES” and ££ C-A-IPSTLAEsT. 7 '
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “ Capstan" Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. [Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

Saturdays—3oth November and 7th December, 1895.
H. H. MOORE, Hon. Sec.
The seven Australias own among them
Some 122 million sheep, 14 million cattle, two
million horses, 13 hundred thousand swine.
Mr. E. P. Dawbarn, of Sydney, claims the
credit of discovering the real character of the
woman Lennox, for an alleged assault upon whom
Buttner was to have been hanged, at Sydney, a
year or two ago. “Being in Brisbane,” writes
Mr. Dawbarn, “ I discovered the real character
of the woman. Returned to Sydney and saw the
Minister for Justice, who at once sent to Brisbane
for all particulars, with the result as known.
The officials in Brisbane expressed surprise to me
that no enquiry had been made locally concerning
the girl’s character. She had been an inmate of
Woogaroo lunatic asylum, where I first heard of
her."

Tlie latest thing out—the New Woman
with the latch-key.
The time-honored “ sickening thud ” of
Melb. evening-paper execution-reports is defunct.
Melb. Hekald concluded the Emma Williams
“hanging” with “ A convulsive cry ! ” And its
loathsome contemporary’s version was “ A chok-
ing gasp 1 ”
“ Jack the Ripper ” is now confined in an
English lunatic asylum. He is a religious maniac,
who conceived that God had ordered him to blot
immoral women out of the world ; and it was his
practice, during the time he was committing his
murders, to attend St. Paul’s Cathedral every
morning and give thanks unto Heaven for the
success which had attended his mission. “The
church has never built asylums enough to hold the
insane of its own making. It has shed more blood
than light. It has made more orphans than it
has fed.”
Spagnoletti : “ What makes the trade of
religion stink in the world’s nostrils is that it is a
gate-money business. Hardly has the Melb.
public done laughing over the preposterous folly
of the Y.M.C.A., with its “suitable” saintly
guinea crowd to see Brassey, than the hocus-pocus
about General Booth arouses equal contempt.
He comes to Melbourne. Of course, he is eager
to greet the * soldiery,’ and weeps with joy at the
thought. Of course, they are eager to see him,
and swarm at the wharf. But this won’t do at
all. The most strenuous efforts are made in vain
to keep the ignorant Salvationists in the dark,
and shuffle him through incognito. He keeps
under hatches, then rushes out into his cab at the
last moment. Anyone would think it was Bar-
num’s Gyascutis, with the fuglemen swarming
round to take care nobody sees him. Why don’t
they fetch him ashore in a coffin, or in a barrel,
or the big violoncello, or big drum ? But, mind
you, there’s to be the mighty welcome, when he
really comes. Booth, Jehovah and Co. ! ”
Judge Williams, soon after his promotion
to the Vic. Bench, had a startling experience of
the difference between real facts and police per-
versions of them. A woman of indifferent repute
was charged with purloining jewellery—a scarf-
pin, or something—from a man, late at night, in a
Melbourne street. Constable deposed that the
prosecutor having got into a bit of a brawl,
emerged with the complaint that his scarf-pin
was gone. Thereupon he (the constable) arrested
this woman, who was there or thereabouts,_ and
charged her with the crime, which she admitted,
saying “ I robbed the man.” It was an unanswer-
able case, on the face of it, and the woman seemed
doomed to at least a couple of years’ hard labor.
Yet his Honor was not satisfied of her guilt. He
asked the constable whether anyone could endorse
his story of the candid confession. Constable
stated that some man, whom he didn’t know,
must have overheard the words, but unfortunately
the desirable witness disappeared without leaving
his name and address. There seemed no chance
for the prisoner to escape conviction. The judge
was about to sum up when a man came forward
and asked to be sworn. He was, as it appeared,
the man who overheard the alleged confession,
and he, had come to express a disinterested belief
in the woman’s innocence. She did speak the
words proved against her, but not in the sense
attributed to them by the policeman. When
charged with the theft she had shown much
astonishment, and her answer was “ I robbed the
man ! ” with a heavy accent on the personal pro-
noun. The active and intelligent officer’s
evidence collapsed into dust and ashes, and the
undefended female was presently discharged with-
out any further stain on her character.

"Daore was Roselle’s evil genius.”— Melb. paper.
Amy Roselle’s father was a Jewish schoolmaster.
When Daore heard of H. B. Conway’s recent attempted
suicide he said it was very foolish.
Amy Fawsitt, original heroine of the “ Two Roses,”
was Amy Roselle’s rival in youth, and perished miserably
in a New York tenement house, not without suspicions of
foul play.
Ed. Bulletin, —Some of Mr. Tapley's marmalading
acquaintances are trying to find a grievance in the fact
that the end of his engagement with W. and M. wasn’t
announced by a flourish of trumpets, and a benefit. There
were no trumpets or benefit in the bond, presumably.
This engagement, like others of equal importance, died a
natural death, and Tapley, for his part, passed away with
a peaceful smile on his face. The last member of the
opera co. on whom the Finn thrust a gratuitous benefit
was Ryley, as far as I remember. That was years ago,
when Ryley had not been very long with them, and his
popularity was at its meridian. —Melb.
The Firm s new man, Court,ice Pounds, is not being run
for all he is worth at the start of his engagement, and so
will improve on acquaintance. Tapley and Brownlow’
were fired off on the rocket principle. Tapley, as the
curate in ‘‘Vicar of Bray,” gave the first impression that
he might be an exceedingly clever character-actor, but
the public soon found that the goody-goody part of Sand-
ford played Tapley, whose young-curate manner was pro-
bably born in him. So with Brownlow. He belongs to
the heavy, romantic class of baritones, and as the royal
bearded masher of “ Ma Mie Rosette ” he displayed all of
his stage effects to the very best advantage. Here again
the character played the actor, so to speak, by adapting
itself to his marked mannerisms.

in “ King John.”
Paradis; “What is the use of mincing the matter’
Amy Roselle was too old. The drama is not the hurdy-
gurdy of yore. It is as S“rious a pursuit as any. The
actress is necessarily short-lived 'professionally), like the
cricketer, cycler, jockey, or racehorse, for the matter of
that. Is this a hardship? No, indeed. The wages are
immense. They can make as much in ten years as they
used to do in a lifetime, and so will have to make up their
minds that their lifetime is ten years, and act the ant
accordingly.”
D-ar Bulletin,—Who saj's that Amv Roselle “ wasn’t
seen at anything like her best in Australia?” Probably
a Sydney writer. She may have lost heart after the
disastrous season here, but Melbourne certainly got some
very fine work from the talented lady in “A Wife’s
Ordeal,” and one or two other plays. Her “Rizpah”
monologue, too, was about the strongest bit of tragic
acting achieved by any English artist in Melbourne during
the last dozen years.—Yarra-Yarra.
“In their death they were not divided.”—2 Samuel 1

The hardest thing about the Dacre tragedy is the
people who insist on hanging their little advt. on the
coffins of the dead actor and actress. The following letter
by Lewis Scott in S.M. Herald is a case in point:—
“I should like to dispel any impression that Mr. Arthur
Dacre was being unduly burdened by the part of Dean
Maitland. He had six days in which to study it. It was
a part fed by every other, and any of our leading
juveniles, such as Scot Inglis, H. R. Roberts, Cvril
Keightley, or Harry Plimmer, could have mastered it in
three days. In fact, I offered on Saturday night
to study it and play it on Monday; but, of
course, I had a large superficial knowledge of the
whole play. Mr. Dacre asked me to see the manage-
ment, and have him relieved of all worry about cos-
tume, and some uncertainty which had arisen about his
contract. All this was done. On Saturday night, about 10,
he told me he was all right, that he would make a big hit
on Monday, and that he and I would take the play to
London, where he had lots of rich friends to finance its
production. He asked me to dine with them at ion
Monday, dress there, and go to the theatre. Mrs. Dacre
was, she said, in love with her part, and felt she should
have the sympathy of the audience all through. But
they were both mentally and bodily unwell, and when I
told Daore to cut whatever delayed the story in his part,
he smiled, and said he wouldn’t cut a line, he liked it all,’’
&c. Herein Mr. Scott (whom the general public only
know as an ex-civil-servant) manages to convey that he is
an actor of much ability, and capable of taking an im-
portant leading character at the shortest notice. Also,
that though the four “leading juveniles” in question
could learn the part in three days, he could do it in two.
Also, that Mr. and Mrs. Daore, who should have been
among the very best judges in Australia, were full of
admiration for Mr. Scott’s play, and Mr. Dacre reckoned
it good enough to boom in London. Which is a good deal
of advertisement to hang on to the simple statement that
the deceased actor wasn’t quite so tired as people sup-
posed.

The fracas between a prominent and heavyweight
Thespian and a certain theatrical agent is the principal
gossip at Melb. “ Poverty Point ” just now. Briefly put:
the agent neglected to observe the spirit of de mortuis
nil nisi bonum, and the Thespian instructed him to

was the heavy Thespian.

Clever acrobat Dan Feeley, of Fillis’s circus, has, every-
one will regret to hear, been crippled for life in South
Africa. He rose from a sick bed to show at a benefit to
Fillis, and, when doing his leap over 30 fixed bayonets,
one of them pierced his leg just below the knee. Mortifi-
cation compelled amputation.

Showman: "The cardinal mistake in Booth’s Melb.
circus-procession was showing him to the people, which
spoilt the gat j -money. I would have put him in a cara-
van, with a hole on top, through which he might twiddle
the belltopper on a stick. The show is getting sadly

cobbler, hallelujah knightman, saved gutter-snipe, and ail

just like the new banks. Started to meet a felt want
and help the struggling trader, -they drop into the swim
with the cook salmon of the big accounts. Booth, on a
dunghill, is a mighty power. Traversing Melbourne in a
superb carriage and pair, he is a mixture of Davies and
Kennedy, with a spice of Coppin.”

A certain mesmerist met with one nasty experience.
A young man, who had been put “under the influence,”
was benevolently punished by his friends afterwards with
a description of the asinine figure he cut while fishing off
a chair and singing hymns, etc. The boy got mad, and
went on the stage next night full of antagonistic resist-
ance. He pretended to “go off” as usual, and when the
professor” commanded him to “hit me,” he did, and
continued the pummelling till the Prof, had to flee to his
dressmgroom. The audience enjoyed the show hugely,
but the mesmerist has omitted that item from his bill
ever since.
While in Sydney old Sophia, the Maori guide to the
Hot Lakes, visited a curiosity shop and tried to buy an
ancient war canoe paddle to which some legend.iry inter-
est attached. The price asked was beyond the old Maori’s
means, and, after examining the relic with the keenest
interest, she sadly returned it to the shopkeeper.
The first lady-cyclist has arrived in the far West. When
Ada Delroy and co. arrived at Bourke railway-station she
mounted a bike and vanished out of sight. “A woman
on a cycle 1 it spread like wildfire. Men and women
with plates and dishcloths, flatirons, and shaving-brushes
still in their hands rushed madly down the street to see
the marvellous sight. The Dean case is dead—stone-dead
in Bourke. The woman on the bike has taken its place.
The Calculating Boy, at present touring Australia, is a
son of the “Georgia Magnet,” famous in the London halls
a few years back. Johnny, his pa and the Magnet are now
residing in Robe-st., St. Kilda. where the little lady’s
(private) exhibitions occasionally startle guests at her
residence.

One soulful Melb. black-and-white artist has amongst
his studio treasures three squares of carpet, by which he
sets a priceless stole. He will tell you “with entranced
eye’’that they came from the departed Gee-Gees’dressing-
rooms, and that the dainty (and probably bare) feet of
Grace and Maud have pressed their velvety pile. The
fortunate possessor nightly kneels reverently upon each
in turn, and says the little prayer that all good artists
aay.
One night, at the old Dublin Queen’s, “ Othello ” was
on, and the audience was spell-bound by the realistic
way in which the Moor was bolstering his wife. After a
terrific struggle Desdemona got away, rushed down to
the footlights, and exclaimed: “Good God! what a
narrow escape I’ve had 1 The drunken brute really in-
tended to finish me."

Portus—of next Australian Eleven will be about the most

in Australia by a practical and scientific man.
* ♦ * *
Vet: The usual remedy for a shying horse is the
whip—about the worse thing in many cases. Many shy
through some eye-trouble, such as short-sight or cataract,
which prevents the horse seeing an object till he is close
to it and thinks he is right on it. A blind horse never
shies, because he never sees anything. What’s the good
of the whip in a case of cataract ? When a sound horse
shies at anything, lead him slowly up to it, so that he
may see it close and know it is harmless. Gibers again
sutler from nerves. You don't whip a nervous woman—
at least not in public.
* * * *
Recent researches into the records of birth of
16,000 foals show that 97 colts are born for every 100
fillies ; that up to the end of the third month of gestation
the foetus is sexless; that the sex is determined by the
bodily condition of the mother in the fourth and fifth
month ; that if the mare, and therefore the foetus, be
well-nourished, the sex is most likely to he female. The
age of the stallion seems to have no effect on the sex of
the foal, but an old mare, being less strong than a
younger one, is likely to drop a colt. Summed up, the
facts seem to mean—healthy mares in their prime with
abundant food will most likely drop fillies; old or ill-fed
or unhealthy mares will mostlikely drop colts.

For the last six months Ikey Mo’s Melbourne
weekly has been grinding out yarns under the head of
“Systems of Betting, or Battling with the Books.” It
goes without saying that the object is to lure punters
away from the Tote system, in which there are no bookies
to battle with.

George Bubear, who played an up-and-down
game with poor old Hanlan at a Texas regatta last week,
threatens Do come over to Australia anon. He and his
English mates may possibly want to have a cut at one or
two of our men, he says, but it Bubear should arrive it
will be found that he doesn’t dream of cutting high. He
was here some years ago, and his prudence in selecting
suitable “marks” was most praiseworthy. As second-
rate scullers go, Bubear is a wise man.

In the Melbourne lawn-tennis tournament, last
week, four N.S.VV. men and half-a-dozen Victorians com-
peted for the single championship. In the first round,
VVindeyer (N.S.VV.) wiped out Fox (N.S.W.), and Rice
(N.S. VV.) wiped out Irving (Vic.), whilst the other N.S. W.
man and live .of the Vies, stood, out of the round alto-
gether. Then, in round No. 2, a couple of the Vies, were
vanquished by their own countrymen, and the fifth went
down before Rice. Also, VVindeyer appeared again and
wiped out the other N.S.VV. mao, wiio hadn’t played in
the first round. Therefore, Rice and VVindeyer had to
win two games each before arriving at round No. 3, hut
the remaining pair of Vies, got there on the strength of
only one win eich. And, whereas a couple of Vies, were
hound to be left in the third round, Rice ran a double
risk of getting defeated by a Vic., in which case only
Windeyer, from N.S.VV., would have remained, along with
three Vies., for tlie final ties. A lawn-tennis tournament
moves in what seems a mysterious way to the outsider.
Regarded as an interprovincial event its conduct is most
unsportsmanlike. The tournament was almost as un-
interprovincial as possible in the first two rounds, and all
its eccentricity favored the local men.

The Boston (U.S.) Police News makes some
remarks about Griffo; “Nobody in America would be
likely, I think, to cry himself into a decline over young
Griffo’s announced purpose to ‘soon return to Australia.’
The spirit of the imp of all hoodlum darkness was never,
I believe, quite so strong in any of the errant race of
fighters as it is in Griffo. He is the sancho and the star
sewer-rat of the sparring profession. He has been em-
broiled about all the time with somebody, and is seem-
ingly a helpless prey to his low propensities. Drink don’t
change Griffo for the worse, as it did Sullivan, because
Griffo is at nit times a ‘ m ice,’and destitute utterly of
regard for what he pledges himself to in order to obtain
money. I don't know how many ambitious managers
have had ‘ Griffo under contract. He will sign with any
man for anything, providing he receives a cash-iu-hand
advance. Personally, lie has run himself out wherever he
has been a little while known. People who think highly
of him as the greatest nonesuch of scienoed boxers don’t
want to meet him or talk with him, and will run around
the corner if they see him coming. The hostility of all
the police authorities in and about New York to Griffo is,
for good reason, decided and deep-rooted. Such as he is
unfortunately intensify the prejudices of the community
against all pugilists and sparrers. The chances of the
class for livelihood are pulled down by the ways in which
he outrages public decency and makes himself an element
of disorder. There will surely be a sigh of relief in
America if Griffo changes ids residence back to his own
country. His matchless and masterly cleverness in hand-
ling himself as an exponent of the ‘ manly art ’ is the one
solitary thing about him that can be dwelt upon with
admiration or even with patience.”

The Vic. four-oared championship ended in an
easy win for the Mercantile, who paddled home four
lengths ahead of Albert Park—in turn separated by a
length from the Wendouree (Ballarat). The famous
Yarra Yarra Club, which has won this event for five years
running, was not represented—chiefly, perhaps, for want
of a decent craft. Ohamley, the stalwart veteran, who
has for some time past rowed No. 3 in the Yarra four, this
time occupied a similar seat in the Albert Park boat
which was stroked by the lanky Powell, winner of the
Champion Sculls in Brisbane. The Mercs, were stroked
by H. T. Davies, sec. of the local rowing association, H.
Lindgren rowing No. 3, Sloan, the brilliant Fitzroy foot-
baller, No. 2, and Dawson, the “cash-amateur" cy'clist,
who raised such trouble at the last inlerprovincial con-
tests, being bow. Davies showed his crew’s superiority
from the start.

No crowd could have taken Zimmerman more
kindly than the Sydneyites did iast week ; “ dropping ”
to the fact that “ Aathah ” was sick, they hooted gentlv
when he drew off after going two laps. Nathless, it was
a mistake to bill Zimmerman so largely. Rumours of
his indisposition were all over the towni the week preced-
mg last Saturday. It was only his brilliant riding
on the Saturday before that saved a demonstration.
Walford, Zim.’s manager, was away in Mel-
bourne until Saturday morning; he says that Zim. will
not be billed and will not start again until he is in true
form. Zim. has been medically forbidden to ride for
three weeks, and will take a holiday in the Blue Moun-
tains. His trouble appears to be malarial fever.

had a bundle. He had too good a reputation for game

that he had no bundle at cycling last Saturday. After
winning the £lOO Metrop, Handicap, he came out
(without having had even time for a rub down) and won
the £lO three-mile Open Handicap. It was a great per-
formance, and showed his true form from every stand-
point.

Close on two months ago the true form of
Meadham, Payne and Graeme was pointed out by Tub
Bulletin. The style i i which these men rode on Saturday
in the big Metrop. Handicap fully met expectations
Meadham and Graeme won their heats in fine style, while
Payne was beaten on the post through an unexpectedly
brilliant and game finish by Elliott. If Elliott had saved
the spurt he put in his finish—he went by Parsons as if
the latter were standing still—he could have had more
say in the final.
The final saw a desperate finish between Mead-
ham (N.S.W.), Martin (America), Payne and MMnnes
(N.S.W.); but Meadham, who had made the pace for
three-quarters of a lap, got home by a few inches from
MTnnes. Martin, the scratch man who gave MTnnes
20yds. and Meadham 65, ran third. Martin’s pluck drew
an extra £25 from the League.
In giving Martin .£25 and Porta .£25 the
League acted generously ; Porta broke the small bone
of his arm through an accident while training. The
League cleared £l7OO on the Carnival, and awarded
“Florrie” Hanslow, the chief engineer, £lOO and
Barkell, sub-engineer, £5O.
The hot pace set by the triplet in the Five Miles
Australian Championship last Saturday shut up the sick
Zimmerman after two laps; Lewis (N.S.W.) cleverly
snatched the triplet from Parsons (Vic.), and set the field
a rattling go. Martin, O’Brien, Chapman, Simpson and
Payne all collapsed in quick order, and at the seventh lap
only Lewis, Parsons, Iredale, and Megson hung on. At
the ninth lap Megson cried go, and the race became a
duel between Parsons and Lewis. Parsons won, and said
it was the most gruelling race he ever rode. Had Lewis
known how much Parsons was baked, the finish might
have been closer. Iredale, who was third, would have
done better but for a nasty fall early in the afternoon.
When Gov. Hampden arrived he aaid—or was
represented by a daily as having said—that he favored a
certain brand of cycle. Now, it is announced that he has
ordered three cycles of quite another brand. This, per-
haps, as a knock at the firm which used him as an advt -
hoarding on the very day he landed.

• * * *
Writes a Bulletin correspondent : Zim. shows
bad judgment in not accepting for the Austral,
which, on paper, on performances, and on form, looks
a “dead snap for him did he choose to start.
[He denies that he ever entered, and he is
now too ill to race, anyhow. Ed. B.] This
year s limit man in the big event will he thrown off
the 360 yd. mark, whereas in Tom Busst’s year, when the
champion carried off the £2OO from scratch, he had to
give away as much as 420yds. According to this, the
handicappers are apparently of opinion either that Zim.
is a 60yds. poorer man than Busst, or that the third-class
men of the present time are 60yds. better than those of
six years ago. What ground is there for either of these
beliefs? The first may be summarily dismissed, and the
second is almost as ridiculous. The number of cyclists
has increased tenfold since Busst’s era, and every pos-
sessor of a jigger in Australia fancies his chance for
the big prize. Accordingly, entries are now received
from tenth-raters who can scarcely straddle a machine.
The plaint in former years has always been that the rear
men handicapped out; this year they seem to have
the race finished bar shouting—and blocking. To analyse
the men on the back marks, Martin and Porta haven’t a
possible show, giving Parsons start; and that rider, on
Sydney form, would require much more than the 60yds.
allotted him to make Zim. warm.
Now that the Yankee has dropped out, how-
ever, the race piomises well for Parsons. He has men
every few yards ahead to help him along, assuming that
the heats are fairly distributed, and his strength can
carry him through the grass at a pace calculated to crack
up the second-raters badly. Taking all things into con-
sideration, there are worse things than Parsons for the
Austral of '95.
* * • •
* lit is but 18 months since Porta, the Italian
cyclist, first rode a machine, yet he has carried off the
100 kilometres of Italy against all the European cracks.
His mate, Martin, the Yank, was also a quick learner,
winning his first race of 100 miles (!) three weeks after
starting. Porta has been enthusiastically taken up by
the Italian residents of Melb., who, headed by basso
profuudo De Alba, have made a perfect little god of the
Milanese youth.
* * * ». I
Father John M‘Kenna, a Maoriland priest
weighing 16J stone, recently put up a bicycle record by
doing the 11 miles from Masterton to Carterton, along a
road that is neither very smooth nor very level, in 37min.
The bulky Father on a bike, with his shiny hat driven
hard down on his head and his murky coat-tails flying
far behind him, should be worth seeing.
Harris, the English cyclist, talks about every-
thing but cycling. His pet hobby is photography, and
he spends all he makes on the track in plates and chemi-
cals, developing thousands of plates he never afterwards
takes a print from. He holds that the visit of first-class
riders will do Australians a world of good. “When first
we went to France,” he says, “there were only second-
raters there. Now they turn round and beat us.”
• * * «
George Turner, the 18-year old son of the Vic.
Premier, is an amateur cyclist who could give a few of
the pro’s yards and a beating.
* * * *
Megson, having now fainted on the track twice
i i long races, had better give up the game. Often when
a woman faints it doesn’t mean very much ; but when an
apparently strong man faints it always means a great
deal.

The secretary of Sydney Agricultural Society
has taken time by the forelock; he has laid down a
banked cinder cycle-track—3J laps to the mile with a long
finishing straight, which should favor the making of Aus-
tralian records.
• • • *
Gov. Brassey on a “ trike ” is a weird sight.
He wears white flannel trousers, a long thin grey coat,
and a hard boxer hat.
• * » »
Harkins, the 16-year-older who won the Sydney
Wheel Race £lOO prize, is a Sydney native, and learnt to
ride only a year ago. His first race was at the Highland
Gathering in Jan. ; since then he has jigged about 20
times, but until he secured “ the big money” he was
placed hut once-second to Graeme in a two-mile hatid : -
cap last July. He is sft. Sin., and 9st. 121 b., and has
gained 51b. during his past six weeks’ training. He was
never pushed during the race, and ascribes his race to a
good sprint which he put in when the competitor he
most dreaded came near him. That rival had come up
after a real long sprint, and the one Harkins then put in
“busted” him. Harkins rode a machine geared to 68.
He is a dentist’s apprentice, and will finish his dental
t duoation in America or England. He intends to cycle
there too.

A recent cycle meeting started a fairly-
dimensioned hubbub about a couple of allegedly stiff
competitors riding rigged machines, and about one alleged
stiff offering during the race to pace another man for a
tenner.

Barker, the “Queensland giant,” in their recent fight at
Sydney, is unlikely ever to recover from the effects of his
injuries—which will probably affect his head to the day of
his death.

prietor of the new “ Cannalin ” remedies, free
samples and explanatory pamphlets will be for-
warded to any part of the colonies on application
to the Dep6t for Australasia, 257 George-street,
Sydney, N.S.W. References kindly allowed to a
number of prominent Australian citizens cured
after years of suffering, in many instances where
operation had been followed by a severer form of
the complaint—in others where it was about to
take place as a last resort.*

Another split in the Theosophical Society,
again just as Mrs. Besant was leaving England.
But this time she thought it wise to remain on
the spot, and her departure for India has been
postponed from Oct. 11 to Dec. 4. Herbert Bur-
rows, the most prominent male member of the
Society, has seceded, making wholesale allega-
tions of fraud against “H.P.8.” and the whole
management of the Society. Burrows is a well-
known Socialistic and Freethinking lecturer in
London, and is acknowledged by all who know
him to be transparently truthful and honest.
Afc Kalgoorlie (W.A.), the calico roof has
blown off the court-house, and the sun-blaze pours
right in. The public is still required to take its
hat off when it enters the building, out of respect
for the Majesty of the Law, so that litigation is
simply another name for sunstroke. In quite a
lot of cases now judgment goes by default, because
the other party to the case lias had to take his bare
head out of the glare.
A Dunolly (Vic.) solicitor’s bill of costs
against his client, the subject of legal proceedings
to recover, contained one item—“to making out
bill.” Judge Gaunt inquired if a grocer commonly
charged his customers for making up the bill for
goods sold. Verily, it pays to be a lawyer.
The latest Kanakaland leper chronicle is
that one of the “cheap and reliable,” an un-
doubted leper, begged his way on foot from
Geraldton, N.Q., to Townsville; another, after
medical examination, was carefully placed in the
lock-up at Townsville to mingle with eight ordin-
ary drunks. However, as Col. Sec. Tozer cheer-
fully says, leprosy isn’t catching.
The Duke of Marlborough fulfilled the
Ruskinian obligation that young couples should
see plenty of each other before marriage. An
American journal, in its social intelligence,
recently stated that “ the Duke of Marlborough
and Miss Consuelo Vanderbilt went bathing
together yesterday ! ”
The Dean case remains very much where
it was, and the batch of alleged conspirators are
still awaiting trial with what grace they may.
The only complete account of the developments of
the Dean case (and curse) to date is that given in
The Bulletin’s History, which consists of 20
pages and cover, full Bulletin size, and is
enriched by many illustrations and portraits of
the principal persons concerned. Post-free, 7d.
per copy'; agents and storekeepers supplied at
wholesale rates.
Another phase of the alien question. A
N.S.W. North Coast clergyman dressed down his
congregation on a recent Sunday for their nig-
gardliness in re the collection-plate. His weekly
collections averaged only 305., while his household
exes, amounted to over 40s. He told them that
it might be possible for them to import a Hindoo
or a Chinese clergyman to attend to their spiritual
wants for such a pittance as 305., but they would
never get a Maynooth man to do it. The point of
the rebuke was that many fat members of the
congregation were Hindoo employers.
Amongst the pensions granted last year
by the British Govt, was one of <£loo per annum
to the well-boomed poet, William Watson. This is
the neurotic young man who, a couple of years ago
or so, made a rush for the Prince of Wales’s car-
nage as it was passing down Rotten Row, with
the intention (as it subsequently turned out) of
kissing Tummy’s hand or boots or any other
accessible part of his Royal Highness. He was
given in charge at the time as being mad—but it
looks as if there was method in his madness. For
probably this frenzied outburst of loyalty, rather
than his poetry, is responsible for his pension.
Caterer Beach, brother-in-law of Chief
Justice Way, and the principal Adelaide
restaurateur, died the other day, aged 66. It is
related that when Way engineered vice-regal
functions he would call his humbler relative into
his room and say, “ Well,' Beach, which is it to
be, caterer or guest ? Not both.” The old man
invariably chose the more profitable capacity.
Beach long ago made money on the Vic. gold-
fields—and he kept it.
“The System.” Extract from a Sydney
newspaper, 1826 : “A coroner’s inquest was held
m the county gaol, on Thursday last, on the body
of John Murphy, a capital convict. The unfor-
tunate man was condemned to die 19 weeks ago
for a robbery committed in the Maoquarie-place
residence of Dr. Warded, ever since which period
he has been confined in a small cell, on a chain,
on a scanty allowance of bread and water, and
lingered from day'to day tdl the sentence was
slowly carried into execution, to which effect a
verdict was returned.” And that is less than 70
years ago.
The Vic. Audit Office is known among
civil servants as the, “Deathtrap.” It is on the
top floor of the old Treasury, right under the roof.
Its roofs are low-ceilinged and ill- ventilated, and the
passages are exposed to the currents of air rushing
up the stair-shafts, so that, what with stuffi-
ness in summer and draughtiness in winter, it
is in season a veritable stinkhole and freezing-
chamber. These influences, combined with the
daily climbing up and down stairs, have struck
many clerks with consumption. Five of these
have died during as many years. It was at one
time proposed to abandon the place to the ghosts
of these poor fellows, but the retrenchment policy
intervened, and a few pounds are going to be
spent on improving it instead.
The other day the Scotch express ran off
the rails, and among the unlucky passengers were
a Mrs. Macgregor, who got a leg fractured, and
her sister, killed on the spot. Thereupon the
cable liar—having been recently reprimanded,
perhaps, for not putting enough Australian colour
into his work—wired the information that Mrs.
Macgregor was a Ballarat lady, and her late
lamented sister was suspected of coming from the
same city. A Melbourne journal with fatuous
imaginativeness bestowed the name of “Miss
Macgregor” upon the dead relative, which led one
to suppose that Macgregor, of Ballarat, had mar-
ried one of his cousins on the paternal side. Any-
way, it seemed clear that Australia could take
credit from this catastrophe for Mrs. M.’s broken
limb, and her family connection with the corpse.
Then came a further cable message stating that
Mrs. Macgregor was an ordinary British female
as might have been expected. She didn’t come
from Ballarat. It is more than likely that nobody
in the train, or at St. Neots, where the accident
happened, had ever heard of Ballarat. The pro-
bable fact of the matter is that the cable-liarist
met friend Duncan Gillies as he was leaving his
A.G. office at about 12.45 p.m., after a tiring
day’s work, and having showed him a list of the
victims names, said : “Do any of these sound
Australian?” And Gillies replied: “ Weel I
remember a Mrs. Macgregor at Ballarat.” Then
the purveyor of European intelligence with Aus-
tralian color in it rushed away to despatch his
melancholy fiction.
“ Wallaby is the native word for kan-

S.G. : “ From the scientific point of view,
Lola Monfcez must be looked upon as a neurotic
degenerate; her lying, vanity and pretence as a
child, her temper, instability, alcoholism, liaisons
as a woman, her lapse to what can only be con-
sidered as religious mania, miscalled penitence,
just before her end, all point to this conclusion.
Her brilliance, her personal magnetism, her
genius, it might almost be called, tell the same
tale of an unsound brain.”
The Far Western (N.S.W.) landholder is
sending up his little supplication just now that
God, having neglected thus long to answer the
prayers for rain offered up by the pious ones on
both sides of him, may be pleased to hold the
rain off for a couple of months or so—until he
gets his land inspected for the purposes of the
re-appraisement of rent. A dozen special
appraisers are now out in the West, including E.
B. L. Dickens, and as both drought and rabbits
are to be taken into account, and as the rent now
to be determined upon is to stand for a minimum
of seven years, it is obvious that the drought and
the rabbit-plague, now particularly severe, are
not an unmixed curse. Anyhow, if men will
occupy that far-back country and pay decent
wages, the rent cannot well be too low. By the
way, apropos Mr. Dickens’ qualifications as an
appraiser, queried lately, Dickens was manager of
Mt. Murchison (Momba Pastoral Co.) 18 years
ago, and has ever since been in the stock and
agency business. He was a Crown appraiser
under the former Act.
Henry Lawson’s book, which may be
called “Block and Backblocks: Australian Verses
by Henry Lawson,” or something of that sort, will
be out shortly and will, like “ Banjo’s ” (orders
for which are rapidly flowing in), be a handsome
volume printed in new type on fine paper. The
price will be ss. 6d., post free from this office, and
The Bulletin, after seeing the material of which
the book is composed, has no hesitation in saying
that it deserves to be, and will be, an immediate
and permanent success.
Mr. Nathan Herman, the proprietor of the
Misfit Parlor in Wynyard-square, Sydney, will be
remembered in that city for many years as the
man who struck a new idea. In every country
where man is sufficiently civilised to have his
clothes and boots made to order, there is neces-
sarily a large accumulation of garments which
have been made to fit the frame of particular pur-
chasers, who didn’t call for them, or couldn’t pay
for them, or died, or absconded, or went
mad, or got into gaol before delivery, and
so left them on the maker’s hands. And
in every case there is someone else about
whom the rejected article would fit, for
everybody’s clothes —except those of Dick Dead-
eye and the Hunchback of Notre Dame —fit
somebody else, and this person would be glad to
get a first-class garment at a reduced price, and
the builder of it would be pleased to get rid of it
at almost any sacrifice. The difficulty always has
been to bring the misfit and the man it will fit
together, and Mr. Herman solved the problem by
accumulating in one place misfits from all
the ends of the earth, so that everybody who
can possibly be fitted by human ingenuity
may go there and be satisfied. He is
also a universal provider—a dealer in safes,
crockery, furniture, bedding, carpets, linoleums,
oil-cloths, drapery, jewellery, watches, cigars,
tobacco, tea (401 b. chest for 30s. or 121 b. tin for
10s.), cutlery, fancy goods, ironmongery, &c., &c.
But the misfit idea was his own special inspira-
tion, and he is proud of it. Even the famous
£2 bale, which contains a suit of clothes, two
shirts, two undershirts, six pairs socks, two ties,
two collars, six handkerchiefs, set studs, hat, pair
braces and pair of Loots, all sent to the railway
station or wharf nearest to the buyer’s residence
for 405., doesn’t appeal to his soul like that great
original idea. Mr. Herman has lately broken out
into pianos, firearms, ammunition, and other new
departments, and makes srentlemen’s suits for
£1 9s. 6d., and supplies dress-suits at one-fourth
the usual price. Also, he has broken out into so
many other lines of business of late that no one
knows for certain what he has got, and the things
that he hasn’t got could mostly, be enumerated on
the back of one postage stamp, and even then the
stamp wouldn’t be unduly crowded.*

At Perth (Scotland) a man lately got 20
days’ gaol for pounding his wife with a kitchen
fender bearing the legend in brass letters,
“ Home, Sweet Home ! ”
A certain Australian Q.C. was once de-
fending a co-respondent in divorce, and it was
doubtful if the jury could be brought to take a
lenient view. They were evidently halting
between two opinions, and the Judge’s summing-
up would probably settle the matter. This sum-
ming-up the Q.C. strongly suspected would be
adverse to his client, so he rose to the occasion.
He knew that the Judge carried in his most
fleshy part a charge of shot as the result of a
youthful amour. So he perorated thus: “If
petitioner’s story were true, why had he not acted
as an angry man would ? I know a case in which
a vile scoundrel, a dishonorable scoundrel, an un-
speakable villain, a disgrace to humanity and to
his profession, was detected in his intrigues, and
the injured party put a lot of small shot into him,
which he is sitting on at this day. Did petitioner
show any such signs of having learned a shameful
secret?” and so on. The Judge got wild, and
summed up, as calculated, so bitterly and so un-
fairly against the co-respondent that the jury
revolted against the judicial directions out of
natural combativeness, and found a verdict
accordingly.
John Francis Donovan is the third shunte 1 '
killed at Perth railway-station in six months, as a
result of the Westralian Government’s parsi-
mony. At the inquest an assistant ■ shunter
deposed: “Saw Donovan when he came on.
Donovan said he was tired out and full-up of the
job.” Witness had not seen shunting done by
one man until he came to Westralia, and he had
had 17 years experience. “In Victoria, usually
three men attended to the shunting and there
were eight-hour shifts. If there had been another
man an accident might have been averted.” The
driver of the shunting-engine said to Donovan,
just before the accident, “ I suppose you are very
nearly tired out.” He said, “Yes, I am, I have
been working pretty nearly all day single-
handed.” Deceased said when witness came up
to him, after the accident, “Ah, Jimmy,
this is the result of working short-handed.”
The coroner: “This man had a great deal
to do there was evidence that he was
thoroughly fagged out.” The jury strongly
condemned the system of overworking the em-
ployes, and gave it as its opinion that (1) There
were not sufficient men to do the work, and (2)
the hours of labor were too long for the protec-
tion of the travelling public and the employes.
Writes S.M. Herald’s special correspon-
dent from Liverpool :
The ordinary business man of Britain lias about as
much sympathy for “his dear brother,” “his own people
on the other side of the worid, his own flesh and blood,
held to England by that unseverable silken tie” (expres-
sions frequently heard from public platforms, especially
Australian ones), as the ordinary brick of commerce.
There is used here a brick with a vitrified glassy face,
smooth as a tile. That is the brick which meets this case.
It has a smooth face, but a very bard one. . . . Such
a man will trade with America or Armenia, just as he
will with Australia. The smallest fraction, say the
thirty-second part of a farthing, will turn the scale in
favor of the country which offers it. Sentiment is per-
haps necessary to the Imperial Institute, but it doesn’t
exist in the trade centres of London, Liverpool, Man-
chester, or many other places I have visited.
In other words, the sole use of the “silken tie”
and the “crimson thread ” is to make webs in
which British spiders may lie low and trap un-
wary Australian flies. Just what The Bulletin
has pointed out for the last 16 years.

Again last night the house was crowded to its
legal capacity to witness this the strangest and
most absorbing of all modern Dramas
The Silence of Dean Maitland.
By LEWIS SCOTT.
SYNOPSIS OF SCENERY AND
INCIDENTS:
Prologue, Scene I.—The village of Melbourne.
Outside the Blue Boar. Ben Lee on the
Track. Killing of Ben Lee.
TrT>TT ACT 1.-THe“ VICAR A.GE.
Cyril Maitland's return home.
Presents for everybody. “You are as popular,
kind, and thoughtful as you are eloquent.” The
absence of Henry Everard. Remorse of Cyril
Maitland. His attempted confession. Vision of
Henry Everard in his prison cell.
ACT 2.—THE CHURCHYARD.
Lee’s Tombstone. The Bishop makes
Cyril Maitland Dean of Belrninster.
ACT 3.-THE DEANERY.
The Doctor’s Revenge. Everard free from
prison. His interview with Lilian Young. BEN
LEE ARRIVES FROM AMERICA. Dean
Maitland disowns him. He threatens the Dean.
Maitland’s interview with his blind son. Alma
Lees death. “The only witness of my crime
dead. ” Dean Maitland is offered the Bishopric of
Belrninster. Dean Maitland remains silent. “The
only witness of my sin dead. It is an omen from
Heaven.”
ACT 4—T?HE GOOD PEOPLE ON THEIR
WAY TO HEAR DEAN MAITLAND
PREACH.
BELMINSTER CATHEDRAL. The Choir.
Ihe Congregation. The Choir sings “ Adeste
Eideles.’ Dean Maitland confesses, and expiates
his crime at the foot of the altar. “My God !
My God ! My punishment is greater than I can

Crowded on Saturday afternoon and evening,
when a long and diversified programme, in which
artists of repute took part, was gone through in
right excellent style. In the afternoon at the
matinee a large number of children appeared to
thoroughly enjoy themselves. . . . The pro-
gramme, which was composed of songs and
ballads, medleys and dancing, and comic perform-
ances generally, proved well-merited applause,
ihe artists were re-called again and again. The
trourlays acted with great spirit and were vigor-
ously applauded.” B

Sunday Times, Nov. 24, ’95.
The latest addition to Sydney variety shows
has undoubtedly hit public taste, and last night
the house was filled in every part, the fact that
several well-known first-class artists had been
added to the. company proving an additional
attraction. In the first part, Miss Nellie
btntch, who made her first appearance, ren-
dered I was Dreaming ” in such a pleasing
manner as to at once make her a fast favorite with
the audience, who recalled her several times, and
she finally responded with ‘ Killarney.’ In their
farce, ‘ lhat Awful Girl,’ the Gourlays were seen
at their best; Miss Gourlay singing and dancing
with the vivacity for which she is noted ; and Mr.
Gourlay keeping the audience in roars of laughter.
Miss Cora Bailey soon danced herself into favor,
and there can be no question about her cleverness. '
The Bulletin, Nov. 30
“A Rood business is still doing at the Sydney
Music Hall, and sundry new artists turn up each
week to lend variety to the bill. The harmless,
necessary corner-man gets some new ideas now
and then, and aims queer conundrums at the man
m the centre, and some good singers and dancers
brighten up the proceedings, but nothing very
dreadful happens. . . Miss Amy Gourlay and her
brother Bill have joined the co., and make it a
good deal stronger than it was before, and nothing
in the bill calls for particular execration at present.

A deposit receipt sale in connection with
ot. James’s Building Society, Melbourne, is an-
nounced in another column for 12th December,
when a number of valuable properties in the
Southern city and its suburbs will be submitted
to the public. Deposit-receipts in the Society
will be accepted in payment at their face value. Of
course, as is usually the case, the buyers attend-
ing this first sale will get the cream of the pro-
perties to be sold. Messrs. Keogh and Allard,
acting with Gemmell, Tuckett and Co., are con-
dupttng the sale.*
An interesting announcement to printers,
stationers and investors is made elsewhere by
Messrs. Danby, Butler, and Fischer, the premier
accountants of Melbourne. Owing, no doubt, to
the stress of the recent disastrous times in the
Southern province, the enormous business of Fer-
gusson and Mitchell, wholesale printers and
stationers, has fallen into liquidation; the stock
sheets tot up £37,550, and Messrs. Danby, Butler,
and Fischer are prepared to receive tenders for
the estate up to 12th December.*
Xmas is a religious festival at which it is
everybody s duty to eat and drink more than is
good for him, and if he doesn’t want to aggravate
the indigestible character of the occasion it is
essential that he should get good liquor. That is
to say, he should get it from Mr. P. R. Larkin’s
old-established emporium, 778-782 George-street,
Sydney. Mr. Larkin’s price-list is an imposing
document of 12 pages, containing the names and
prices or some 240 different liquors. P. R. Larkin
himself is a guarantee of good feeling as well as
good liquor towards all mankind. The fact that
there are 29 different varieties of old Scotch
whisfky alone on his list is a thing for the
W.C.T.U. to ponder over. Twenty nine kinds of
Scotch whisky and only one kind of teetotalism 1
The newest thing in Xmas cards is the
card, issued by George Robertson and
Co., of 361 George-street, Sydney. It is exceed-
ingly artistic, is a new and purely Australian
idea, and reproduces all the delicate shades of the
emu egg to perfection. In other departments the
firm is making a great effort for the holiday
season, and has laid in an enormous stock of
travelling-cases, jewel and dressing-cases, desks,
photo-albums, cases and frames, gold and silver-
mounted purses, card and letter-cases, wallets,
pocket-books, cigar and cigarette-cases in all kinds
of leather and all variety of new colors. The
firm is also strong on toy books, presentation
books, children’s books, miscellaneous books, and
books generally, artistic stationery, all manner of
Xmas and New Year cards, and innumerable
other items of miscellaneous gorgeousness.
LONDON WOOL-MARKET VERSUS AUS-
TRALIAN SALES.
In this important controversy the National
Wool Company, of Melbourne, commenting upon
it, remarks : “Of course, Australian sales are not
regarded favorably by those whose interest it is
to centralise all trade in London, but at the same
time there are cogent reasons why Australians
should insist on disposing of their raw products
in their own trade centres. In fact, it is the
decentralisation already existent, in our opinion,
which at various times has saved the market from
utter collapse. Were the wool products of Aus-
tralia, Argentina, and the Cape, all disposed of
in London, sales might be held continuously all
the year round, manufacturers would supply
themselves from day to day, adopt a hand to
mouth policy, no stocks would be held, and prices
would drop to zero. The conditions which neces-
sitate the holding of stocks are those which tend
to hold up the market. Manufacturers holding
stocks will be led by the strongest motives of self-
interest to see that their trade rivals do not buy
cheaper than they have bought, hence there is a
permanent stimulus to give prices an upward
tendency. Wool-buyers operating here keep that
speculative element alive; had it not been so, the
recent low range of values might have been lower
still. Again, while we feed the London market
at the expense of the Australian, we subsidise the
London middlemen to the yearly tune of .£700,000
for commission and warehousing.” Concluding
their remarks, the National Wool Company asks
the question : “Is it not Australia’s policy to
give all possible employment to her own people,
and particularly when this course is the course of
self-interest as well?
Messrs. Burns and Bright (whose names
probably induced them to go into the lamp-trade
together), specialists in lamps and stoves, have
opened a warehouse at 148 Pitt-street, Sydney,
wiiere, if they don’t get the entire trade of N.S.W.,
they propose at least to deserve it. They deal in
every article in their line, but their speciality—
the culminating point of their ambition and prin-
cipal source of pride and satisfaction—is the
Australis Stove, which combines more strong
points than any other single stove in the market.
All the points are controlled by one damper. It
burns wood or coal and answers equally well in-
doors or outside, requires no setting, and has a
jam-making apparatus in addition to the other
ordinary conveniences of a first-class stove. B.
and B. also deal in table and hall decorations and
lamps of every description for every purpose, bub
their latest and newest feature is a kerosene night-
light, which costs just 3s. 4d. for 200 hours’ burn-
ing, or one-fifth of a penny per hour.
The big firm of Gowing Bros., men’s and
boys’ mercers, clothiers, outfitters, and hatters,
established in Sydney 27 years, has taken over
the extensive premises at 486, 488 and 490 George-
street, lately occupied by Cecil Hordern. The
new establishment is the best-lighted and most com-
modious of its kind in the Southern Hemisphere,
and faces the central entrance of the coming new
markets. Buying at first-hand from the largest
manufacturers for cash, Gowing Bros, are pre-
pared to dress the men and boys of this country
at a cost within easy reach of everyone, be he the
richest or the poorest. The firm particularly
draws attention to the 455. suit, and stakes its
reputatiomon it being the best thing in this line
on the Australian market. Patterns and self-
measurement forms are sent post free, so the bush-
man has only himself to blame if he doesn’t turn
out to mash his Sunday-girl in a suit which would
ingratiate him with the most fashionable girl in
the city. A man well-dressed commends himself
to the whole world, and nowadays Gowing Bros,
have solved the way for a well-dressed world to
be continuously pleasing itself. #
Melb. Argus often tries to joke nowadays,
but once upon a time it never permitted a smile
in its columns, The-fjrst joke that ever appeared
in an Argus leader happened—that is the word —
some years ago, just after Mrs. Campbell-Praed
and Justin McCarthy published their novel,
“T ie Right Honorable.” The writer, in a mood
of after-dinner friskiness,said : “This book, ‘The
Rig it Honorable,’ is the work of a man and a
vvo .i oi most Right Honorables are.” The thing
civ te l such a stir that it was long ere any other
memoer of the staff ventured to laugh in office-
hours.
