A Pretorian (South Africa) “ apology ” :
I, Petrus Daniel Struweg, residing on the farm Drie-
hoek, district of Marthinus, Wesselstroom, S.A.R.,.declare
herewith that the evidence which I gave in the Court at
Marthinus Wesselstroom on the 21st August, 1895, under
my oath in the case of N. D. Coetzer versus The Widow
Herbst was false.
I confess hereby, in the presence of seven persons that
everything that I said against Messrs. J. Meek and 0. Vos
to the detriment of their honor and character is nothing
but lies and not true, and I say that, henceforth, I shall
live as a liar, and if, at any future time, I shall say any-
thing to the detriment of any other person, they shall
have the right to shut my lying mouth, and further I am
willing to have this apology published three times in
some newspapers, and I shall pay the charges therefor.
As witnesses, &c.
Mrs. Kilpatrick’s famous case against the
owners of the lost steamer Alert has got a step
further. That lady’s husband was second en-
gineer of the cranky steamer, which went down
off the Victorian coast one day in Dee. ’93, and
carried him along with it to the sea-bottom. She
claimed damages from the owners, and, in Feb.
’95, the broken-winded law overtook the case, and
after much expensive argument awarded her .£6OO,
on the ground that the Alert wasn’t a proper and
seaworthy vessel and fit for the work in which
she was engaged. If it had been a hanging or a
flogging that was in question the law would have
got at the case a lot earlier, and its decision would
have been carried out in a month at the latest,
but Property was at stake, and in these cases
the law goes very slowly indeed. A new trial was
applied for and granted, and then the law spent
nine months getting its second wind. At last it
arrived with the violent energy of a snail in
ballast, and threw itself bodily into the ease a
second time, and after wading through another
ocean of argument it emerged at the other side,
this time for a verdict of .£791, or .£l9l more than
the first one. The widow, who has been living in
poverty for two long years awaiting the painful
gradualness of the courts, will now probably get
her money unless it is all gone in costs, or unless
a few more trials ensue. Even Sleep gets old and
cobwebbed while awaiting the final decision of the
law in a case where Property is involved.
Yoski, the Japanese murderer and suicide,
of Thursday Island (writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent) was a constant terror to the colored popula-
tion, and his country people in particular. At
the local Yoshiwara his demands for amatory in-
dulgence were promptly supplied, as they were
usually accompanied by a display of his revolver.
Before the establishment of local sittings of the
higher court little escapades of this kind were
usually kept quiet, to avoid a trip toCooktown at,
perhaps, the one good season of the year; but, in
such suppressed cases, the boss of the station
adjudicated, and his verdict was never questioned
nor its rigor abated. Yoski once deliberately
shot a countryman, one of his crew; but as
the wound, though extremely painful, didn’t
promise to be mortal, he was let off with
an unmerciful trouncing administered by the
Judge in person, and a fine of £25 by way of
a solatium to the wounded—the hush-money to
be a first lien on his earnings. Whenever, during
the currency of the promise, Yoski got angry and
looked devilish his creditor would say, “ Very
well, I go long Thursday Island, tell pliseman ; ”
but on his subsiding into a peaceful mood the
victim would add, “All right, I take it that
£25.” While anchored in his lugger on the shell-
ing-grounds, Yoski saw, some little distance off,
a man pulling by in a dingey. He hailed him ;
but, as the puller took no notice, Yoski reached
into his cabin for his Winchester and coolly fired
■point blank at him half-a-dozen shots, two of
which went through the boat’s side—fore and aft
its astonished occupant. Thinking it time to
surrender, the dingey came alongside, when
Yoski, with a profound salaam, explained : “ Ex-
coose me, my friend, I think it that dingey belong
me.” With the death of one of his victims (Mrs.
Tommy Japan) closes another chapter of the Car-
bine sweep history. The winning ticket was
owned by a syndicate of Japs, in which Tommy
held the lion’s share, and drew about .£7OOO. He
very soon went home, where he invested largely in
business, and promptly “ went down for his
stuff.” Soon after he died, and his widow re-
turned here a few months ago. Yoski wanted
her to marry him ; but she refused —hence her
sudden exit from life’s stage. The other victim,
Fujetta Tatsu, was the best tattooed man I ever
saw.

Alex. Sutherland, a Melb. poet and peda-
gogue, says ha despises American iiutnor. It is,
tie remarks, extravagant and outrages facts. Mr.
Sootherlan’ is reported to be preparing a series of
lectures which will introduce an uneducated
public to a school of humor that is humor. As a
Scotchman (or, at any rate, a person of Scotch
descent) Alexawnder should be specially qualified
to do this. Scotchmen are said to “joke wi’
deefficulty,’ but that is, no doubt, because they
are conscientious and joke with the fear of God
in their hearts, instead of throwing off their jests
in the spontaneous, slipshod fashion of humorists
who have no sense of responsibility.
CnfcTiett Walker, the popular N.S.W,
Principal Under-Secretary, whom admirers have
placed in the sculptor’s hands, entered the N.S.W.
Civil Service at the age of 15, and has stayed
there almost 40 years. He ordinarily claims to be
a Tasmanian, though in reality he was born at
sea, somewhere in the vicinity of the Cape of
C°od Hope. The title “Principal,” prefixed to
his official designation, was the coinage of his pre-
decessor, the poet Halloran, and was countenanced
out of courtesy ; and many suppose that courtesy
alone sanctions it still. But Mr. Walker, noticing
this in a newspaper some years ago, wrote point-
ing out that the title is his by official gazette
authority.

SEEST- WILLIAM SULLIVAN-
Sergeant William Sullivan is up to
something—he is up to the wall, upright
to the wall, right up to the upright wall.
It is a queer position for a real live ser-
geant ; it looks a painful one, but he does
not show his face—that would tell a tale.
He is laughing, although he is straining
every nerve to keep his equilibrium. It
is not because Sergeant Sullivan is a
giddy man that he makes this attempt to
lean sideways straight up to the wall.
Neither does he try it because there
is no convenient lamp - post to em-
brace. The fact is, he was offered
Five Pounds if he could do it, and, as
the picture shows, he attained the
desired position, but he didn’t attain the
desired Prize, for we were inquisi-
tive, and found out the Old Soldier
dodge. He had a nail on his finger-
an iron one —and driven into the wall.
That wasn’t in the piece, so Sergeant
Sullivan retired, and he says, “You
can’t pull the breeches off a High-
lander, and you can’t do that—it’s an
impossible motion.” Now, readers, the
proprietor of Beecham’s Pills invites you
and all your friends to place your right
side right up to an upright wall, every
portion of your side from ankle to the
shoulder being in contact with the wall.
Then attempt to raise the left foot from
the ground while the right side of the
body is still in contact with the wall. If
there is no fun to be got out of this
experiment, you are in a bad way, and
we feel it our bounden duty to remind
you that for ensuring buoyant spirits,
and the capacity to enjoy life to the
full, BEECHAM’S BILLS are
Worth a Guinea a Box!

“ Tales of the Convict System.”
The working-classes are represented on
the Council of Melb. Workingmen’s College by
one delegate only. The control of the college has
practically passed out of their hands.
Says Rosebery : “ There are two happy
epochs—ouo the acceptance, and the other the
resignation of the seals of office.” An allegory :
Engagement and divorce.
A State schoolmaster has a few leaderettes
from Melb. Herald cut out and framed, as shock-
ing examples in the art of composition. The boys
get niarks for picking out the grammatical blun-
ders. Journalism evidently has its educational
uses..
“ A WOMAN IN THE GASEF
Dr. J. J. Miller is trying hard to induc-
Vio. Govt, to compel a regular veterinary inspec-
tion of dairy herds for tuberculosis, and will pro-
bably win in the end. In Queensland, recently,
out of a herd of 150 cattle from the best grazing
grounds, all but 25 had tuberculosis more or less
badly.
Dewan : Re “shamming Abraham ; ” last
century Abraham was cashier of the Bank of
England for 60 years, and his name became a
synonym for the notes which he signed. Hence,
to “sham Abraham” was originally to counterfeit
a bank-note : then to counterfeit anything ; now
(chiefly) to counterfeit illness.
Cancer is also to be attacked by serum-
therapy, it appears; the serum to be from an
animal in which erysipelas has been induced arti-
ficially in a mild form. And the blood serum of
the horse is to be used as a remedy for tuber-
culosis, it being stated that he is naturally im-
mune from that disease.
Prof. Liversidge, of Sydney University,
has found that a brick soaked in oil does not lose
the absorbed oil, and is absolutely water-proof.
Anyhow, bricks so soaked weighed exactly the
same,.after four years’exposure to the weather, as
when taken out of the oil. But sandstone, soaked
in oil, weighed the same, after four years’ weather-
ing, as before it was placed in the oil. Neverthe-
less, curious to relate, though the oil had thus
apparently left it, the stone remained impervious
to water.
The tea-cup storm re accountant Col. Tem-
pleton and his bossing of the precious Vic. army
whilst the Imperial Commandant went picnicking
in another province, seems to have blown over
at the instance of Gov. Brassey. Anyway, the
local officer suddenly ceased to defy the other
gaudy gentleman, and next night Brassey jumped
at a passing opportunity to drag the name of Mrs.
Colonel Templeton, “ with honor and gratitude,”
into a little speech. Templeton, presumably, had
knuckled down to oblige Government House.
Item from the Investors’ Review (Lon-
don) :
Within the last quarter of a century, to go no further
back, vva have spent nearly ±800,000,000 on our army and
navy, including the cost of little wars not paid for by the
Indian ryot—patient animal that he is, fat votes of credit
for the delectation of our bureaucracy, and such odd-
ments. And the national revenue from drink has in the
game period of time come to within about ±120,000,000 of
this overwhelming sura. The drunkards, therefore, have
been the true guardians of our empire and upholders of
the brotherly goodwill which subsists between nations,
and gets itself emphasised at all military parades and
naval shows throughout Europe. Behold then the
secret of our greatness! If the stability of our
empire had depended on the income-tax or on a
poll-tax, on a land-tax, on “inhabited house duty,” or on
“ss. a quarter upon imported grain,” or on all these
together, it would have long since come to nought. Rest-
ing on our magnificent drinking capacity, it has not only
endured but increased in splendor unto this hour—witness
the array and navy estimates passim . Prom this point of
view we have always regarded the drunkard as the
noblest of national benefactors and greatest mainstay of
the constitution. Upon the highest grounds of imperial
interests, then, we plead for the liberty of all Englishmen
to get drunk as often as seems to them good. Their noble
thirst brings the Exchequer and the local rates even now
a good ±34,000,000 a year.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
Undoubtedly the largest buyer of opals in the
rough state is H. Newman, the well-known Mel-
bourne jeweller, who has been connected with
this branch of the gem trade since its inception,
and keeps a large staff of skilled lapidaries busily
employed on these and other Australian gems.
He is also a large buyer of pearls and diamonds,
and gets through an immense quantity of Queens-
land sapphires. The firm has been established
for over 40 years, and has a reputation for straight
dealing which is sufficient guarantee to con-
signors of rough gems that they will be honorably
dealt with. The establishment is still at the old
spot in Elizabeth-street, right opposite the Post
Office, and is well worthy of a visit of inspection.*
Dear Bulletin, —The black plague which,
at Hongkong, last year, killed 2000 people between
June and Aug., illustrates what might happen if
hordes of Chinese are permitted to settle in Aus-
tralia. The disease was a kind of black typhus,
and produced swellings in fTie groins and armpits,
being entirely similar to the black plague of
London, 300 years ago. It was caused by the
filthy habits of the low-class Chinese coolie, and
was stamped out by the heroic conduct of some
of the European residents, who attended to the
burial of the bodies, burning of the effects of the
diseased people, and whitewashing of their hovels.
About 30 of these volunteer-workers took the
plague-fever themselves, but all recovered save
two. — The.ldler. Hongkong, Sept. 30, ’95.
Bards of the Backblocks .
THE COUNTRY BRAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Six long years’ battling in the bush is not so very
gay,
And so [ chucked the job and took once more the
Sydney way.
I knew, of course, my style was not exactly up-to-
date,
And so I thought I’d make a change—in hats, at
any rate.
The old slouch hat I’d had for years I thought
would make folks smile,
And so I made a big resolve to have the latest
style;
And when I got as far as Bourke—the first big
town you see—
I got the latest:thing they had—it was a cabbage-
tree.
But when I got to Dubbo —why the folks began to
grin—
Said they, “Bourke saw him coming, and they
took this bushy in.”
This broke me up—l bought in place of my new
cabbage-tree
A Panama with turban round—a gorgeous thing
to see.
That cady suited me to rights—l felt ’tvvas just
the thing—
I felt as good as Tyson feels, and better than a
king,
Until at Bathurst I arrived, and there my beer
was flat,
I felt .I had the bushy brand still plainly in my
hat.
I bought a hat of alpine shape—l tell you it was
great,
Said I : “At last I’ve got a hat that’s really up-
to-date.”
Alas ! when I to Sydney got, a news-boy said to
me—
“Hi, bushy, bow’s the sheep out-back? you’re on
a fine old spree !”
Now, here’s the truth about the bush from my
own point of view :
It grips a man at every point and soaks him
through and through.
It draws his legs out thin and long and knocks
his knees together ;
It works until it makes his face a sign-post of the
weather.
It makes him long and lank as well, and gives him
slouching shoulders ;
It gives him, too, a camel walk to paralyse be-
holders.
So, when you come to Sydney town, don’t change
your rig on landing—
No clothes that ever yet were made can hide the
country branding.
ZEF.
WHEN BROTHER, PEETREE PRAYED.
A RECOLLEOTION.
’Tvvas a sleepy little chapel by a wattled hill
erected.
Where the storms were always muffled, and an
atmosphere of peace
Hung about beneath the gum-trees, and the gar-
den was respected
By the goats from Billybunga and the washer-
woman’s geese.
In the week-days it was sacred to my young
imagination ;
From its waljsjthere oozed a sentiment of rever-
ence profound ;
And on Sunday morns the murmuring of the
childish congregation
Seemed to spread a benediction in the bush-
land far around.
But when Brother Peetree prayed all the parrots
flew dismayed,
And the hill shook to its centre, and the trees and
fences swayed ;
And we youngsters heard the rumble of the Day
of Judgment there,
When the pious superintendent wrestled manfully
in prayer.
They were horny-handed Methodists and men of
scanty knowledge,
Who controlled that “ little corner of the vine-
yard ” by the pound ;
Their theology was not the kind that’s warranted
at college,
But their faith was most abundant, and their
gospel always sound.
Brother Peetree was a miner at the Band of Hope.
His leisure
He employed in “sticking porkers” for his
neighbors, and his skill
Was a theme of admiration; but his soul’s sub-
limest pleasure
Was to speak a prayer on Sundays in the chapel
’neath the hill.
Froze the marrow in our bones at the sound of
hollow groans,
And the shrieks of moral anguish, and the awful
thunder tones;
And we saw the Hell-fire burning, and we smelt
it in the air,
When dear Brother Peetree struggled with the
Lord of Hosts in prayer.
Brother Peetree always started with a murmured
supplication,
Knelt beside a form, serenely, with a meek,
submissive face,
But he rose by certain stages to a rolling exhorta-
tion.
And a wild, ecstatic bellowing for sanctity and
grace ;
And he threw his arms to heaven, and the seats
went down before him
As he fought his way along the aisle, and prayed
with might and main.
With hysterical hesceohings. Then a sudden
peace fell o’er him,
And he finished, softly sobbing, at his starting-
point again.
And the elders, to their ears pale with reverential
fears,
And the sisters and the choir indulged in hot,
repentant tears ;
And the sinners for salvation did with eagerness
declare.
When beloved Brother Peetree wrestled mightily
in prayer.
EDWARD DYSON.

An Unknown Interlude,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The room in which they sat showed that
they were quite well-to-do. The chairs and floor
were richly carpeted. The brass on the fender
and coal-scuttle was bright and massive, the
pictures choice originals. But in all the room
there was hardly any comfort. The fire had gone
low, and although it was six o’clock in winter, the
gas had not been lighted.
Presently out of the silence there came the
sound of the woman yawning, and in the yawn
there was that depth of laziness belonging only to
people whose dispositions are wholly steeped in
sloth.
“ The fault was yours.” It was the man speak-
ing. “The fault was entirely yours. If you had
not spoilt her, it would never have happened.”
“You did not take too much interest in her.
It was not because of that, that she went away.
It is of no use yottr being disagreeable to me.
Who could tell that s e was so deceitful? ”
A young man came into the room. He was
their son; Neither took any notice of him. He
crossed the roo n and lighted the gas, and as he
did so a knock came to the door.
The two looted at each other, and the next
instant saw the boy dart towards the door. A
moment lalcr he returned. He was bearing some-
thing in his arms.
“ 1 knew it! ’ cried the woman, “ I knew it! ”
and that was all she said.
Then her daughte ’s eyes, those beautiful star-
like eyes, looked wonderingly at her. And as
they looke 1, so full of anguish were they, that
they seemed half hidden by a mist.
The next moment the girl fell forward.
* # •
It was eighteen months since she had sud-
denly left the house ; what had happened to her
since then ?
“ He has, of course, deserted her,” said the
wiman. “That is quite evident.”
“ >She had better die,” she was thinking in her
mind. “A woman disgraced! She had much
better die.”
“I always told you how it would end,” said
the husband. “ Licentio isness was graven on his
face. Yet you encouraged him to come to the
house.”
She was used to this abuse, but to-night she
turned white, and, shutting the door with a bang,
s ie left the apartment.
• # #
During the days that passed she haunted
the girl’s room. Restless and eiger, with a
passion for cleta’l, before death came into the
h mse she must know all that had occurred. In
her mind she saw the man that had taken away
her child, drinking, gambling, and making love to
women. To the verdict of her husband she had
a Ided two vices more. There were endless tales
of vice and injury lurking between her and her
daughter. Surely when at last the girl should
s. eak the very walls would tremble with horror !
But she never spoke ; only shuddered once or
twice as the wo nan’s step approached.
#' • #
She was dying.
A movement of suspense passed over the mother
sitting beside the bed. Could she be going to
oie without a word ?
For days the mother had been much happier.
Her daughter had spoken sometimes to her, and
had seemed to listen in return wth interest. At
least, had she not seen he smiling !
It was t ue. She smiled at what she saw in her
own mind—the world that had been hors after she
left home. She would always carry it with her —
even into the grave.
• # «
Her mother lifted her body in his arms.
His mother screamed. “I am going to lay her
at his side,” he saio, and bore her from the room.
His mother went back to the bed.
There, on the white sheets, lay a wedding-ring
clasping firmly a piece of something black.
Sue turned ve y red. Her husband said, “It
is a piece of ploth ” —he paused—” from a pall
and he trembled.
I hat was all they ever learned of their child.
“Edward will, tell us all.” Her eyes were
moist with happy tears. “ Edward will tell us all
when he comes - back. How strange ! How
strange ! ” F
But he did not o&mtTback.

H. L. SCOULLAR.
Didn’t Run Smooth,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A close, sleepy summer evening. A drowsy hum
fro n the interior of a village church, wherein the
worthy minister was just reaching his five-and-
twentieth minute of a discourse on the alleged
fact that Joshua made the sun stand still. Out-
side, enjoying the calm sunset and the refreshing
coolness after the heat of the day, was the verger.
•Somehow, vergers rarely love sermons, but prefer
« pipe in the open. To him, escaping with relief
from the close dulness of the sacred edifice, spoke
a youth, “made in New South Wales.”
To gaze on such a lovely being was a feast of
b auty, to hear him speak a never-ending joy.
He stood, confessed “colonial’’-made, and shamed
t e sunset with the glow he wore. Baffled recoils
th ■ pen which dares attempt the picture of the
sp.endid thing he was. Only the pimples on his
N.d.ow cheek, marring his ample mouth’s expres-
sive lines, and the close-bitten nails upon the
1 aws which strove to hide behind his 9in. cuff,
1 .oked hardly fitting adjuncts to a glory which
s emed to shout upon the stilly night, “In this
style, eighteen bob ! ”
This noble product of our later age approached
the placid verger. “ ’Off long ” —thus spake he —
“ ’Ow long do the old geeser gen’ly work ’is
chin ? ”
The verger removed his pipe and shortly re-
plied, “About half-an-hour.”
“ Gawn ! ” exclaimed Adonis. And, in a
breath, he proceeded to unfold the burden of an
enterprise of love, miscarried through ill-chance.
“ A bloomin’gal,” this was his sad complaint—
“ a bloomin’gal I met this afternoon made it a
promise that I’d find ’er ’ere, sittun’ on the
seckind seat from the back. An’ now I carnt lay
’auds on ’er. Pre-aps you’d tell me if you’ve seen
a gal with yaller ’air, green ’at and scarlet feather,
a-sittun in this bloomin’ Weslee’n Church?”
The verger shook his head, but, in an off-hand
way, remarked that the sacred edifice before them
was labelled Anglican, and had no connection
with any other house in the trade.
“Is this th’ English Church?” Adonis cried,
as the crass error he had fallen into smote on his
mind with horrid force; “then, darn me, if I
’aven’t patronised the wrong blanky show 1 ”
And he rushed hotfooted away, seeking for the
fane where Love and Wesleyanism smiled and
waited for him.
DOLLY.
Youth,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was long after midnight and we were exchang-
ing confidences. Replying to me, the doctor said :
“ What is the happiest time of a man’s life ? My
happiest time was in my University days. I was
very poor, I worked very hard, and I was in love.
Perhaps love was the charm. And yet in after
time I have been in fiercest love and have enjoyed
comparative leisure and luxury. But no matter
about that —
“ It was a hard struggle for my parents to pay
for my education. I had pocket-money occasion-
ally. When my mother sold a few eggs she sent
me the proceeds. I can understand how religion
has its beginnings in ancestor-worship.
“ I remember one occasion when I was rich in
the possession of six shillings. I had written a
love-letter and was looking for a birthday
memento to enclose in it. The balance, after pay-
ing for the love-token, would be my only asset for
an unknown period. I inspected the contents of
the shop windows and of the book-shops to which
I had access, but all the articles of taste were
only for richer people than I. At last I hit upon
card the design of which was artistic, and which
contained a verse that was really poetry. I
looked and coveted, and with an effort 1 sum-
moned courage to ask the price. It was six shil-
lings. I paid the money and sent the card, and
returned to my dismal lodgings penniless, but
happy.’’
“ And the girl, doctor ? ”
The doctor made no reply.
WALA.
A Good Team,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They were discussing the horse-power of various
well-known bullock-teams in a pub. beyond
Mossyface, and I hung over some refreshment
and listened whilst the coach-driver doctored his
broken brake. A tall, spare young man, the
color of old bronze, with protruding knees, and
the gravity of a moulting mopoke, had taken up
the theme, and held the floor.
“’Taint no good a man tellin’halfer what he
knows about cattle,” he said, “ ’cos he don’t gain
nothin’ by it but the reputation of bein’ a
jumped-up liar. That’s my experience. But I’m
goin’ to tell you one little thing that’s worth
knowin’, bein’ able to prove it. Know that load’r
galvernised iron an’ things I took to Swift Creek
lars’ month ? Had ten ton up, an’ slush to the
hub right through. Well, I was crossin’ the
creek a few miles the other side Oassilis when I
got bogged. Had Bolliver an’ Briton leadin’ an’
Black Gin an’ Clipper in the pole, ten others
beside. S’pose you don’t deny my team can haul ?
Well, this burst they jest laid right down to it,
an’ pulled till you’d a-thought they’d yank the
crimson world all skew-wiff. I stirred ’em
up all I knew, an’ yelled like a maniac. It’s bad
business gettin’ stuck in that bit of creek. Pre-
sently I saw her coinin’, an’ I jest knocked hat-
fulls of hair offer them raw hides, an’ near bust
rneself cursin’. They brought her along beautiful
fer about fifty yards, an’ then I let up an’ called
spello. But when I turned round to take bearin’s,
strike me blind if there wasn’t that blanketty
blank waggon right there, jammed in the middle
of the gory creek. We hadn’t budged her a hinch.
I was knocked silly, right off me sanguinary
chump. Could ’a bet me life we’d shifted the load
a whole heap, an’ yet there it was in the same ole
shop, an’ the polers with their heels still in the
water. Well, I had another dig at it. Them red
uns of mine took a grip, an’ lifted with a long, low
heave that must ’a fetched this universe if some-
thin’ else hadn’t come. They was bringin’ her up
again beautiful, an’ I danced alongside fer a hun-
dred yards or so, an’ called on ’em till I was blue
in the face. Then we stood off agin an’ took

breath. Now, you can boil me whole if that gory
concern wasn’t stuck fast in the same spot—she
hadn’t budged outer the creek halfer foot. You
know me, I’m tough, but that thing made my
hair stick up stiff, an’ I fell a-tremblin’ like a kid.
Anyhow, 1 had another buck in at it after

comes, beautiful, all together, slow and sure.
Then I up an’ spotted what was happenin’.
Sure’s death we was towin’ that blanky creek

yards right out uv its course.”
The bullocky mopped his manly brow on his'

at a gulp, and then concluded.
“ Had to unload an’ dig me waggon out. Ask
up at Oassilis if the creek ain’t gone off its course
’bout seven mile back.”
SILAS SNELL.
ONLY FOUR TO MAN THE PUMPS.
Dear, dear! When you come to think of it how
closely related things are ; how one thing b ings
up another. Ideas are like a lot of beads on a
string, aren’t they ?
A letter I have just been reading makes me i e-
member what happened to me one winter about
twenty years ago. The story is too long to tell
here, so 111 merely give you the tail-end of it. I
was supercargo on a bark bound fi om London to
Rio. A tremendous gale, lasting live days,
wrecked us. Forty-eight hours after it ceased
there were four men and no more left on the
vessel. The captain had been killed by a falling
spar, three of the crew washed overboard, and the
rest of the thip s company (save us four) went
away in the long-boat with the first and second
mates. We were taking in water through a leak
at the rate of six in lies an hour. Working with,
all our might the four of us could pump that out
in forty minutes, but we must do it every hour.
It was awful wo k. For two days we kept it up,
without sleep Then we stopped, took lo the
quarter boat and shoved off. The sea was quiet
—no wind. While we lay to within a mile of her
the ship threw up her no s e and went down stern
first. We were picked up the next day by a
Danish b ig.
Now the odd thing is that the letter that re-
minded me of that experience has nothing what-
ever to say about ships. Please help me to And
out the association
The lady who writes the letter says that in
July, 1881, she got a bad fright. Exac ly what it
was she doesn't tell. 1 wish she did. Anyway it
so upset her that she didn’t get over the effects of
it for nine years. After that her appetite fell off;
she lost all real relish for food, and what she did
eat only made trouble instead of nourishing her.
It gave her pain in the pit of the stomach and
(curiously enough) between the shoulders. She
says her eyes and skin presently turned yellow as
a but e.cup. lie. - face and abdomen swelled, and
her feet the same, the latier so much so that she
was obliged to have her shoes made larger.
“ I got little sleep at night,” she says, “ and was
in so much pain I had to be propped up with pil-
lows. For weeks together 1 could not lie down in
bed. I had a dry, hollow cough, and bad night
sweats. Then diarrhoea set in and my bowels'
became ulcerated. I was often in dreadful agony
for forty-eight hours at a time. Then I would
have a chill as though a bucket of cold water were-
poured down my back. I got so low I could no
longer sew, knit, or do any housework or look
after my children. My sister had to come ami
help in the house.
“ Everybody said 1 was in a decline and must
die. What I suffered for eight years tongue,
cannot toll. The doctor could do nothing for me.
lie said my complaint was complicated and bad to
deal with. In 1886 I wen tas an outdoor pa ient
to the Shrewsbury Infl marj, but only got
transient relief. ”
The writer is in good health now, but why did
her case remind me of the shipwreck ? Lei’sseitle
that first. The association is easy and natural.
Just see. The ship tank because we four men
hadn’t the strength to pump out the water as fast
as it came in. twenty men might have g..t her
into port. It is the last straw that breaks the
camel s back ; the last unsupplied need thaimakesr
poverty abject and desperate.
These bodies of ours carry the seeds of disease
with them all the time —chiefly the poisons created
by imperfect digestion, made worse by ca eless
habits. But as long as nothing extraordinary
happens we manage to scrape along in a half-and-
half sort of fashion. Yet we've got in on blood
the stuff that any of a dozen diseases is inade of,
only waiting for something to set it afire. While
the liver, kidneys, and lu.igs and skin keep us
fairly free —that is, don’t let the load get too heavy
—we say, “Oh, yes, I’m tolerably well, thank
you.” Little pains and unpleasant symptoms
bother us now and then, but we don’t fancy they
mean anything.
By-and-bye something happens. A cold, too
hearty a meal, a night of dissi ation, an affliction
through death or loss of property, a fright, as in
Mrs. Bunce’s case, &c. Over we go. The last
straw has crushed us. One loose spark has blown
up the barrel of powder. The crew is too small to
save the ship. The kidneys, liver, skin, a d
stomach strike work, and we must have help right ■
away or perish. All of which means tua expios.on
of latent indigestion and dyspepsia poisons in tao
blood.
There ! isn’t it plain why I thought of the ship 1
Now for the conclusion of the lady’s story. She
says: “In 1889 I first heard of Mother Soigors
Curative Syrup. Half a bottle made me feel
better, and by keeping on taking it 1 was soon
strong and well as ever. (Signed) Mrs, Ann
Bunco, The Park, Worthen, near Shrcwsbu.y,
February 22nd, 1893. ”
If there were only a way to save sinking ships
as certain and trustworthy as Mother Seigel’s
medicine is in the case of sinking human bodies,
what a blessing it would be to poor sailors.*
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
